Borrowing Consumables is Qard
Ruling with reasoning on borrowing consumables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ariya (non-consumable loan) and qard (consumable loan) and apply the Hanafi ruling that borrowing consumable items is treated as qard.

Core Idea
Borrowing a consumable item is legally a loan, not a true ariya.

In Hanafi fiqh, the distinction between ariya and qard hinges on whether the item can be used without being consumed. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that borrowing items like food or fuel is legally a loan, making the borrower liable to return its like or value. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper classification of contracts and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on borrowing items that are consumed by use. The ruling is that such borrowing is considered qard (loan), not ariya.
The hukm is that the borrower must return the like or value.
2
Understand the Key Distinction
The central criterion is whether the item can be used while the substance remains. If not, it is qard.
Example: borrowing a piece of bread (tharid) – it is consumed, so qard.
3
Note the Exception for Mutual Generosity
If there is a prior relationship of familiarity (bisasata), the borrowing may be treated as gratuitous permission (ibaha) rather than qard.
This exception relaxes the rule in cases of mutual trust.
4
Apply to Examples
Work through the examples given: borrowing a patch for a shirt or a stick for building (qard unless the borrower says 'I will not return it'), and borrowing a dirham for weighing (ariya because not consumed).
The difference hinges on the possibility of returning the same item.
5
Compare with Borrowing Animals
Borrowing an animal for use (e.g., riding) is ariya, not qard, because the animal is not consumed. An invalid loan of an animal (for consumption) would be different.
This reinforces the rule: use without consumption = ariya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتجاوز عما سماه وإن أطلق له أن ينتفع أي نوع في أي وقت شاء وعارية الثمنين والمكيل والموزون والمعدود قرض. ومراده أن إعارة ما لا يمكن الانتفاع به مع بقاء العين قرض ولو كان قيمياً حتى لو قال أعرتك هذه القطعة من الثريد فأخذها وأكلها فعليه مثله أو قيمته وكان قرضاً إلا إذا كان بينهما مباسطة فيكون ذلك دلالة الإباحة كذا في الخلاصة وفي المحيط لو استعار رقعة ليجعلها على قميصه أو خشبة يدخلها في بنائه فهو ضامن لأنه قرض هذا إذا لم يقل لا ردها عليك فإن قال فهو عارية لأن القرض لا يكون عينه واجب الرد فصار إعارة قيدنا بكونه لا يمكن الانتفاع به مع بقاء عينه لأنه لو أمكن بأن استعار در هماً ليعاير به میزانه كان عارية فليس الانتفاع بعينه كعارية الحلي وإذا كان عارية ما ذكرنا قرضاً كان قرض الحيوان للاستعمال عارية لا قرضاً فساداً لأن القرض الفاسد أن يأخذ الحيوان ليستهلكه وينتفع به ثم يرد عليه مثله وهذا فاسد وهو مضمون بالقيمة كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان.
He disregards what he named, and if he gave absolute permission, he may benefit in any manner at any time. And the borrowing of items measured by weight, capacity, and count is a loan. His meaning: that lending what cannot be benefited from while the substance remains is a loan, even if it is a fungible item. So if he said, 'I lend you this piece of tharid,' and he took it and ate it, then upon him is its like or its value, and it is a loan, unless there is familiarity between them, then that indicates permission. As in al-Khulasa. And in al-Muhit: if one borrows a patch to put on his shirt or a stick to insert into his building, then he is guarantor because it is a loan, provided he does not say, 'I will not return it to you.' If he says that, then it is ariya because a loan does not require the return of the same item. So it becomes ariya. We restricted it to cases where benefit is not possible while the substance remains, because if benefit is possible, such as borrowing a dirham to weigh his scale, it is ariya. So benefit by the substance itself is like borrowing jewelry. And if ariya of what we mentioned is a loan, then borrowing an animal for use is ariya, not a loan, because an invalid loan is taking an animal to consume and benefit, then return its like. That is invalid and it is guaranteed by value. As in Fatawa Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with reasoning. The author clarifies that borrowing (ariyah) of items that are consumed by use—such as foodstuffs measured by weight, capacity, or count—is legally considered a loan (qard), not a true ariya. The key distinction is that in a true ariya, the item must be returnable in substance. When the benefit necessarily consumes the thing, the contract is a loan, and the borrower must return either the like (if it is a standard fungible) or the value (if it is a non-standard fungible). The exception is when there is a prior relationship of mutual generosity, which may indicate gratuitous permission. The passage also discusses cases like borrowing a patch for a shirt or a stick for building: if no explicit statement of non-return is made, it is a loan making the borrower liable. However, if the borrower says 'I will not return it,' then it remains a true ariya because the owner knows the item will not be returned. Borrowing a dirham for weighing is a valid ariya because the dirham is used but not consumed. Borrowing an animal for use is ariya, not a loan, because the animal is used without being consumed; an invalid loan of an animal would be for consumption and return of a like animal, which is invalid and requires compensation by value.

Key Terms
عارية (āriyah): A contract whereby the owner permits another to benefit from a non-consumable item with the obligation to return the item itself.
قرض (qarḍ): A loan of a consumable item where the borrower must return its like or value.
مستعير (mustaʿīr): The borrower in an ariya contract.
إباحة (ibāḥah): Gratuitous permission to use an item without any obligation of return or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, borrowing a dirham to weigh on a scale is:
A) Qard, because the dirham is valuable.
B) Ariya, because the dirham is not consumed in use.
C) Invalid, because scales are not permitted.
D) Sale, because the dirham is used.

Key Takeaway
Borrowing consumable items is treated as a loan (qard) in Hanafi fiqh, requiring return of the like or value, whereas borrowing non-consumable items remains a true ariya (non-consumable loan).

Borrowing Land for Building or Planting
Ruling on borrowing land for construction or planting, including revocation and compensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ariya of land for building, identify when compensation is due, and apply the rule that ariya is revocable.

Core Idea
Ariya is revocable, but if a time is set, the lender compensates the deficiency caused by premature removal.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the Hanafi position on borrowing land for construction or planting. It establishes that such a contract is valid because the benefit is known, yet it remains revocable. However, when a time period is agreed upon, the lender becomes liable for the decrease in value if he revokes early. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of ariya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The passage begins by stating that lending land for building or planting is valid (sah) because the benefit is known. Identify the act (ariya of land for building/planting) and its basic ruling (valid).
The benefit being known is a condition for validity.
2
Determine the revocability and its implications
The lender has the right to revoke because ariya is non-binding. Understand that ariya is generally revocable.
Revocability is a key feature of ariya in the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the compensation scenarios
When no time is set, no liability. When time is set and revocation occurs before expiry, lender compensates the deficiency. Distinguish between timed and untimed ariya.
The timer creates an expectation, making the borrower 'deluded'.
4
Examine the method of calculating compensation
Compensation is the deficiency caused by removal, evaluated at the time of retrieval. Apply the valuation rule: value as standing (if could remain until period end) minus value after removal.
Valuation date is day of retrieval, per Fatawa Qadikhan.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction
The note about choice between removal and full value vs. deficiency only indicates an intra-Hanafi difference. The mufta bihi is the deficiency only.
The author follows the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أعار أرضاً للبناء أو الغراس صح لأن المنفعة معلومة. وله أن يرجع لأنها غير لازمة. ويكلف قلعهما أي قلع البناء والغرس ويجبر المستعير على القلع إلا إذا كان فيه مضرة بالأرض فإن كان يترك بقيمته مقلوعاً. ولا يضمن إن لم يوقت أي لا ضمان على المعير إذا رجع إن لم يوقت لها وقتاً لأنها غير لازمة ولم يغره. وإن وقت فرجع قبله ضمن ما نقص بالقلع بأن يقوم قائماً غير مقلوع يعني بكم يشتري بشرط قيامه إلى المدة المضروبة. وتعتبر القيمة يوم الاسترداد كما في فتاوى قاضيخان لأنه صار مغروراً من جهته. فإن قلت إذا لم يضر بالأرض وإن كان القلع يضر بالأرض لا يقلع إلا برضا صاحبها ويضمن له قيمته مقلوعاً. وظاهره مع ما قبله أن القلع إذا لم يضر بالأرض كان الخيار للمستعير بين قلعه وبين تضمين جميع القيمة وهو مخالف لما في الكتاب حيث جعل له تضمين ما نقصه القلع لا تضمين جميع القيمة.
And if he lends land for building or planting, it is valid because the benefit is known. And he has the right to revoke because it is not binding. And he forces him to remove them, i.e., removal of the building and planting, and the borrower is compelled to remove unless there is harm to the land, then it is left with its value as removed. And he is not liable if he did not set a time, i.e., no liability on the lender when he revokes if he did not set a time because it is not binding and he did not delude him. And if he set a time and revoked before it, he is liable for the deficiency caused by removal, by evaluating it standing, not removed, meaning how much it would be worth if it were to remain until the stipulated period. And the value is considered on the day of retrieval, as in Fatawa Qadikhan, because he has been deluded by him. If it is said: when there is no harm to the land, and if removal harms the land, it is not removed except with the owner's consent and he guarantees its value as removed. The apparent meaning with what preceded is that if removal does not harm the land, the borrower has the choice between removing and guaranteeing the entire value, contrary to what is in the book which assigns him only the deficiency from removal, not the entire value.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proofs and discussion. The author explains the ruling on borrowing land for building or planting. The contract is valid because the benefit is known (the land is used for construction or cultivation). The lender may revoke the ariya at any time because it is not a binding contract. However, the lender can force the borrower to remove the building or plants. This removal is compulsory unless it would cause harm to the land, in which case the borrower leaves the structure and the lender pays its value as if it were removed (i.e., the depreciated value). If no time was set for the ariya, the lender incurs no liability when revoking because the borrower was not misled. But if a specific time was agreed upon and the lender revokes before expiry, the lender must compensate for the decrease in value caused by premature removal. The compensation is calculated as the difference between the value of the standing structure if it could remain until the agreed period and its value if removed. The valuation is made at the time of retrieval. The passage then notes an apparent contradiction: some sources indicate that if the removal does not harm the land, the borrower may choose between removal or claiming the entire value, but the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq (the matn) holds that only the deficiency from removal is compensated.

Key Terms
قلع (qalʿ): Removal of a structure or plant from borrowed land.
مغرور (maghrūr): One who is deluded or misled, here the borrower who relied on the set time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a lender revokes an ariya of land for building before the agreed time, what is the borrower entitled to?
A) The full value of the building.
B) Nothing, because ariya is revocable.
C) The deficiency in value caused by premature removal.
D) The cost of construction.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ariya of land for building is valid and revocable, but the lender must compensate the borrower for the decrease in value caused by premature removal if a time was set.

Joint Liability in Ariya: Two Riders on a Borrowed Animal
Ruling on joint liability in borrowed animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when two persons jointly cause harm to a borrowed animal, and explain the basis of equal division.

Core Idea
When two riders jointly cause the death of a borrowed animal, each is liable for half its value.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʼiq explains a specific scenario of joint liability in the contract of ʻāriyah (loan for use). When two borrowers ride the same animal and both contribute to its death, the liability is shared equally. The underlying principle is that causation determines liability, and when causation is shared, liability is divided proportionally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
Two persons ride a borrowed animal together. The animal dies as a result of their combined use.
2
Determine the outcome
The animal suffers fatal injury or death. The cause is identified as their joint riding.
3
Extract the ruling
The text states: 'He is liable for half its value.'  Since both rode together, each is liable for half.
The ruling assumes both riders are equally causative.
4
Analyze the talīl (reasoning)
The reason given: 'because the cause of its injury was their riding together.'  The shared act creates shared liability.
This is a simple causal link without qiyās.
5
Note the unclear phrase
The text includes 'and one of them by removal' which is corrupted. It may refer to an additional use or to a more detailed scenario, but does not affect the core ruling.
OCR high risk; the phrase is unclear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضمن نصف قيمتها معناه أنها ركباها معاً لأن سبب العطب ركوبهما معاً وأحدهما بالقلع
He is liable for half its value, meaning that they both rode it together, because the cause of its injury was their riding together, and one of them by removal?
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This is a brief ruling statement explaining a case of liability in ariya. The text states that when two persons ride a borrowed animal together and the animal dies as a result, each is liable for half the value. The reasoning is that the cause of the animal's death was the combined riding of both individuals. The phrase 'and one of them by removal' is unclear; it might refer to a scenario where one rider uses the animal to remove something, but the OCR is corrupted. The ruling applies when both riders are equally responsible for the harm. If only one rider were using the animal, he would be fully liable. This follows the Hanafi principle that liability in ariya is based on misuse or negligence.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is each rider liable for half the value?
A) Because the first rider mounted first
B) Because the cause of death was their riding together
C) Because the animal was borrowed
D) Because the lender did not consent

Key Takeaway
When two borrowers jointly cause the death of a borrowed animal, liability is shared equally because causation is shared.

Borrower's Right to Remain Until Harvest
Ruling on borrower's right to remain until harvest with analogical reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a lender revokes borrowing of land for cultivation before harvest, and apply the qiyas to lease (ijarah) to justify the borrower's right to remain with equivalent rent.

Core Idea
A borrower who sows crops cannot be expelled until harvest, protected by analogy to lease.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical question in the law of 'ariyah (gratuitous loan): if land is borrowed for cultivation and the lender revokes before harvest, does the borrower lose everything? The Hanafi answer balances the property rights of the lender with the investment of the borrower, using qiyas to the rules of lease (ijarah) when a crop is still growing. Understanding this ruling illuminates how Hanafi fiqh protects productive labor and prevents waste.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the case is about borrowing land (ariyah) for cultivation, where the lender revokes before the crop is harvested.
The borrower has already sowed and the crop is growing.
2
Extract the ruling
The borrower cannot be expelled until the harvest is complete. If the lender revokes, the crop remains with an equivalent rent (ujr al-mithl) until ripening.
This applies whether or not a time was set for the borrowing.
3
Locate the qiyas
The ruling is analogized to a lease (ijarah) that ends before the crop ripens. In that case, the lessee may leave the crop with equivalent rent.
The effective cause (illa) is protecting both parties' interests and preventing waste.
4
Apply the principle to other cases
The same logic applies when a borrower builds a wall or house on borrowed land: the building belongs to the borrower but the landowner gets equivalent rent for the use.
The passage in Al-Muhit treats borrowing for building as an implied invalid lease (ijarah fasidah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استعارها ليزرعها لا تؤخذ منه حتى يحصد الزرع وقت أو لم يوقت لأن له نهاية معلومة فيترك بأجر المثل إلى وقت الإدراك إذا رجع لأن فيه مراعاة الحقين كما في الإجارة إذا انقضت المدة والزرع لم يدرك بعد فإنه يترك بأجر المثل مراعاة للجانبين. فإن قال رب الأرض أعطيك البذر ونفقتك وأخرجك ويكون ما زرعت لي ورضي به المزارع، فإن كان لم يطلع من الزرع شيء لا يجوز لأن المزارع يصير بائعاً الزرع وبيع الزرع قبل النبات لا يجوز وبعدما خرج فيه كلام. وأشار المؤلف في المغني إلى الجواز. ولو بنى حائطاً في الدار المستعارة استرد المعير الدار، فإذا أرد المستعير أن يرجع عليه بما أنفق ليس له ذلك، وليس له أن يهدم الحائط إن كان البناء من تراب صاحب الأرض. وفي المحيط : لو استعار أرضاً ليبني ويسكن وإذا خرج فالبناء لرب الأرض فلرب الأرض أجر مثلها مقدار السكنى والبناء للمستعير لأن هذه إجارة معنى لأن الإعارة تمليك المنافع بغير عوض، ولما شرط البناء له كانت إجارة فاسدة لجهالة المدة والأجرة لأن البناء مجهول فوجب أجر المثل.
And if he borrows it for cultivation, it is not taken from him until he harvests the crop, whether he set a time or not, because it has a known end; so it is left with an equivalent rent until the time of ripening if he revokes, because it is taking into account both rights, as in lease when the term ends and the crop has not yet ripened, then it is left with an equivalent rent out of consideration for both sides. If the landowner says, 'I will give you the seed and your expenses and expel you, and what you planted is mine,' and the cultivator agrees; if nothing has appeared from the crop, it is not valid because the cultivator becomes a seller of the crop, and selling a crop before it appears is not valid. After it appears, there is discussion. The author in al-Mughni indicated permissibility. And if one builds a wall in the borrowed house, the lender recovers the house; so if the borrower wants to claim back what he spent, he has no right, and he may not demolish the wall if the building material is from the landowner's soil. In al-Muhit: if one borrows land to build and live, and when he leaves, the building belongs to the landowner, then the landowner owes an equivalent rent for the duration of residence, and the building belongs to the borrower because this is an implied lease; since the lending is a free transfer of benefits, but when he stipulated the building for himself, it became an invalid lease due to the ignorance of the term and rent, because the building is unknown, so an equivalent rent is due.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage covers multiple rulings related to borrowing land for cultivation and construction. First, if someone borrows land to sow crops, the borrower cannot be removed until the harvest is complete, whether or not a specific time was set. This is because the crop has a known end (maturity). If the lender revokes, the crop remains with an equivalent rent until harvest, balancing the rights of both parties, analogous to a lease where the term expires but the crop is not yet ripe. This follows the principle from contract law. Second, if the landowner offers to take over the crop by reimbursing the seed and expenses, it is generally not valid if the crop has not yet sprouted because it would be a sale of a non-existent thing. If the crop has emerged, there is some leniency. Third, if a borrower builds a wall in a borrowed house, the lender can recover the house, but the borrower cannot claim compensation or demolish the wall if the material is from the landowner. Fourth, if one borrows land to build and live, and upon leaving the building stays, the building is considered owned by the borrower, but the landowner gets an equivalent rent for the use of the land. This is treated as an invalid lease (because the ariya condition made it a lease), hence an equivalent rent is due.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujr al-mithl): Equivalent rent; the market rate for the use of the land.
إجارة فاسدة (ijārah fāsidah): An invalid lease contract due to ignorance of term or rent.
عارية (ʿāriyah): Gratuitous loan of a chattel or land for use.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a lender revokes a borrowing of land for cultivation before the crop is ready, what happens according to the Hanafi school?
A) The borrower must remove the crop immediately.
B) The borrower may leave the crop until harvest by paying an equivalent rent.
C) The lender must pay for the crop.
D) The borrower loses all rights.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school protects the borrower's investment by allowing retention of the crop with payment of equivalent rent, using qiyas to lease to ensure fairness.

Who Bears the Cost of Returning a Borrowed Item? The General Rule and the Pledge Exception
Ruling on the cost of returning a borrowed item with an exception for pledge borrowing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule on mu'nat al-radd for ariyah and identify the exception for ariyah li al-rahn, explaining the reasoning behind the shift.

Core Idea
The cost of returning a borrowed item is on the borrower, except when the item is borrowed for pledging—then the lender pays.

This passage establishes the basic rule that the borrower (musta'ir) bears the expense of returning a lent item because the benefit was his. However, if the borrower takes the item only to pledge it as collateral (ariyah li al-rahn), the lender also benefits, so the return cost shifts to him. This exception, reported by Qadikhan, is justified by analogy to lease where the owner benefits from rent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The act is returning the borrowed item, and the ruling is that its cost is wajib on the borrower (musta'ir).
2
Recognize the underlying principle
The party who benefits from possession (manfa'ah) bears the return cost. Since the borrower took it for his own benefit, he pays.
3
Spot the exception
When the borrowing is solely for the purpose of pledging (ariyah li al-rahn), the lender (mu'ir) bears the cost, as per Qadikhan's fatwa.
4
Understand the reasoning (talil)
The lender also benefits because the item becomes guaranteed in the hands of the pledgee and he can claim its value from the borrower, making it analogous to a lease (ijarah).
5
Note the two differences between ariyah for pledge and ordinary ariyah
First, the borrower for pledge, if he violates and then returns to compliance, is absolved from liability. Second, the return cost itself is on the lender in this special case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومؤنة الرد على المستعير لأن الرد واجب عليه لما أنه قبضه لمنفعة نفسه والأجرة مؤنة الرد فتكون عليه. وفائدة كونها على المستعير تظهر أيضاً فيما لو كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضى الوقت فأمسكها المستعير فهلكت ضمنها لأن مؤنة الرد عليه. ويستثنى من إطلاقهم ما ذكره قاضيخان في فتاواه في فصل من يرهن مال الغير : رجل أعار شيئاً له حمل ومؤنة ليرهنه فرهنه قالوا إن رد العارية يكون على المعير، وفرق بينها وبين غيرها من العواري في غير هذا يكون الرد على المستعير لأن هذه إعارة فيها منفعة لصاحبها فإنها تصير مضمونة في يد المرتهن وللمعير أن يرجع على المستعير بقيمته فكانت بمنزلة الإجارة. فقد حصل الفرق بين العارية للرهن وغيرها من وجهين : الأول ما ذكرناه أن المستعير للرهن لو خالف ثم عاد إلى الوفاق برئ عن الضمان بخلاف غيره. الثاني ما ذكرناه في هنا ويدخل في المستعير الموصى له بالخدمة فإن مؤنة رد العبد عليه كما في المستعير. قوله : (والمودع) أي مؤنة الرد على مالك الوديعة لأن منفعة القبض حاصلة له لأنه يحفظ العين ومنفعته عائدة إليه. قوله : ( والمؤجر ) أي مؤنة الرد على المؤجر لا المستأجر لأنها مقبوضة لمنفعة المالك لأن الأجر سلم له به فإذا أمسكها المستأجر بعد مضي المدة لا يضمنها ما لم يطالبه صاحبها بالرد. وفي الفصل السادس من إجارة الفتاوى البزازية قال صاحب المحيط مأذون فيه فلهذا ضمن النصف حتى لو أركب غيره فقط ضمن الكل هذا ما ظهر لي ولم أر من نبه عليه أبو السعود يعني أركب غيره بعد ما ركب هو لأن له أن يعير ما اختلف استعماله إن لم يعين منتفعاً. قوله : (الأول ما ذكرناه) أي في قوله في كتاب الوديعة بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر.
And the cost of return is on the borrower because return is obligatory upon him since he took it for his own benefit, and the return cost is on him. The benefit of it being on the borrower also appears in the case where the borrowing is temporary and the time passed and he held it, then it perished; he is liable because the cost of return was on him. And an exception to their absolute statement is what Qadikhan mentioned in his Fatawa in the chapter on one who pledges another's property: a man lent an item that had transport cost and expense to be pledged, and he pledged it; they said the return of the ariya is on the lender. And there is a difference between it and other borrowings: in other cases the return is on the borrower because this lending has benefit for the owner since it becomes guaranteed in the hand of the pledgee, and the lender can claim its value from the borrower, so it is like a lease. So the difference between ariya for pledge and other ariya is established in two ways: first, that the borrower for pledge, if he violates and then returns to compliance, is absolved from liability unlike others. Second, what we mentioned here. And included in the borrower is the one bequeathed service, for the cost of returning the slave is on him as in the borrower. His statement: (and the depositor) meaning the cost of return is on the owner of the deposit because the benefit of possession is for him since he preserves the item and its benefit returns to him. His statement: (and the lessor) meaning the cost of return is on the lessor, not the lessee, because it is possessed for the benefit of the owner since the rent is paid for it. So if the lessee holds it after the term ends, he is not liable until the owner demands its return. And in the sixth chapter of lease from al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya, the author of al-Muhit said: authorized in it, so for this he is liable for half; even if he lets another ride only, he is liable for the entire value. This is what appeared to me, and I have not seen anyone who pointed it out except Abu al-Su'ud, meaning he lets another ride after he himself rode, because he may lend what usage differs if he did not specify a user. His statement: (the first we mentioned) i.e., in his statement in the book of deposit, contrary to the borrower and lessee.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with analysis on who bears the cost of returning a borrowed item. The general rule is that the borrower (musta'ir) bears the return cost because the borrowing was for his benefit, and return is his obligation. Even if the borrowing was for a fixed term and the borrower kept the item after expiry, he becomes liable for its loss because return was due. The exception is when the borrowing is for the purpose of pledging (ariyah li al-rahn). In that case, the return cost falls on the lender because the lender also benefits: the item becomes guaranteed in the hands of the pledgee, and the lender can claim its value from the borrower if lost. This is akin to a lease where the owner benefits from rent. The passage also clarifies that the depositor (mudi') bears the return cost for a deposit (wadi'ah) because he benefits from the safekeeping. For lease (ijarah), the lessor (mu'jir) bears the return cost, not the lessee, because the possession is for the owner's benefit. If the lessee keeps the item after the term, he does not become liable until the owner demands return. The passage ends with a discussion from al-Muhit about liability when a borrower lets others ride a borrowed animal: if authorized, he is liable only half? Actually, the text is messy: it says 'liable for half' and 'if he lets another ride only, he is liable for the entire value'. It seems the author is noting a nuance from Abu al-Su'ud.

Key Terms
مؤنة الرد (mu'nat al-radd): The expense or cost of returning a borrowed item to its owner.
عارية للرهن (āriyah li al-rahn): Borrowing an item specifically to pledge it as collateral for a loan.
مستعير (musta'ir): Borrower
معير (mu'ir): Lender

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a standard borrowing (ariyah), who bears the cost of returning the item?
A) The lender (mu'ir).
B) The borrower (musta'ir).
C) The item itself.
D) Both equally.

Key Takeaway
The return cost follows the benefit: borrower pays unless the lender also benefits, as in ariyah li al-rahn.

Return Costs in Deposit and Lease, and Liability for Unauthorized Use
Rulings on return costs in deposit and lease, and liability for unauthorized use

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the return cost for deposit (wadi'ah) and lease (ijarah), and explain the liability when a borrower or lessee allows unauthorized use.

Core Idea
The return cost is on the depositor and lessor, and a borrower who exceeds authorization may be liable for loss.

This part of the passage extends the analysis of mu'nat al-radd to other contracts: deposit (wadi'ah) and lease (ijarah). In deposit, the depositor bears the return cost because he benefits from safekeeping; in lease, the lessor bears it because the rent is his benefit. The passage also discusses a nuance: if a borrower or lessee allows another person to use the item without permission, he becomes liable for half or full value depending on authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for deposit (wadi'ah)
The cost of returning a deposit is on the owner (depositor), not the depositee, because the benefit of safekeeping goes to the owner.
2
Identify the ruling for lease (ijarah)
The cost of returning a leased item is on the lessor (owner), not the lessee, because the lessor benefits from the rent. The lessee is not liable for loss after the term until the owner demands return.
3
Understand the riding liability scenario
If a borrower of a rideable animal lets another person ride it without permission, liability depends on authorization: if authorized in general, he may be liable for half (if he also rode himself) or full (if he only let the other ride).
This is from al-Muhit and al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya.
4
Apply the principle of benefit
The common thread: the party who derives the primary benefit bears the return cost. For unauthorized use, liability arises from exceeding the permission.

Who Bears the Cost of Returning a Borrowed Item?
Lists who bears return cost for loans, deposits, leases, usury, and pledges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for who bears mu'nat al-radd and identify the exception for loans made for pledging.

Core Idea
The borrower bears the cost of return because the benefit is his; exception: loan for pledge shifts cost to lender.

In Hanafi fiqh, when you borrow something, you must return it. But who pays for the transport or effort? The general rule is that the borrower bears this cost because he took the item for his own benefit. However, there is a notable exception: if the item is loaned specifically so the borrower can pledge it as collateral, the lender benefits too, so the cost falls on the lender. This lesson unpacks the rule and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the default position: the borrower (musta'ir) bears the cost of returning the borrowed item.
This is because the borrower took possession for his own benefit.
2
Recognize the exception
Note the exception for a loan intended for pledging (āriyah li al-rahn): here the lender bears the cost.
The lender benefits because the item becomes secure; thus it resembles a lease.
3
Apply to other possessors
Extend the reasoning to other parties: depositor (owner) bears cost for wadi'ah, lessor for ijarah, usurper always pays, pledgee pays for rahn.
In each case, the cost falls on the one who benefits from the possession.
4
Handle the special case of the common hired worker
For an ajir mushtarak (e.g., tailor), the cost of returning the garment is on the worker, not the owner.
This is because the worker benefits from the wage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومؤنة الرد على المستعير لأن الرد واجب عليه لما أنه قبضه لمنفعة نفسه والأجرة مؤنة الرد فتكون عليه وفائدة كونها على المستعير تظهر أيضاً فيما لو كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضى الوقت فأمسكها المستعير فهلكت ضمنها لأن مؤنة الرد عليه ؛ كذا في النهاية . ويستثنى من إطلاقهم ما ذكره قاضيخان في فتاواه في فصل من يرهن مال الغير : رجل أعار شيئاً له حمل ومؤنة ليرهنه فرهنه قالوا إن رد العارية يكون على المعير، وفرق بينها وبين غيرها من العواري في غير هذا يكون الرد على المستعير لأن هذه إعارة فيها منفعة لصاحبها فإنها تصير مضمونة في يد المرتهن وللمعير أن يرجع على المستعير بقيمته فكانت بمنزلة الإجارة اهـ. فقد حصل الفرق بين العارية للرهن وغيرها من وجهين : الأول ما ذكرناه أن المستعير للرهن لو خالف ثم عاد إلى الوفاق برئ عن الضمان بخلاف غيره. الثاني ما ذكرناه هنا ويدخل في المستعير الموصى له بالخدمة فإن مؤنة رد العبد عليه كما في المستعير ؛ كذا النهاية قوله : (والمودع) أي مؤنة الرد على مالك الوديعة لأن منفعة القبض حاصلة له لأنه يحفظ العين ومنفعته عائدة إليه قوله : ( والمؤجر ) أي مؤنة الرد على المؤجر لا المستأجر لأنها مقبوضة لمنفعة المالك لأن الأجر سلم له به فإذا أمسكها المستأجر بعد مضي المدة لا يضمنها ما لم يطالبه صاحبها بالرد. وفي الفصل السادس من إجارة الفتاوى البزازية قال صاحب المحيط مأذون فيه فلهذا ضمن النصف حتى لو أركب غيره فقط ضمن الكل هذا ما ظهر لي ولم أر من نبه عليه أبو السعود يعني أركب غيره بعد ما ركب هو لأن له أن يعير ما اختلف استعماله إن لم يعين منتفعاً. قوله : (الأول ما ذكرناه) أي في قوله في كتاب الوديعة بخلاف المستعير والمستأجر. قال مشايخنا : هذا إذا كان الإخراج بإذن صاحب المال ولو بلا إذنه فمؤنة الرد مستأجراً أو مستعيراً على الذي أخرج .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : الأجير المشترك كالخياط ونحوه مؤنة الرد عليه لا على رب الثوب قوله : (والغاصب) أي مؤنة الرد على الغاصب لأن الواجب عليه الرد والإعادة إلى يد المالك دفعاً للضرر عنه قوله : ( والمرتهن) أي مؤنة الرد على المرتهن لا الراهن لأن الغنم حصل له ولهذا اختص به من بين سائر الغرماء حتى يستوفي دينه منه أولاً فكان الغرم عليه. قال في الخلاصة : إن مؤنة الرد على الراهن وفيه كلام لا يخفى، وقد قدمنا حكم نفقة العين المستأجرة وكسوتها.
His statement: (And the expense of return is on the borrower) because returning is obligatory upon him, since he took possession for his own benefit, and the expense of return is upon him. The benefit of it being on the borrower also appears in the case where the loan was for a fixed time and the time passed, so the borrower held it and it perished—he is liable because the expense of return was upon him; similarly in al-Nihaya. And an exception to their general statement is what Qadikhan mentioned in his Fatawa in the chapter on pledging the property of others: A man lent something that has transport and expense to be pledged, and he pledged it. They said: the expense of returning the loan is on the lender. And there is a difference between it and other loans—in other cases the expense is on the borrower, because this loan has benefit for its owner, since it becomes secured in the hand of the pledgee, and the lender can claim its value from the borrower, so it is like a lease. So there is a difference between the loan for pledge and other loans in two ways: first, what we mentioned: if the borrower for pledge violates and then returns to compliance, he is freed from liability, unlike others. Second, what we mentioned here. And included in the borrower is the legatee of service (musa bihi al-khidma), for the expense of returning the slave is upon him like the borrower; as in al-Nihaya. His statement: (And the depositor) means the expense of return is on the owner of the deposit, because the benefit of possession is for him—he preserves the item and its benefit returns to him. His statement: (And the lessor) means the expense of return is on the lessor, not the lessee, because it was taken into possession for the owner's benefit, since the rent is received by him. So if the lessee holds it after the term, he is not liable unless the owner demands return. In the sixth chapter of Ijarah from al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyya, the author of al-Muhit said: it is permitted, so he is liable for half; if he only had someone else ride, he is liable for the whole. This is what appeared to me, and I have not seen anyone point it out—Abu al-Saud means: if he had someone else ride after he himself rode, because he may lend what varies in use if he did not specify a user. His statement: (the first what we mentioned) refers to his statement in the book of Deposit: unlike the borrower and lessee. Our scholars said: this is if the removal was with the permission of the owner; if without his permission, then the expense of return, whether lessee or borrower, is on the one who removed. End. In al-Khulasa: the common hired worker (ajir mushtarak) like a tailor and the like—the expense of return is on him, not on the owner of the garment. His statement: (And the usurper) means the expense of return is on the usurper because he is obligated to return and restore to the owner's hand to remove harm from him. His statement: (And the pledgee) means the expense of return is on the pledgee, not the pledgor, because the benefit goes to him—for this reason he is distinguished from other creditors in that he may first collect his debt from it, so the loss is upon him. In al-Khulasa: the expense of return is on the pledgor, and there is discussion in it that is not hidden, and we have already presented the ruling on the maintenance of the leased item and its clothing.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage details the ruling on who bears the cost (mu'nat al-radd) of returning a borrowed item to its owner. The general rule is that the borrower bears the cost because he took possession for his own benefit. However, an exception is made for a loan intended for pledging (al-āriyah li al-rahn): if the item is given for the borrower to pledge, the lender (mu'īr) bears the return cost because the lender benefits (the item becomes secure). The text also specifies that the depositor (mūdi') bears the cost of returning a deposit (wadi'ah) because safekeeping benefits him. The lessor (mu'jir) bears the cost for leased property because he receives rent, so possession is for his benefit. The usurper (ghāṣib) bears the cost as he must undo his wrong. The pledgee (murtahin) bears the cost because he benefits from holding the pledge. There is a note that for a common hired worker (ajir mushtarak) like a tailor, the return cost is on him, not the owner. The passage also references the first point (about loan for pledge) from the book of Deposit.

Key Terms
مؤنة الرد (mu'nat al-radd): The cost or expense incurred in returning a borrowed item to its owner.
عارية للرهن (āriyah li al-rahn): A loan of an item for the specific purpose of pledging it as collateral.
المستعير (al-musta'ir): The borrower.
المودع (al-mūdi'): The depositor (owner of the deposit).
المؤجر (al-mu'jir): The lessor.
الغاصب (al-ghāṣib): The usurper.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The pledgee (creditor holding collateral).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For a standard, temporary loan of a car, who bears the cost of returning it to the owner?
A) The lender (owner)
B) The borrower
C) Both equally
D) The government

Key Takeaway
The controlling principle is benefit: the one who benefits from possession bears the cost of return; the exception for loan-for-pledge confirms this because the lender also benefits.

6 min
Discharge of Liability by Customary Return of Borrowed Items
Ruling with istihsan-based reasoning and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts return of a borrowed animal to the owner's stable as valid discharge by istihsan and custom, and identify conditions where direct return is required.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) and istihsan allow a borrower to discharge liability by leaving the item at the owner's property, but only for customary items.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a practical issue: if you borrow a horse, must you hand it directly to the owner, or can you tie it to his stable? The Hanafi answer, relying on istihsan and custom, is that returning to the owner's stable suffices for items like animals and slaves, but not for precious objects, nor for usurped or deposited goods. Let's unpack the reasoning and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Basis
State the Hanafi ruling: the borrower is discharged if he returns the animal to the owner's stable or the slave to the owner's house. Note that the basis is istihsan (juristic preference) and custom (urf), not strict analogy.
The text explicitly says 'by istihsan' (استحساناً).
2
Understand the Custom (Urf) Argument
Recognize that returning borrowed items to the owner's property is customary, just like returning household tools. This custom makes the return valid even without direct handover.
The analogy to household tools (آلة البيت) shows the scope: everyday items.
3
Distinguish Types of Items
Note the restriction: the ruling applies to animals and slaves, but not to a precious necklace (عقد جوهر). For valuable items, direct return to the lender is required because the custom does not cover them.
This distinction is from al-Hidayah.
4
Specify the Place of Return
The return must be to the stable (إصطبل) or house, not to the owner's land (أرض). The text contrasts with a less precise return.
From al-Muhit: returning to land does not discharge.
5
Contrast with Usurpation and Deposit
For usurped goods (ghasb) and deposits (wadi'ah), the rule is different: direct return to the owner is required because the duty is to undo the wrongful act (for ghasb) or because the depositor's trust demands direct handover (for wadi'ah). Similarly, a lessee (musta'jir) follows the deposit rule.
This contrast is explicitly stated in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن رد المستعير الدابة إلى إصطبل مالكها أو العبد إلى دار المالك برئ) عن استحساناً لأنه أتى بالتسليم المتعارف لأن رد العواري إلى دار الملاك معتاد كآلة البيت. قيد بالدابة والعبد لأنها لو كانت عقد جوهر لا يردها إلا إلى المعير لعدم ما ذكرنا من العرف؛ كذا في الهداية. وقيدنا بالإصطبل لأنه لو ردها إلى أرض مالكها لا يبرأ ؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (بخلاف المغصوب والوديعة حيث لا يبرأ إلا بالرد إلى المالك لأن الواجب على الغاصب نسخ فعله وذلك بالرد إلى المالك دون غيره والوديعة ليس فيها عرف لعدم رضاه بالرد إلى الدار أو من في عياله لأنه لو ارتضاه لما أودعها إياه والمستأجر كالوديعة؛ كذا في المحيط
His statement: (And if the borrower returns the animal to the stable of its owner or the slave to the house of the owner, he is discharged) by istihsan because he performed the customary delivery, since returning borrowed items to the owner's houses is habitual, like household tools. It is restricted to the animal and slave because if it were a precious necklace, he would only return it to the lender due to the absence of the custom we mentioned; as in al-Hidaya. We restricted it to the stable because if he returned it to the owner's land, he is not discharged; as in al-Muhit. His statement: (Unlike the usurped item and the deposit where he is not discharged except by return to the owner) because the duty on the usurper is to undo his action, and that is by returning to the owner, not others; and for the deposit there is no custom, since he would not be pleased with return to the house or his dependents—if he were pleased, he would not have deposited it with him. And the lessee is like the deposit; as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage discusses the discharge of liability when returning a borrowed item. By istihsan (juristic preference), if the borrower returns a borrowed animal to the owner's stable or a slave to the owner's house, he is discharged from liability because custom (urf) accepts this as a valid return. However, this only applies to ordinary items like animals and slaves; for valuable items like a precious necklace, it must be returned directly to the lender. Also, the return must be to the stable, not just to the owner's land. The text contrasts this with cases of usurpation (ghasb) and deposit (wadi'ah): for a usurped item, the duty is to undo the wrongful act, which requires direct return to the owner; for a deposit, there is no custom of returning to the owner's property because that would not satisfy the depositor's trust. Similarly, a lessee is treated like a depositor.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departure from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of custom or necessity.
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice that is accepted as a basis for legal rulings.
عارية (ʻariyah): Borrowing; a contract where possession is given gratuitously for use, with liability for return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is returning a usurped item (ghasb) not discharged by leaving it at the owner's house?
A) Because the owner might not find it.
B) Because the usurper's duty is to undo his wrongful action, which requires direct return to the owner.
C) Because custom does not apply to usurpation.
D) Because the item is always valuable.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) and istihsan allow a borrower to discharge liability by leaving a borrowed item at the owner's property, but only for customary items and at the proper place.

Ruling on Returning a Borrowed Item with the Owner's Servant or Agent
Ruling on discharging liability by returning a borrowed animal with the owner's servant or agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a borrower is discharged from liability by returning a borrowed item with another person, and distinguish between valid and invalid modes of return according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
By custom, returning a borrowed animal with the owner's servant discharges liability; with a stranger, it does not.

In this passage, the author explains how a borrower can be freed from liability when returning a borrowed animal. The key factor is custom (urf): sending the animal with the owner’s slave or employee is sufficient, because such persons are customarily trusted to return items. However, sending with a stranger is not valid, unless the loan term has expired and the borrower has become a transgressor. This lesson unpacks the details and the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? The discharge of the borrower when returning a borrowed item. The ruling is that the borrower is discharged if he returns with certain persons.
The act is the return, not the borrowing itself.
2
Recognize the role of custom
The validity depends on custom (urf). Which persons are considered trustworthy by custom? The owner's slave, the owner's monthly hired worker, and the borrower's own slave.
Custom may vary by time and place, but the text gives specific examples.
3
Distinguish borrower vs depositor
The rule applies only to the borrower (musta'ir), not to a depositor (mudi'). If a depositor returns a deposit with the owner's servant, he is not discharged because custom does not extend to that case.
The text explicitly restricts the ruling to the borrower.
4
Apply the distinction for valuable items
The ruling is about ordinary items like animals. For precious items, returning with a young boy (even of the owner) would not discharge, because custom does not accept that.
This shows that custom is context-dependent.
5
Understand the mufta bihi
The text says 'unlike with a stranger' indicating that the borrower cannot deposit with a stranger. But the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) permits it during the loan term. The restriction applies after the term expires, when the borrower becomes a transgressor.
This reconciles the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن رد المستعير الدابة مع عبده أو أجيره مشاهرة أو مع عبد رب الدابة أو أجيره برئ بخلاف الأجنبي) للعرف . قيد بالمستعير لأن المودع لو رد مع عبد رب الدابة أو أجيره لا يبرأ لعدم العرف، ولو رد مع عبده لا يضمن لأن له أن يستحفظ به وقيد بالدابة لأنه لو كان شيئاً نفيساً فردها إلى يد غلام صاحبها ضمن لعدم العرف به. وأطلق في عبد رب الدابة فشمل عبداً يقوم عليها أو لا وهو الأصح . وفي قوله بخلاف الأجنبي» إشارة إلى أن المستعيرة ليس له الإيداع من الأجنبي، وقد تقدم أن المختار المفتى به جوازه فتعين أن تكون هذه المسألة محمولة على ما إذا كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضت مدتها ثم بعثها مع الأجنبي لأنه بالإمساك بعد المدة يصير متعدياً
His statement: (And if the borrower returns the animal with his slave or his monthly hired worker, or with the owner's slave or his hired worker, he is discharged, unlike with a stranger) due to custom. It is restricted to the borrower because if the depositor returned with the owner's slave or hired worker, he is not discharged for lack of custom. If he returns with his own slave, he is not liable because he may entrust him. It is restricted to the animal because if it were a precious item and he returned it to the hand of the owner's young boy, he would be liable for lack of custom. And it is unrestricted regarding the owner's slave, so it includes a slave who attends to the animal or not—this is the most correct. His statement 'unlike with a stranger' indicates that the borrower does not have the right to deposit with a stranger. It was previously stated that the chosen position (mufta bihi) permits it, so this issue must be understood as when the loan was for a fixed time and the time expired, then he sent it with a stranger, because by holding after the term he becomes a transgressor.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage deals with the discharge of liability when the borrower returns a borrowed animal with someone else. By custom (urf), if the borrower sends the animal with his own slave or monthly hired worker, or with the owner's slave or hired worker, he is discharged. However, this is only for the borrower; a depositor (mudi') is not discharged by sending with the owner's servant. The borrower can also send with his own slave because he is entitled to entrust him. The ruling applies to common items like animals; for precious items, sending with the owner's young boy would not discharge liability. The text also clarifies that sending with a stranger is not valid, but the relied-upon position (al-mukhtār al-muftā bihi) is that the borrower may deposit with a stranger; the contrary here is explained as applying after the loan term has expired, making the borrower a transgressor.

Key Terms
أجير مشاهرة (ajir mushaharah): A hired worker employed on a monthly basis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A borrows a horse from B. Which of the following is a valid way for A to be discharged from liability by returning the horse?
A) Sending the horse with a stranger.
B) Sending the horse with B's monthly hired worker.
C) Leaving the horse on B's front porch.
D) Sending the horse with A's neighbor.

Key Takeaway
By custom, a borrower is discharged from liability when returning a borrowed animal with the owner's slave or employee, but not with a stranger unless the loan term has expired.

Wording of the Loan Contract for Land: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wording of land loan contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred wording for a land loan contract according to Abu Hanifa and explain the rationale based on specificity of language.

Core Idea
For land borrowed for farming, use 'at'amtanī'—specific wording clarifies the purpose of the loan.

The Hanafi school pays close attention to the wording of contracts to avoid ambiguity. When borrowing land for cultivation, there is a subtle difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding what the borrower should write in the contract. This lesson examines the reasoning behind each view and the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa recommends the borrower write when borrowing land for farming.
The key phrase is 'at'amtanī arḍaka' (you have fed me your land).
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
State the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) that the borrower should write 'a'artanī' (you have lent me). What is their evidence?
They argue that 'i'ārah' is the established term for a loan contract.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Understand why Abu Hanifa prefers 'it'ām' over 'i'ārah'. What is the conceptual advantage?
The word 'it'ām' is specific to agriculture, whereas 'i'ārah' includes other uses like building.
4
Determine the scope of the ruling
Note that this difference applies only to land. For other items like garments or houses, the standard 'a'artanī' is used.
The text explicitly restricts the 'it'ām' wording to agricultural land.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this difference? How does it affect the validity or interpretation of the contract?
The difference is in the preferred wording; both wordings are likely valid, but using 'it'ām' more clearly indicates the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ويكتب المعار أطعمتني أرضك أي إذا استعار أرضاً بيضاء للزراعة يكتب المستعير أنك أطعمتني أرضك لأزرعها ما أشاء من . غلة الشتاء أو الصيف عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يكتب أنك أعرتني لأن لفظ الإعارة موضوعة له وله أن لفظ الإطعام أدل على المراد لأنها تخص الزراعة والإعارة تنتظمها وغيرها كالبناء ونحوه فكانت الكتابة بها أولى. قيد بالأرض لأن في إعارة الثوب والدار يكتب أعرتني ولا يكتب ألبستني ولا أسكنتني .اهـ. والله أعلم.
His statement: (And the borrower writes: 'You have fed me your land') that is, if he borrows empty land for cultivation, the borrower writes: 'You have fed me your land to plant whatever I wish of winter or summer crops' according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: he writes: 'You have lent me' because the word 'loan' (i'ārah) is specifically for it. But it is said that the word 'feeding' (it'ām) is more indicative of the intended meaning because it is specific to agriculture, while 'loan' includes it and other things like building and the like, so writing with it is preferable. It is restricted to land because in lending a garment or a house, he writes 'You have lent me' and does not write 'You have clothed me' or 'You have housed me.' End. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This is an intra-madhhab dispute regarding the wording of a written contract for borrowing land for cultivation. Abu Hanifa holds that the borrower should write 'You have fed me your land' (at'amtanī arḍaka) because the word it'ām (feeding) is specifically used for agriculture and clearly indicates the purpose. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that he should write 'You have lent me' (a'artanī) because the term i'ārah (loan) is the proper word for such contracts. The author (of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) supports Abu Hanifa's view, arguing that it'ām is more specific and thus preferable. He notes that this ruling is specific to land; for other items like clothes or houses, the standard 'a'artanī' is used.

Key Terms
إعارة (i'ārah): The contract of lending or giving something for gratuitous use.
إطعام (it'ām): Literally 'feeding'; used metaphorically to mean giving land for cultivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what should a borrower write in the contract when borrowing land for farming?
A) 'You have lent me your land' (a'artanī arḍaka).
B) 'You have fed me your land' (at'amtanī arḍaka).
C) 'You have given me your land' (a'taytanī arḍaka).
D) 'You have rented me your land' (akratanī arḍaka).

Key Takeaway
When borrowing land for cultivation, the recommended wording is 'at'amtanī arḍaka' because it is specific to agriculture and prevents ambiguity.

Returning a Borrowed Item via the Owner's Servant or Employee
Ruling on discharge via owner's slave/employee based on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on discharging a borrower when the borrowed item is returned via the owner's slave or hired worker, and apply the role of custom ('urf) in determining liability.

Core Idea
Custom ('urf) makes return via owner's slave or hired worker valid; strangers require special conditions.

In Hanafi fiqh, the borrower (musta'ir) is discharged from liability when the borrowed item is returned to the owner in a customary manner. This lesson examines the ruling when the borrower sends the item with the owner's slave (slave) or hired worker (ajir). The key factor is custom ('urf): what people customarily accept as valid delivery. The passage also distinguishes the borrower from the depositor (mudi') and clarifies the special case of a stranger after the borrowing term expires.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is returning a borrowed item (here a riding animal) via a third party. Determine when the borrower is discharged from liability. According to the passage, returning with the owner's slave or hired worker, or with the borrower's own slave or hired worker, discharges the borrower.
The key condition is that the third party is connected to the owner or the borrower in a customary way.
2
Identify the proof: custom ('urf)
The proof cited is custom ('urf). The Hanafi school relies on widespread practice (ta'amul) to validate delivery methods. Custom determines that sending the item with the owner's slave or hired worker is a valid return.
Custom is a secondary source (dalil aqli) when primary texts are silent.
3
Distinguish borrower from depositor
The passage restricts the ruling to the borrower. If a depositor (mudi') returns a deposit with the owner's slave, he is not discharged because no custom supports it. The borrower, however, may use his own slave or hired worker as he is permitted to entrust the item.
This distinction shows that the ruling is not generalizable to all bailments.
4
Note the exception for strangers after term expiry
A stranger (ajnabi) is not allowed except when the borrowing was for a fixed term that has expired. After expiry, the borrower becomes a transgressor (mut'addi) and must return the item by any means, even via a stranger.
This exception clarifies the limit of custom: it does not validate delivery to a stranger during the proper term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن رد المستعير الدابة مع عبده أو أجيره مشاهرة أو مع عبد رب الدابة أو أجيره برئ بخلاف الأجنبي) للعرف . قيد بالمستعير لأن المودع لو رد مع عبد رب الدابة أو أجيره لا يبرأ لعدم العرف، ولو رد مع عبده لا يضمن لأن له أن يستحفظ به وقيد بالدابة لأنه لو كان شيئاً نفيساً فردها إلى يد غلام صاحبها ضمن لعدم العرف به. وأطلق في عبد رب الدابة فشمل عبداً يقوم عليها أو لا وهو الأصح . وفي قوله بخلاف الأجنبي» إشارة إلى أن المستعيرة ليس له الإيداع من الأجنبي، وقد تقدم أن المختار المفتى به جوازه فتعين أن تكون هذه المسألة محمولة على ما إذا كانت العارية مؤقتة فمضت مدتها ثم بعثها مع الأجنبي لأنه بالإمساك بعد المدة يصير متعدياً
His statement: (And if the borrower returns the riding animal with his own slave or his hired worker on a monthly basis, or with the owner's slave or hired worker, he is discharged, unlike with a stranger) due to custom. It is restricted to the borrower because if the depositor (mudi') were to return with the owner's slave or hired worker, he would not be discharged due to the absence of custom; and if he returns with his own slave, he is not liable because he is permitted to entrust it to him for safekeeping. And it is restricted to a riding animal because if it were a valuable item and he returned it to the hand of the owner's servant, he would be liable due to the lack of custom. And it is left unrestricted regarding the owner's slave, so it includes a slave whether he tends to it or not, and this is the correct view. And in his statement 'unlike a stranger' is an indication that the borrower does not have the right to deposit it with a stranger. It has previously been mentioned that the preferred and fatwa-approved view is its permissibility, so it must be that this issue is taken to refer to the case where the borrowing was for a fixed term and the term has expired, and then he sent it with a stranger, because by retaining it after the term he becomes a transgressor.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the borrower's discharge. The author explains that if the borrower returns the borrowed riding animal via the owner's own slave (servant) or a hired worker (ajir), or via his own slave or hired worker, he is discharged because this is customary. However, if he sends it with a stranger, he is not discharged unless the borrowing was temporary and the term has expired, making him a transgressor (mut'addi). The ruling is restricted to the borrower: if a depositor (mudi') tried to return a deposit via the owner's slave, he would not be discharged because no custom supports that. The restriction to a riding animal (dabbah) is because for valuable items, returning via the owner's servant would not be customary. The text also clarifies that the owner's slave includes any slave, regardless of whether he ordinarily works with that animal. This shows the Hanafi reliance on custom (urf) in determining the validity of delivery.

Key Terms
أجير مشاهرة (ajir mushaharah): A hired worker employed on a monthly basis.
متعدي (mut'addi): One who transgresses by exceeding the limits of permissibility, here by retaining the borrowed item beyond its term.
عرف ('urf): Custom; widespread practice that is sound and not contradicting primary texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Custom ('urf) determines valid delivery; borrower is discharged if he returns via owner's slave/hired worker or his own, but strangers require the borrowing term to have expired.

Definition and Rulings of Hibah (Gift)
Definition, conditions, and rulings of hibah in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hibah, identify its essential conditions and pillars, and explain its non-binding nature and the effect of invalid conditions.

Core Idea
Hibah is a gratuitous transfer of ownership, valid but revocable until possession is taken.

Hibah, or gift, is a contract of beneficence that transfers ownership of a physical asset without any compensation. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines hibah precisely, distinguishing it from other contracts like sale, loan, and lease. It also sets out the conditions for its validity, the role of offer and acceptance, and the unique Hanafi position that hibah is not binding—making it revocable before possession and immune to conditions like options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and excluded contracts
Read the definition: 'tamlik al-'ayn bila 'iwad'. Note the key elements: transfer of ownership (tamlik), of a physical asset ('ayn), without compensation. Then list what is excluded: ibahah, aariyah, ijarah, sale, and the debtor's gift of debt (which is isqat).
This definition sets the boundaries of hibah.
2
Understand the two types of gift
The text mentions two types: tamlik and isqat. Recognize that when a debtor forgives a debt, it is isqat (waiver), not tamlik. Also note the special case of gifting a debt by a third party, which requires the debtor to be commanded to take possession.
This distinction is crucial in practice.
3
List the conditions of validity
Identify the conditions for the donor (sanity, maturity, ownership) and the subject (taken possession, not undivided, distinguished, unencumbered). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance.
Possession is a condition of validity for the subject.
4
Examine the ruling on revocability and invalid conditions
Note that hibah is not binding—the donor may revoke before possession. Invalid conditions (e.g., khiyar al-shart) do not invalidate the gift; the condition becomes void but the gift remains valid. Example: gifting a slave on condition of emancipation.
This contrasts with sale, where invalid conditions often void the contract.
5
Appreciate the merits of giving
The text ends with a quote emphasizing the importance of teaching generosity. Reflect on the ethical dimension of hibah beyond legal technicalities.
Fiqh is not just rules but also spiritual cultivation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة هي تمليك العين بلا عوض وتصح بإيجاب وقبول كوهبت ونحلت وأطعمتك هي كتاب الهبة لغة التفضل على الغير بما ينفعه ولو غير مال واصطلاحاً ما أشار إليه المصنف قوله : (هي تمليك العين بلا عوض فخرجت الإباحة والعارية والإجارة والبيع وهبة الدين ممن عليه فإنه إسقاط وإن كان بلفظ الهبة. وفي الاختيار أن الهبة نوعان : تمليك وإسقاط وعليهما الإجماع، وأما هبة الدين من غير من هو عليه فصحيحة بشرط أن يأمره بقبضه؛ كذا في المنتقى وغيره. وظاهره أنه ليس بوكيل عنه في قبضه فيملكه ويكون هبة وقد صرح به في المحيط فقال : ولو وهب ديناً له على رجل وأمره أن يقبضه فقبضه جازت الهبة استحساناً فيصير قابضاً للواهب بحكم النيابة، ثم يصير قابضاً لنفسه بحكم الهبة، وإن لم يأذن في القبض لم يجز. وسببها إرادة الخير الواهب دنيوي كالعوض وحسن الثناء والمحبة من الموهوب له، وأخروي وشرائط صحتها في الواهب العقل والبلوغ والملك فلا تصح هبة المجنون والصغير والعبد ولو مكاتباً أو أم ولد أو مدبراً أو مبعضاً وخير المالك، وفي الموهوب أن يكون مقبوضاً غير مشاع متميزاً غير مشغول على ما سيأتي تفصيله، وركنها هو الإيجاب والقبول، وحكمها ثبوت الملك للموهوب له غير لازم حتى يصح الرجوع والفسخ وعدم صحة خيار الشرط فيها فلو وهبه على أن الموهوب له بالخيار ثلاثة أيام. صحت الهبة إن اختارها قبل أن يتفرقا ولو أبرأه على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام صح الإبراء وبطل الخيار ؛ كذا الخلاصة. وأنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة حتى لو وهب لرجل عبده على أن يعتقه صحت الهبة وبطل الشرط. ومحاسنها كثيرة حتى قال الإمام أبو منصور : يجب على المؤمن أن يعلم ولده الجود والإحسان كما يجب عليه أن يعلمه التوحيد والإيمان إذ حب الدنيا رأس كل خطيئة؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (وتصح بإيجاب كقوله وهبت ونحلت وأطعمتك هذا الطعام) في كتاب الهبة قوله : (وقد صرح به في المحيط) أي بكونه وكيلاً عنه في قبضه تأمل قوله: (فشمل ماذا كان
Book of Gift. It is the transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation. It is valid with offer and acceptance, like 'I gave', 'I granted', and 'I fed you'. It is linguistically: doing good to another with something beneficial, even if not wealth; and technically: what the author indicated. His statement: (It is the transfer of ownership of an asset without compensation excludes Ibahah, Aariyah, Ijarah, Bai', and the gift of a debt by the debtor, for it is a waiver even if in the wording of gift. In al-Ikhtiyar, gift is of two types: transfer of ownership and waiver; and there is consensus on both. As for the gift of a debt by someone other than the debtor, it is valid on condition that he commands him to take possession; as in al-Muntaqa and others. And its apparent meaning is that he is not a deputy for him in taking possession, so he owns it and it becomes a gift. This has been explicitly stated in al-Muhit, where it says: If a person gifts a debt he has against a man and commands him to take possession of it, and he does so, the gift is valid by istihsan; he becomes a taker for the donor as a deputy, then becomes a taker for himself as a gift; and if he does not permit taking possession, it is not valid. Its cause is the donor's desire for good, worldly like compensation, good praise, and love from the donee, and hereafter. Conditions for its validity regarding the donor: sanity, maturity, and ownership; so the gift of a madman, minor, slave (even if maktub, umm walad, mudabbar, or muba'ad) and the owner's choice (?) is not valid. Regarding the donee, the subject must be taken possession of, not undivided, distinguished, not encumbered, as will be detailed later. Its pillar is offer and acceptance. Its ruling is the establishment of ownership for the donee, but it is not binding, so revocation and annulment are valid, and the condition of option (khiyar al-shart) is not valid in it. So if he gifts to him on condition that the donee has an option for three days, the gift is valid if he chooses before they separate. If he gives him an acquittance on condition that he has an option for three days, the acquittance is valid and the option becomes void. And it is not invalidated by invalid conditions; even if he gifts a slave to a man on condition that he emancipates him, the gift is valid and the condition becomes void. Its merits are many; Imam Abu Mansur said: It is obligatory for a believer to teach his child generosity and beneficence as it is obligatory to teach him monotheism and faith, for love of the world is the root of all sin. His statement: (And it is valid with an offer like 'I gave', 'I granted', and 'I fed you this food') in the Book of Gift. His statement: (And has been explicitly stated in al-Muhit) i.e., that he is a deputy for him in taking possession; consider his statement: (so it includes what was...
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage introduces Kitab al-Hibah (Book of Gift) from al-Bahr al-Raiq. It provides the technical definition of hibah: the transfer of ownership of an asset (tamlik al-'ayn) without compensation (bila 'iwad). This definition excludes: ibahah (permission to use), aariyah (loan), ijarah (hire), and bai' (sale). Also excluded is the gift of a debt by the debtor to the creditor, as that is a waiver (isqat), not a transfer of ownership. The gift of a debt by a third party is valid only if the debtor is commanded to take possession of the debt (i.e., to pay it to the donee). The passage then lists conditions of validity: the donor must be sane, adult, and owner; the donee must take possession; the subject must be specific and not undivided; etc. The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance. Notably, hibah is not binding; the donor may revoke it. Conditions like khiyar al-shart (option) are invalid in hibah, but invalid conditions do not void the gift itself. The passage ends with a quote from Imam Abu Mansur emphasizing the importance of teaching generosity.

Key Terms
تمليك العين (tamlik al-'ayn): Transfer of ownership of a specific physical asset (as opposed to a benefit or debt).
بلا عوض (bila 'iwad): Without any compensation or exchange from the donee.
إسقاط (isqat): Waiver; dropping a right, such as forgiving a debt. In hibah, when the debtor gives the debt to the creditor, it is isqat, not tamlik.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if someone gifts a slave to another with the condition that the donee must emancipate him?
A) The gift is invalid because the condition is invalid.
B) The gift is valid but the condition becomes void.
C) Both the gift and condition are valid.
D) The gift is valid only if the donee actually emancipates the slave.

Key Takeaway
Hibah is a non-binding transfer of ownership without compensation, valid upon offer and acceptance, but revocable before possession.
