Definition and Essential Elements of Hibah (Gift)
Defines hibah and expands on its pillars, conditions, and variants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hibah, identify its pillars and conditions, and distinguish it from similar contracts.

Core Idea
Hibah is voluntary ownership transfer without compensation; its validity hinges on offer, acceptance, and specific conditions.

In this lesson, we analyze the opening definition of the chapter on gifts (hibah). The author defines hibah as tamlik al-ayn bi la 'iwad, which immediately distinguishes it from loans, leases, and sales. Understanding this definition is the foundation for all subsequent rulings on gifts. We will dissect the definition, examine its pillars and conditions, and explore special cases like the gift of debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Locate the definition phrase 'هي تمليك العين بلا عوض' (tamlik al-ayn bi la 'iwad). Break it into three components: tamlik (transfer of ownership), al-ayn (specific property), bi la 'iwad (without compensation).
Each component excludes other contracts.
2
Exclude non-hibah contracts
Using the definition, see what is excluded: ibahah (permission to use) excludes ownership transfer; ariyah (loan) excludes ownership; ijarah (lease) includes compensation; bay' (sale) includes compensation. Thus, only hibah fits the definition.
3
Analyze the gift of debt
The text distinguishes between gift of debt by the debtor (which is isqat/waiver) and by a third party (valid only if the donor orders the donee to collect). This shows the definition's application to intangibles.
This is an expansion probe into a nuanced variant.
4
Extract conditions and pillars
Identify conditions for the donor (sanity, maturity, ownership) and for the gift (must be delivered, non-shared, distinguished, not encumbered). The pillar (rukn) is offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul).
Conditions ensure the transfer is valid and enforceable.
5
Understand the effect of invalid conditions
The text states that invalid conditions (e.g., 'I gift you my slave on condition that you free him') do not invalidate the gift; the condition is void but the gift stands. This is a key Hanafi principle.
This principle appears in other contracts too.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة
هي تمليك العين بلا عوض وتصح بإيجاب وقبول كوهبت ونحلت وأطعمتك
هي
كتاب الهبة
لغة التفضل على الغير بما ينفعه ولو غير مال واصطلاحاً ما أشار إليه المصنف قوله : (هي تمليك العين بلا عوض فخرجت الإباحة والعارية والإجارة والبيع وهبة الدين ممن عليه فإنه إسقاط وإن كان بلفظ الهبة. وفي الاختيار أن الهبة نوعان : تمليك وإسقاط وعليهما الإجماع، وأما هبة الدين من غير من هو عليه فصحيحة بشرط أن يأمره بقبضه؛ كذا في المنتقى وغيره. وظاهره أنه ليس بوكيل عنه في قبضه فيملكه ويكون هبة وقد صرح به في المحيط فقال : ولو وهب ديناً له على رجل وأمره أن يقبضه فقبضه جازت الهبة استحساناً فيصير قابضاً للواهب بحكم النيابة، ثم يصير قابضاً لنفسه بحكم الهبة، وإن لم يأذن في القبض لم يجز. وسببها إرادة الخير الواهب دنيوي كالعوض وحسن الثناء والمحبة من الموهوب له، وأخروي وشرائط صحتها في الواهب العقل والبلوغ والملك فلا تصح هبة المجنون والصغير والعبد ولو مكاتباً أو أم ولد أو مدبراً أو مبعضاً وخير المالك، وفي الموهوب أن يكون مقبوضاً غير مشاع متميزاً غير مشغول على ما سيأتي تفصيله، وركنها هو الإيجاب والقبول، وحكمها ثبوت الملك للموهوب له غير لازم حتى يصح الرجوع والفسخ وعدم صحة خيار الشرط فيها فلو وهبه على أن الموهوب له بالخيار ثلاثة أيام. صحت الهبة إن اختارها قبل أن يتفرقا ولو أبرأه على أنه بالخيار ثلاثة أيام صح الإبراء وبطل الخيار ؛ كذا الخلاصة. وأنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة حتى لو وهب لرجل عبده على أن يعتقه صحت الهبة وبطل الشرط. ومحاسنها كثيرة حتى قال الإمام أبو منصور : يجب على المؤمن أن يعلم ولده الجود والإحسان كما يجب عليه أن يعلمه التوحيد والإيمان إذ حب الدنيا رأس كل خطيئة؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (وتصح بإيجاب كقوله وهبت ونحلت وأطعمتك هذا الطعام)
فشمل ما إذا كان على وجه المزاح فإن الهبة صحيحة؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وشمل ما إذا أضاف الهبة إلى جزء يعبر به عن الكل كما إذا قال وهبت لك فرجها كان هبة؛ كذا في الخلاصة أيضاً. وشمل ما لو قال لقوم قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم رجل منهم ملكها، وكذا بقوله أذنت الناس جميعاً في ثمر نخلي من أخذ شيئاً فهو له فبلغ الناس من أخذ شيئاً يملكه ؛ كذا في المنتقى. وظاهره أن من أخذه ولم يبلغه مقالة الواهب لا يكون له كما لا يخفى. وقيد بالطعام لأنه لو قال أطعمتك أرضي كان عارية لرقبتها وإطعاماً لغلتها؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : ( وجعلته لك) لأن اللام للتمليك ولهذا لو قال هذه الأمة لك كان هبة، ولو قال لك حلال لا تكون هبة إلا أن يكون قبله كلام يستدل به على أنه أراد به الهبة كذا في الخلاصة . قيد بقوله «لك» لأنه لو قال جعلته باسمك لا يكون هبة ولهذا قال في الخلاصة : لو غرس لابنه كرماً إن قال جعلته لابني تكون هبة، وإن قال باسم ابني لا تكون هبة. ولو قال أغرس باسم ابني فالأمر متردد وهو إلى الصحة أقرب اهـ. قوله : (وأعمرتك هذا الشيء لأن العمرى تمليك للحال فتثبت الهبة ويبطل ما اقتضاه من شرط الرجوع، وكذلك لو شرط الرجوع صريحاً يبطل شرطه أيضاً كما لو قال وهبتك هذا العبد حياتك وخياته أو أعمرتك داري هذه حياتك أو أعطيتها حياتك أو وهبت هذا العبد حياتك فإذا مت فهو لي أو إذا مت فهو لورثتي هذا تمليك صحيح وشرط باطل لما تقدم أنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة.
قوله : ( وحملتك على هذه الدابة ناوياً الهبة لأن الحمل على الدابة إركاب وهو تصرف في منافعها لا في عينها فتكون عارية إلا أن يقول صاحبها أردت الهبة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد عليه، ومثله أخدمتك هذه الجارية قوله : وكسوتك هذا الثوب) لأنه يراد به
Book of Gift. It is the transfer of ownership of a specific property without compensation, and it is valid with offer and acceptance, such as 'I gave' and 'I gifted' and 'I fed you'. ... (continues full passage translation)
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a definition and expansion on the rulings of hibah (gift) from the Hanafi perspective. The author defines hibah as tamlik al-ayn bi la 'iwad (transfer of ownership of a specific property without compensation). This excludes ibahah (permission to use), ariyah (loan), ijarah (lease), and bay' (sale). The pillar (rukn) is offer and acceptance. Conditions for the donor: sanity, maturity, and ownership. Conditions for the gift: it must be delivered, non-shared (ghayr musha'), distinguished, and not encumbered. The ruling established is that the donee owns the property, but the gift is not irrevocable—the donor may revoke under certain conditions. The passage also discusses various forms of offer wording and their effects, such as when the donor jests or attaches invalid conditions.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): Tamlik al-ayn bi la 'iwad (transfer of ownership without compensation)
تمليك العين بلا عوض (tamlik al-ayn bi la 'iwad): Transfer of ownership of a specific property without receiving any compensation in return.
إيجاب وقبول (ijab wa qabul): Offer and acceptance: the two pillars (rukn) of the gift contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the definition of a gift (hibah)?
A) The transfer of ownership without compensation
B) The loan of an object for use without charge
C) The sale of an item at a reduced price
D) A conditional transfer of property

Key Takeaway
Hibah is a valid contract of gratuitous transfer of ownership, defined by offer and acceptance, with specific conditions on donor and gift.

Explicit Expressions of Hibah and Their Legal Effect
Defines explicit expressions of hibah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify explicit expressions that constitute a valid hibah in Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling for each expression.

Core Idea
Hibah is valid by explicit words of tamlik, even if uttered in jest.

The Hanafi school recognizes hibah as a legal transfer of ownership that can be effected by clear, explicit language. This lesson examines the primary phrases that are considered sarih (explicit) in constituting a gift, including the key principle that jest does not invalidate the gift. Understanding these expressions is essential for correctly categorizing a donor's words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression
Extract the exact words used by the donor from the passage. Examples include 'food', 'I made it for you', 'I granted you lifetime usufruct', 'I mounted you on this animal'.
Focus on the phrase itself, not the surrounding context.
2
Check if it is explicit (sarih)
The commentator states these are 'sariha fiha' – explicit for hibah. Explicit expressions require no further intention to create ownership.
If the expression is sarih, the gift is valid even if said jokingly.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on jest
Unlike other contracts (e.g., divorce, sale), hibah in jest is valid. The passage says 'if it is in jest, the hibah is valid'.
This is specific to hibah; other schools may differ.
4
Examine special cases
The passage covers when the gift is made to an unspecified person ('one of you') or to the public (palm tree fruit). In such cases, ownership transfers upon taking with the donor's permission.
Note the condition that the taker must hear the offer.

الهبة) لأن الحمل على الدابة إركاب وهو تصرف في منافعها لا في عينها فتكون عارية إلا أن يقول صاحبها أردت الهبة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد عليه، ومثله أخدمتك هذه الجارية. قوله: (وكسوتك هذا الثوب) لأنه يراد به التمليك قال تعالى ﴿أو كسوتهم﴾ [المائدة: 89] ويقال كسا الأمير فلاناً ثوباً إذا ملكه لا إذا أعاره. وفي الخلاصة: لو دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. ولو قال متعتك بهذا الثوب أو بهذه الدراهم فهي هبة؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وداري لك هبة تسكنها) لأن قوله تسكنها مشورة وليس بتفسير لأن الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنه، فإن شاء قبل مشورته وإن شاء لم يقبل كقوله هذا الطعام لك تأكله أو هذا الثوب لك تلبسه، وقد تقدم أن العمرى كالهبة فقوله هنا «هبة» ليس بقيد بل لو قال داري لك عمرى تسكنها كان كذلك؛ نص عليه في النهاية. قوله: (لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة) بنصب «هبة» فيهما على الحال ويحتمل انتصابهما على التمييز لما في قوله داري لك من الإبهام يعني أنها عارية فيهما لأن السكنى محكم في تمليك المنفعة فكان عارية قدم لفظ الهبة أو أخره. ولو ذكر بدل سكنى عارية كان عارية بالأولى، ولو قال هي لك هبة إجارة كل شهر بدرهم أو إجارة هبة فهي إجارة غير لازمة فيملك كل فسخها بعد القبض ولو سكن وجب الأجر؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وقبول) أي صحت الهبة بالإيجاب والقبول في حق الموهوب له لأنه عقد فينعقد بهما كسائر العقود. قيدنا بكونهما في حق الموهوب له لأنها تصح بالإيجاب وحده في حق الواهب لما ذكروا في الأيمان أنه لو حلف أن يهب عبده لفلان فوهب فلم يقبل بر في يمينه بخلاف البيع والقبول تارة يكون بالقول وتارة بالفعل. ومن الثاني ما قدمناه من قوله لو قال قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم فليأخذها شاء فأخذها رجل منهم تكون له وكان أخذه قبولاً. وما في المحيط من أنها تدل على أنه لا يشترط في الهبة القبول مشكل وفيه: رجل دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. وفي الخلاصة: وغيره.
This is the book of hibah. Food, and making it for you, and granting you lifetime usufruct of this thing, and mounting you on this animal intending hibah: let him take it whoever wishes, so a man takes it, then it is for him because these are explicit expressions. It (the author) made it absolute, so it includes when it is in jest, then the hibah is valid; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes when he adds the hibah to a part that expresses the whole, as if he said 'I gifted you its private part' then it is hibah; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes if he said to a group 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you' then a man from them owns it, and likewise by his saying 'I have given permission to all people in the fruit of my palm tree; whoever takes something then it is for him' and the news reaches the people, then whoever takes something he owns it; likewise in al-Muntaqa. And its apparent meaning is that whoever takes it and the words of the donor do not reach him, it does not belong to him, as is not hidden. And it is restricted to food because if he said 'I fed you my land' it is a loan for its corpus and feeding for its produce; likewise in al-Muhit. His saying: (and I made it for you) because the lam is for making ownersip, and for this if he said 'this slave-girl is for you' it is hibah, and if he said 'it is halal for you' it is not hibah unless there is preceding speech indicating that he intended hibah; like in al-Khulasa. He restricted by his saying 'for you' because if he said 'I made it in your name' it is not hibah, and for this he said in al-Khulasa: if he planted a vineyard for his son, if he said 'I made it for my son' it is hibah, and if he said 'in my son's name' it is not hibah. And if he said 'I will plant in my son's name' it is undecided but closer to validity. His saying: (and I granted you lifetime usufruct of this thing) because 'umra is a present ownership on the spot, so hibah is established and what it implies of a condition of return is void, and likewise if he explicitly conditions return, his condition is also void, as if he said 'I gifted you this slave for your life' or 'I granted you lifetime usufruct of this house for your life' or 'I gave it to you for your life' or 'I gifted this slave for your life, then when you die it belongs to me' or 'when I die it belongs to my heirs', this is a valid ownership and a void condition because it is established that they are not invalidated by void conditions. His saying: (and I mounted you on this animal, intending hibah) because mounting on the animal is riding, which is a disposition on its benefits not its corpus, so it is a loan unless the owner says 'I intended hibah' because he intended a possibility of his speech, and there is strictness upon him, and similar is 'I made this slave-girl serve you'. His saying: (and I clothed you with this garment) because it intends ownership. Allah Almighty said: 'or clothe them' [al-Maida:89] and it is said 'the amir clothed so-and-so with a garment' if he gave ownership, not if he lent. In al-Khulasa: if he gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he does so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And if he said 'I have given you enjoyment of this garment or these dirhams' then it is hibah; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and my house is a gift for you to dwell in) because his saying 'to dwell in' is an advice, not a definition, because a verb cannot define a noun, so he indicated to him regarding his ownership that he may dwell; if he wishes he accepts his advice and if he wishes he does not, like his saying 'this food is for you to eat' or 'this garment is for you to wear'. And it has preceded that 'umra is like hibah, so his saying here 'gift' is not a restriction; rather if he said 'my house is a lifetime grant for you to dwell in' it would be the same; stated by al-Nihaya. His saying: (not a gift of dwelling or a dwelling as gift) with 'gift' in the accusative in both as a circumstantial accusative, and it is possible that they are accusative as a specification because of the ambiguity in his saying 'my house is for you', meaning that it is a loan in both cases because dwelling is dominant in ownership of benefits, so it is a loan whether he puts the word 'gift' first or last. And if he mentions 'loan' instead of 'dwelling' then it is a loan a fortiori, and if he said 'it is for you a gift as lease every month for a dirham' or 'a lease as a gift' then it is a non-binding lease, so each may rescind it after possession, but if he dwells, rent is due; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and acceptance) meaning the hibah is valid by offer and acceptance with respect to the donee because it is a contract, so it is concluded by both like other contracts. We restricted it to being with respect to the donee because it is valid by offer alone with respect to the donor, as they mentioned in oaths: if he swore to gift his slave to Zayd and then gifted and he did not accept, his oath is fulfilled, unlike a sale. And acceptance is sometimes by word and sometimes by act. From the second is what we have presented: if he said 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you, so let him take it whoever wishes' and a man takes her, it becomes his, and his taking was acceptance. And what is in al-Muhit that it indicates that acceptance is not required in hibah is problematic, and in it: a man gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he did so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And in al-Khulasa: etc.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the book of hibah (gift). The commentator, Imam Ibn Najim, explains the various expressions that constitute a valid hibah according to the Hanafi school. The passage covers explicit phrases like 'I gave you food', 'I made it for you', 'I granted you lifetime usufruct', and 'I mounted you on this animal', noting that these are clear in establishing hibah. Importantly, hibah is valid even if done in jest, unlike some other contracts. The commentator distinguishes between phrases that express outright ownership (tamlik) and those that only indicate permission or lending (aariya). For example, saying 'this is for you' (lak) with the lam of tamlik constitutes hibah, while saying 'it is halal for you' does not unless context indicates otherwise. The passage also discusses the concept of 'umra (lifetime grant), where the prohibition of return conditions is void but the grant itself is valid. When someone mounts another on an animal, it is presumed a loan unless the owner states an intention of gift, because the act primarily deals with benefits. A key Qirat verse from Surah al-Maida (5:89) is cited to support that 'clothing' (kiswah) implies ownership in the language. The commentator also addresses the acceptance (qabul) in hibah: while hibah is a contract requiring offer and acceptance, acceptance can be by action (e.g., taking possession) in certain contexts, such as when the donor says 'take it whoever wishes'. The passage thus provides detailed guidance on the proper wording and conditions for a valid hibah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): A contract of gift that transfers ownership of a property without exchange (tabarru'), requiring offer and acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements about hibah in Hanafi fiqh is true according to the passage?
A) Hibah in jest is invalid.
B) If a person says 'I have given my slave-girl to one of you' and a man takes her without hearing the offer, he owns her.
C) Explicit expressions of hibah are valid even if uttered in jest.
D) Hibah to an unspecified person is never valid.

Key Takeaway
Explicit words of tamlik create a valid hibah in Hanafi law, and jest does not affect its validity.

Distinguishing Hibah from Loan: Ambiguous Phrases and Intention
Explains how to distinguish hibah from aariyah using linguistic and intentional cues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between phrases that constitute hibah and those that indicate a loan (aariyah) based on linguistic usage and the donor's intention.

Core Idea
A gift of usufruct is a loan; only transfer of the corpus is hibah.

Many expressions can be ambiguous, potentially indicating either a transfer of ownership (hibah) or merely a permission to use (aariyah). The Hanafi school relies on linguistic principles and the donor's intention to classify these phrases. This lesson explores key examples such as 'I made it for you', 'I clothed you', 'I mounted you', and the concept of 'umra, showing how the law resolves ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase
Examine the words used: e.g., 'I fed you my land', 'I made it for you', 'I mounted you on this animal', 'I clothed you', 'my house is for you to dwell'.
Focus on the verb and preposition.
2
Analyze whether the phrase implies ownership of the thing or its benefit
Linguistically, 'clothing' (kiswah) implies ownership (as per Quran 5:89), while 'mounting' implies benefit. The passage says 'kasa al-amir' means to give ownership, not lend.
The Quranic usage is a guide.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule on ambiguous phrases
If the phrase is kinayah (ambiguous), the donor's intention is considered. If the phrase can be taken as a loan, it defaults to loan unless the donor states he intended gift.
The phrase 'I mounted you on this animal' defaults to aariyah unless the donor says he intended hibah.
4
Handle special cases: 'umra, 'I made it for you' vs 'in your name', and 'my house is for you to dwell'
'Umra is a lifetime grant; the condition of return is void but the grant itself is hibah. 'I made it for you' (lak) is hibah; 'in your name' is not. 'My house is for you to dwell' is advice, not hibah.
Each case has a specific ruling based on the wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا كتاب الهبة. الطعام وجعلته لك وأعمرتك هذا الشيء وحملتك على هذه الدابة ناوياً به الهبة فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل منهم فله لأنها صريحة فيها. أطلقها فشمل ما إذا كان على وجه المزاح فإن الهبة صحيحة؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وشمل ما إذا أضاف الهبة إلى جزء يعبر به عن الكل كما إذا قال وهبت لك فرجها كان هبة؛ كذا في الخلاصة أيضاً. وشمل ما لو قال لقوم قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم رجل منهم ملكها، وكذا بقوله أذنت الناس جميعاً في ثمن نخلي من أخذ شيئاً فهو له فبلغ الناس من أخذ شيئاً يملكه؛ كذا في المنتقى. وظاهره أن من أخذه ولم يبلغه مقالة الواهب لا يكون له كما لا يخفى. وقيد بالطعام لأنه لو قال أطعمتك أرضي كان عارية لرقبتها وإطعاماً لغلتها؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وجعلته لك) لأن اللام للتمليك ولهذا لو قال هذه الأمة لك كان هبة، ولو قال لك حلال لا تكون هبة إلا أن يكون قبله كلام يستدل به على أنه أراد به الهبة كذا في الخلاصة. قيد بقوله «لك» لأنه لو قال جعلته باسمك لا يكون هبة ولهذا قال في الخلاصة: لو غرس لابنه كرماً إن قال جعلته لابني تكون هبة، وإن قال باسم ابني لا تكون هبة. ولو قال أغرس باسم ابني فالأمر متردد وهو إلى الصحة أقرب اهـ. قوله: (وأعمرتك هذا الشيء) لأن العمرى تمليك للحال فتثبت الهبة ويبطل ما اقتضاه من شرط الرجوع، وكذلك لو شرط الرجوع صريحاً يبطل شرطه أيضاً كما لو قال وهبتك هذا العبد حياتك أو أعمرتك داري هذه حياتك أو أعطيتها حياتك أو وهبت هذا العبد حياتك فإذا مت فهو لي أو إذا مت فهو لورثتي هذا تمليك صحيح وشرط باطل لما تقدم أنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. قوله: (وحملتك على هذه الدابة ناوياً الهبة) لأن الحمل على الدابة إركاب وهو تصرف في منافعها لا في عينها فتكون عارية إلا أن يقول صاحبها أردت الهبة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد عليه، ومثله أخدمتك هذه الجارية. قوله: (وكسوتك هذا الثوب) لأنه يراد به التمليك قال تعالى ﴿أو كسوتهم﴾ [المائدة: 89] ويقال كسا الأمير فلاناً ثوباً إذا ملكه لا إذا أعاره. وفي الخلاصة: لو دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. ولو قال متعتك بهذا الثوب أو بهذه الدراهم فهي هبة؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وداري لك هبة تسكنها) لأن قوله تسكنها مشورة وليس بتفسير لأن الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنه، فإن شاء قبل مشورته وإن شاء لم يقبل كقوله هذا الطعام لك تأكله أو هذا الثوب لك تلبسه، وقد تقدم أن العمرى كالهبة فقوله هنا «هبة» ليس بقيد بل لو قال داري لك عمرى تسكنها كان كذلك؛ نص عليه في النهاية. قوله: (لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة) بنصب «هبة» فيهما على الحال ويحتمل انتصابهما على التمييز لما في قوله داري لك من الإبهام يعني أنها عارية فيهما لأن السكنى محكم في تمليك المنفعة فكان عارية قدم لفظ الهبة أو أخره. ولو ذكر بدل سكنى عارية كان عارية بالأولى، ولو قال هي لك هبة إجارة كل شهر بدرهم أو إجارة هبة فهي إجارة غير لازمة فيملك كل فسخها بعد القبض ولو سكن وجب الأجر؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وقبول) أي صحت الهبة بالإيجاب والقبول في حق الموهوب له لأنه عقد فينعقد بهما كسائر العقود. قيدنا بكونهما في حق الموهوب له لأنها تصح بالإيجاب وحده في حق الواهب لما ذكروا في الأيمان أنه لو حلف أن يهب عبده لفلان فوهب فلم يقبل بر في يمينه بخلاف البيع والقبول تارة يكون بالقول وتارة بالفعل. ومن الثاني ما قدمناه من قوله لو قال قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم فليأخذها شاء فأخذها رجل منهم تكون له وكان أخذه قبولاً. وما في المحيط من أنها تدل على أنه لا يشترط في الهبة القبول مشكل وفيه: رجل دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. وفي الخلاصة: وغيره.
This is the book of hibah. Food, and making it for you, and granting you lifetime usufruct of this thing, and mounting you on this animal intending hibah: let him take it whoever wishes, so a man takes it, then it is for him because these are explicit expressions. It (the author) made it absolute, so it includes when it is in jest, then the hibah is valid; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes when he adds the hibah to a part that expresses the whole, as if he said 'I gifted you its private part' then it is hibah; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes if he said to a group 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you' then a man from them owns it, and likewise by his saying 'I have given permission to all people in the fruit of my palm tree; whoever takes something then it is for him' and the news reaches the people, then whoever takes something he owns it; likewise in al-Muntaqa. And its apparent meaning is that whoever takes it and the words of the donor do not reach him, it does not belong to him, as is not hidden. And it is restricted to food because if he said 'I fed you my land' it is a loan for its corpus and feeding for its produce; likewise in al-Muhit. His saying: (and I made it for you) because the lam is for making ownersip, and for this if he said 'this slave-girl is for you' it is hibah, and if he said 'it is halal for you' it is not hibah unless there is preceding speech indicating that he intended hibah; like in al-Khulasa. He restricted by his saying 'for you' because if he said 'I made it in your name' it is not hibah, and for this he said in al-Khulasa: if he planted a vineyard for his son, if he said 'I made it for my son' it is hibah, and if he said 'in my son's name' it is not hibah. And if he said 'I will plant in my son's name' it is undecided but closer to validity. His saying: (and I granted you lifetime usufruct of this thing) because 'umra is a present ownership on the spot, so hibah is established and what it implies of a condition of return is void, and likewise if he explicitly conditions return, his condition is also void, as if he said 'I gifted you this slave for your life' or 'I granted you lifetime usufruct of this house for your life' or 'I gave it to you for your life' or 'I gifted this slave for your life, then when you die it belongs to me' or 'when I die it belongs to my heirs', this is a valid ownership and a void condition because it is established that they are not invalidated by void conditions. His saying: (and I mounted you on this animal, intending hibah) because mounting on the animal is riding, which is a disposition on its benefits not its corpus, so it is a loan unless the owner says 'I intended hibah' because he intended a possibility of his speech, and there is strictness upon him, and similar is 'I made this slave-girl serve you'. His saying: (and I clothed you with this garment) because it intends ownership. Allah Almighty said: 'or clothe them' [al-Maida:89] and it is said 'the amir clothed so-and-so with a garment' if he gave ownership, not if he lent. In al-Khulasa: if he gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he does so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And if he said 'I have given you enjoyment of this garment or these dirhams' then it is hibah; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and my house is a gift for you to dwell in) because his saying 'to dwell in' is an advice, not a definition, because a verb cannot define a noun, so he indicated to him regarding his ownership that he may dwell; if he wishes he accepts his advice and if he wishes he does not, like his saying 'this food is for you to eat' or 'this garment is for you to wear'. And it has preceded that 'umra is like hibah, so his saying here 'gift' is not a restriction; rather if he said 'my house is a lifetime grant for you to dwell in' it would be the same; stated by al-Nihaya. His saying: (not a gift of dwelling or a dwelling as gift) with 'gift' in the accusative in both as a circumstantial accusative, and it is possible that they are accusative as a specification because of the ambiguity in his saying 'my house is for you', meaning that it is a loan in both cases because dwelling is dominant in ownership of benefits, so it is a loan whether he puts the word 'gift' first or last. And if he mentions 'loan' instead of 'dwelling' then it is a loan a fortiori, and if he said 'it is for you a gift as lease every month for a dirham' or 'a lease as a gift' then it is a non-binding lease, so each may rescind it after possession, but if he dwells, rent is due; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and acceptance) meaning the hibah is valid by offer and acceptance with respect to the donee because it is a contract, so it is concluded by both like other contracts. We restricted it to being with respect to the donee because it is valid by offer alone with respect to the donor, as they mentioned in oaths: if he swore to gift his slave to Zayd and then gifted and he did not accept, his oath is fulfilled, unlike a sale. And acceptance is sometimes by word and sometimes by act. From the second is what we have presented: if he said 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you, so let him take it whoever wishes' and a man takes her, it becomes his, and his taking was acceptance. And what is in al-Muhit that it indicates that acceptance is not required in hibah is problematic, and in it: a man gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he did so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And in al-Khulasa: etc.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the book of hibah (gift). The commentator, Imam Ibn Najim, explains the various expressions that constitute a valid hibah according to the Hanafi school. The passage covers explicit phrases like 'I gave you food', 'I made it for you', 'I granted you lifetime usufruct', and 'I mounted you on this animal', noting that these are clear in establishing hibah. Importantly, hibah is valid even if done in jest, unlike some other contracts. The commentator distinguishes between phrases that express outright ownership (tamlik) and those that only indicate permission or lending (aariya). For example, saying 'this is for you' (lak) with the lam of tamlik constitutes hibah, while saying 'it is halal for you' does not unless context indicates otherwise. The passage also discusses the concept of 'umra (lifetime grant), where the prohibition of return conditions is void but the grant itself is valid. When someone mounts another on an animal, it is presumed a loan unless the owner states an intention of gift, because the act primarily deals with benefits. A key Qirat verse from Surah al-Maida (5:89) is cited to support that 'clothing' (kiswah) implies ownership in the language. The commentator also addresses the acceptance (qabul) in hibah: while hibah is a contract requiring offer and acceptance, acceptance can be by action (e.g., taking possession) in certain contexts, such as when the donor says 'take it whoever wishes'. The passage thus provides detailed guidance on the proper wording and conditions for a valid hibah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
عمرى ('umra): A lifetime grant of property to a person; it is considered a hibah with a void condition of return after the donee's death.
عارية (aariyah): A loan of a thing for use without transfer of ownership; the benefits are allowed temporarily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a person says 'I have mounted you on this animal' without additional intention?
A) It is a hibah of the animal.
B) It is a loan (aariyah) of the animal.
C) It is a sale.
D) It is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the classification of a phrase as hibah or aariyah depends on whether it transfers ownership of the corpus or merely the benefit, with linguistic usage and donor intention as guides.

Acceptance (Qabul) in Hibah: When is it Required and How?
Clarifies the role of acceptance in hibah and distinguishes the donor's perspective from the donee's

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of acceptance in hibah according to Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between the donor's and donee's positions.

Core Idea
Acceptance is required for the donee to own, but for the donor's oath, offer alone suffices.

Hibah is a contract, and like other contracts, it generally requires offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul). However, the Hanafi school makes a nuanced distinction: in the context of oaths, the donor's offer alone fulfills his vow, whereas for the donee to acquire ownership, acceptance is necessary. This lesson explores that distinction and how acceptance can be verbal or by act (e.g., taking possession).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts
The donor's context (oath) and the donee's context (ownership). The passage states: 'it is valid by offer alone with respect to the donor... because if he swore to gift and gifted, his oath is fulfilled even if not accepted.'
For the donee, acceptance is needed; for the donor, it is not.
2
Determine the forms of acceptance
Acceptance can be by word (qawl) or by act (fi'l). Example: when the donor says 'take it whoever wishes' and a man takes it, the taking is acceptance.
The act must be done with knowledge of the offer.
3
Resolve the problematic report from al-Muhit
The passage mentions a report in al-Muhit that 'it indicates that acceptance is not required in hibah' but calls it 'problematic' (mushkil). The relied-upon position is that acceptance is required for the donee.
This internal disagreement is resolved by adhering to the majority view.

هذا كتاب الهبة. الطعام وجعلته لك وأعمرتك هذا الشيء وحملتك على هذه الدابة ناوياً به الهبة فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل منهم فله لأنها صريحة فيها. أطلقها فشمل ما إذا كان على وجه المزاح فإن الهبة صحيحة؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وشمل ما إذا أضاف الهبة إلى جزء يعبر به عن الكل كما إذا قال وهبت لك فرجها كان هبة؛ كذا في الخلاصة أيضاً. وشمل ما لو قال لقوم قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم رجل منهم ملكها، وكذا بقوله أذنت الناس جميعاً في ثمن نخلي من أخذ شيئاً فهو له فبلغ الناس من أخذ شيئاً يملكه؛ كذا في المنتقى. وظاهره أن من أخذه ولم يبلغه مقالة الواهب لا يكون له كما لا يخفى. وقيد بالطعام لأنه لو قال أطعمتك أرضي كان عارية لرقبتها وإطعاماً لغلتها؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وجعلته لك) لأن اللام للتمليك ولهذا لو قال هذه الأمة لك كان هبة، ولو قال لك حلال لا تكون هبة إلا أن يكون قبله كلام يستدل به على أنه أراد به الهبة كذا في الخلاصة. قيد بقوله «لك» لأنه لو قال جعلته باسمك لا يكون هبة ولهذا قال في الخلاصة: لو غرس لابنه كرماً إن قال جعلته لابني تكون هبة، وإن قال باسم ابني لا تكون هبة. ولو قال أغرس باسم ابني فالأمر متردد وهو إلى الصحة أقرب اهـ. قوله: (وأعمرتك هذا الشيء) لأن العمرى تمليك للحال فتثبت الهبة ويبطل ما اقتضاه من شرط الرجوع، وكذلك لو شرط الرجوع صريحاً يبطل شرطه أيضاً كما لو قال وهبتك هذا العبد حياتك أو أعمرتك داري هذه حياتك أو أعطيتها حياتك أو وهبت هذا العبد حياتك فإذا مت فهو لي أو إذا مت فهو لورثتي هذا تمليك صحيح وشرط باطل لما تقدم أنها لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة. قوله: (وحملتك على هذه الدابة ناوياً الهبة) لأن الحمل على الدابة إركاب وهو تصرف في منافعها لا في عينها فتكون عارية إلا أن يقول صاحبها أردت الهبة لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه وفيه تشديد عليه، ومثله أخدمتك هذه الجارية. قوله: (وكسوتك هذا الثوب) لأنه يراد به التمليك قال تعالى ﴿أو كسوتهم﴾ [المائدة: 89] ويقال كسا الأمير فلاناً ثوباً إذا ملكه لا إذا أعاره. وفي الخلاصة: لو دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. ولو قال متعتك بهذا الثوب أو بهذه الدراهم فهي هبة؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وداري لك هبة تسكنها) لأن قوله تسكنها مشورة وليس بتفسير لأن الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنه، فإن شاء قبل مشورته وإن شاء لم يقبل كقوله هذا الطعام لك تأكله أو هذا الثوب لك تلبسه، وقد تقدم أن العمرى كالهبة فقوله هنا «هبة» ليس بقيد بل لو قال داري لك عمرى تسكنها كان كذلك؛ نص عليه في النهاية. قوله: (لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة) بنصب «هبة» فيهما على الحال ويحتمل انتصابهما على التمييز لما في قوله داري لك من الإبهام يعني أنها عارية فيهما لأن السكنى محكم في تمليك المنفعة فكان عارية قدم لفظ الهبة أو أخره. ولو ذكر بدل سكنى عارية كان عارية بالأولى، ولو قال هي لك هبة إجارة كل شهر بدرهم أو إجارة هبة فهي إجارة غير لازمة فيملك كل فسخها بعد القبض ولو سكن وجب الأجر؛ كذا في المحيط. قوله: (وقبول) أي صحت الهبة بالإيجاب والقبول في حق الموهوب له لأنه عقد فينعقد بهما كسائر العقود. قيدنا بكونهما في حق الموهوب له لأنها تصح بالإيجاب وحده في حق الواهب لما ذكروا في الأيمان أنه لو حلف أن يهب عبده لفلان فوهب فلم يقبل بر في يمينه بخلاف البيع والقبول تارة يكون بالقول وتارة بالفعل. ومن الثاني ما قدمناه من قوله لو قال قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم فليأخذها شاء فأخذها رجل منهم تكون له وكان أخذه قبولاً. وما في المحيط من أنها تدل على أنه لا يشترط في الهبة القبول مشكل وفيه: رجل دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً. وفي الخلاصة: وغيره.
This is the book of hibah. Food, and making it for you, and granting you lifetime usufruct of this thing, and mounting you on this animal intending hibah: let him take it whoever wishes, so a man takes it, then it is for him because these are explicit expressions. It (the author) made it absolute, so it includes when it is in jest, then the hibah is valid; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes when he adds the hibah to a part that expresses the whole, as if he said 'I gifted you its private part' then it is hibah; likewise in al-Khulasa. And it includes if he said to a group 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you' then a man from them owns it, and likewise by his saying 'I have given permission to all people in the fruit of my palm tree; whoever takes something then it is for him' and the news reaches the people, then whoever takes something he owns it; likewise in al-Muntaqa. And its apparent meaning is that whoever takes it and the words of the donor do not reach him, it does not belong to him, as is not hidden. And it is restricted to food because if he said 'I fed you my land' it is a loan for its corpus and feeding for its produce; likewise in al-Muhit. His saying: (and I made it for you) because the lam is for making ownersip, and for this if he said 'this slave-girl is for you' it is hibah, and if he said 'it is halal for you' it is not hibah unless there is preceding speech indicating that he intended hibah; like in al-Khulasa. He restricted by his saying 'for you' because if he said 'I made it in your name' it is not hibah, and for this he said in al-Khulasa: if he planted a vineyard for his son, if he said 'I made it for my son' it is hibah, and if he said 'in my son's name' it is not hibah. And if he said 'I will plant in my son's name' it is undecided but closer to validity. His saying: (and I granted you lifetime usufruct of this thing) because 'umra is a present ownership on the spot, so hibah is established and what it implies of a condition of return is void, and likewise if he explicitly conditions return, his condition is also void, as if he said 'I gifted you this slave for your life' or 'I granted you lifetime usufruct of this house for your life' or 'I gave it to you for your life' or 'I gifted this slave for your life, then when you die it belongs to me' or 'when I die it belongs to my heirs', this is a valid ownership and a void condition because it is established that they are not invalidated by void conditions. His saying: (and I mounted you on this animal, intending hibah) because mounting on the animal is riding, which is a disposition on its benefits not its corpus, so it is a loan unless the owner says 'I intended hibah' because he intended a possibility of his speech, and there is strictness upon him, and similar is 'I made this slave-girl serve you'. His saying: (and I clothed you with this garment) because it intends ownership. Allah Almighty said: 'or clothe them' [al-Maida:89] and it is said 'the amir clothed so-and-so with a garment' if he gave ownership, not if he lent. In al-Khulasa: if he gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he does so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And if he said 'I have given you enjoyment of this garment or these dirhams' then it is hibah; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and my house is a gift for you to dwell in) because his saying 'to dwell in' is an advice, not a definition, because a verb cannot define a noun, so he indicated to him regarding his ownership that he may dwell; if he wishes he accepts his advice and if he wishes he does not, like his saying 'this food is for you to eat' or 'this garment is for you to wear'. And it has preceded that 'umra is like hibah, so his saying here 'gift' is not a restriction; rather if he said 'my house is a lifetime grant for you to dwell in' it would be the same; stated by al-Nihaya. His saying: (not a gift of dwelling or a dwelling as gift) with 'gift' in the accusative in both as a circumstantial accusative, and it is possible that they are accusative as a specification because of the ambiguity in his saying 'my house is for you', meaning that it is a loan in both cases because dwelling is dominant in ownership of benefits, so it is a loan whether he puts the word 'gift' first or last. And if he mentions 'loan' instead of 'dwelling' then it is a loan a fortiori, and if he said 'it is for you a gift as lease every month for a dirham' or 'a lease as a gift' then it is a non-binding lease, so each may rescind it after possession, but if he dwells, rent is due; like in al-Muhit. His saying: (and acceptance) meaning the hibah is valid by offer and acceptance with respect to the donee because it is a contract, so it is concluded by both like other contracts. We restricted it to being with respect to the donee because it is valid by offer alone with respect to the donor, as they mentioned in oaths: if he swore to gift his slave to Zayd and then gifted and he did not accept, his oath is fulfilled, unlike a sale. And acceptance is sometimes by word and sometimes by act. From the second is what we have presented: if he said 'I have gifted this slave-girl of mine to one of you, so let him take it whoever wishes' and a man takes her, it becomes his, and his taking was acceptance. And what is in al-Muhit that it indicates that acceptance is not required in hibah is problematic, and in it: a man gave a garment to a man and said 'dress yourself' and he did so, it is hibah, and if he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And in al-Khulasa: etc.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the book of hibah (gift). The commentator, Imam Ibn Najim, explains the various expressions that constitute a valid hibah according to the Hanafi school. The passage covers explicit phrases like 'I gave you food', 'I made it for you', 'I granted you lifetime usufruct', and 'I mounted you on this animal', noting that these are clear in establishing hibah. Importantly, hibah is valid even if done in jest, unlike some other contracts. The commentator distinguishes between phrases that express outright ownership (tamlik) and those that only indicate permission or lending (aariya). For example, saying 'this is for you' (lak) with the lam of tamlik constitutes hibah, while saying 'it is halal for you' does not unless context indicates otherwise. The passage also discusses the concept of 'umra (lifetime grant), where the prohibition of return conditions is void but the grant itself is valid. When someone mounts another on an animal, it is presumed a loan unless the owner states an intention of gift, because the act primarily deals with benefits. A key Qirat verse from Surah al-Maida (5:89) is cited to support that 'clothing' (kiswah) implies ownership in the language. The commentator also addresses the acceptance (qabul) in hibah: while hibah is a contract requiring offer and acceptance, acceptance can be by action (e.g., taking possession) in certain contexts, such as when the donor says 'take it whoever wishes'. The passage thus provides detailed guidance on the proper wording and conditions for a valid hibah in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
قبول (qabool): Acceptance in a contract; in hibah, it can be verbal or by action (e.g., taking possession when authorized).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person swears to give his slave to Zaid and then offers the gift but Zaid does not accept, what is the status of the oath?
A) The oath is not fulfilled because no acceptance.
B) The oath is fulfilled because offer alone suffices for the donor.
C) The oath is void because the slave did not accept.
D) The oath is partially fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acceptance is necessary for the donee to own; but for the donor's oath, offer alone is sufficient to fulfill the vow.

Rulings on Gift Phrasing and Acceptance
Commentary on matn regarding gift vs. loan wording and acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between wording that constitutes a gift versus a loan, and explain how acceptance can be by taking possession in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a gift is valid by offer and acceptance; acceptance can be by taking possession, not just verbal declaration.

This lesson unpacks a key passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq on the wording of gifts. The rulings distinguish genuine gifts from loans based on the donor's expressions, and examine how acceptance perfects the contract. A central debate is whether acceptance must be verbal; the preferred Hanafi view allows acceptance by action, relying on custom and sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key issue: gift vs. loan wording
Examine the matn phrases 'وكسوتك هذا الثوب' and 'وداري لك هبة تسكنها'. Determine when words convey ownership (tamlik) vs. mere permission to use (ibaha).
The verb 'كسا' in the Quran (5:89) indicates giving ownership.
2
Analyze the donor's intent through wording
Apply the principle that the legal effect depends on the explicit language and customary usage. Words like 'تمتع' (enjoyment) indicate a gift; 'أنفق' (spend) indicates a loan.
Custom (urf) plays a role: 'جعل' (making) implies ownership transfer in common usage.
3
Examine acceptance methods: speech vs. action
Study the textual proof and arguments: the example of the slave-girl where taking possession constitutes acceptance. Identify the two views within the Hanafi school.
Abu Bakr al-Jassas and Abu al-Layth support acceptance by action; some earlier Hanafis required verbal acceptance.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi debate
Contrast the dalil of the opposing view (gift as contract like sale) and the Hanafi response (taking possession is a clear indication of consent, supported by custom).
The split is not between schools but within Hanafi itself; the mufta bihi is the more flexible view.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling to a scenario
Given a donee who takes a gift without saying 'I accept', state that the gift is valid and binding upon delivery according to the preferred view. Mention the thamarah al-khilaf.
This ruling is relied upon in fatwa, as stated by al-Ramli and others.

كتاب الهبة

وكسوتك هذا الثوب وداري لك هبة تسكنها لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة وقبول

التمليك قال تعالى أو كسوتهم ﴾ [المائدة : 89] ويقال كسا الأمير فلاناً ثوباً إذا ملكه لا إذا : لو دفع إلى رجل ثوباً وقال ألبس نفسك ففعل يكون هبة، ولو دفع إليه دراهم وقال أنفقها تكون قرضاً .اهـ. ولو قال متعتك بهذا الثوب أو بهذه الدراهم فهي هبة ؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (وداري لك هبة تسكنها لأن قوله تسكنها مشورة بضم الشين وليس بتفسير لأن الفعل لا يصلح تفسيراً للاسم فقد أشار عليه في ملكه بأن يسكنه، فإن شاء قبل مشورته وإن شاء لم يقبل كقوله هذا الطعام لك تأكله أو هذا الثوب لك تلبسه، وقد تقدم أن العمرى كالهبة فقوله هنا «هبة» ليس بقيد بل لو قال داري لك عمرى تسكنها كان كذلك ؛ نص عليه في النهاية قوله : (لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة بنصب «هبة» فيهما على الحال ويحتمل انتصابهما على التمييز لما في قوله داري لك من الإبهام يعني أنها عارية فيهما لأن السكنى محكم في تمليك المنفعة فكان عارية قدم لفظ الهبة أو أخره. ولو ذكر بدل سكنى عارية كان عارية بالأولى، ولو قال هي لك هبة إجارة كل شهر بدرهم أو إجارة هبة فهي إجارة غير لازمة فيملك كل فسخها بعد القبض ولو سكن وجب الأجر؛ كذا في المحيط قوله : (وقبول) أي صحت الهبة بالإيجاب والقبول في حق الموهوب له لأنه عقد فينعقد بهما كسائر العقود. قيدنا بكونهما في حق الموهوب له لأنها تصح بالإيجاب وحده في حق الواهب لما ذكروا في الأيمان أنه لو حلف أن يهب عبده لفلان فوهب فلم يقبل بر في يمينه بخلاف البيع والقبول تارة يكون بالقول وتارة بالفعل. ومن الثاني ما قدمناه من قوله لو قال قد وهبت جاريتي هذه لأحدكم فليأخذها من شاء فأخذها رجل منهم تكون له وكان أخذه من قبولاً. وما في المحيط من أنها تدل على أنه لا يشترط في الهبة القبول مشكل وفيه: رجل الجعل عبارة عن التمليك وإن قال اغرسه باسم ابني لا يكون هبة وإن قال جعلته باسم ابني يكون هبة لأن الناس يريدون به التمليك والهبة .اهـ. وفيه مخالفة لما في الخلاصة كما لا يخفى اهـ. قال الرملي في حاشية المنح : ما في الخانية أقرب لعرف الناس .اهـ. ورأيت في الولوالجية ما نصه : رجل له ابن صغير فغرس كرماً له فهذا على ثلاثة أوجه : إن قال أغرس هذا الكرم باسم ابني فلان أو قال جعلته لابني فلان هبة لأن الجعل إثبات فيكون تمليكاً، وإن قال جعلته باسم ابني فالأمر متردد وهو أقرب إلى الوجه الأوّل .اهـ. ولتراجع نسخة أخرى قوله : (وما) في المحيط من أنها تدل على أنه لا يشترط في الهبة القبول مشكل) الضمير في «أنها» للمسألة السابقة ويظهر لي أنه أراد بالقبول القبول صريحاً وأن القبول فعلاً يكفي وعليه يحمل الخلاف في اشتراط القبول وعدمه وبالله التوفيق. قال في التتارخانية وفي الذخيرة قال أبو بكر : حينئذ إذا قال الرجل لغيره وهبت عبدي هذا منك والعبد حاضر فقبض الموهوب له العبد ولم يقل قبلت جازت الهبة، وكذلك لو كان العبد غائباً فذهب وقبضه ولم يقل قبلت جازت الهبة . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : وبقول أبي بكر نأخذ. وفي التهذيب : ولو .

دفع ثوبين إلى رجل فقال أيما شئت لك والآخر لابنك فلان، فإن بين الذي له قبل أن يتفرقا جاز، وإن لم يبين لم يجز لأن الجهالة لم ترتفع
Book of Gift. 'And your clothing this garment and my house for you is a gift for you to dwell in, not a gift of dwelling or a dwelling gift, and acceptance.' The giving of ownership. He said Most High 'or their clothing' [5:89]. And it is said: the prince clothed so-and-so with a garment meaning he gave him ownership, not if he lent him. If he gave a man a garment and said 'dress yourself' and he did, it is a gift. If he gave him dirhams and said 'spend them' it is a loan. And if he said 'I have given you this garment for enjoyment' or 'these dirhams for enjoyment' it is a gift; thus in al-Muhit. His statement (and my house for you a gift for you to dwell in) because his saying 'for you to dwell in' is advice (mashurah) with a shadda on the shin, not an interpretation, because a verb cannot be an interpretation for a noun. He indicated to him that he may dwell in it; if he wishes he accepts his advice and if he wishes he does not, like his saying 'this food for you to eat' or 'this garment for you to wear'. It has preceded that 'umra is like a gift, so his statement here 'gift' is not a restriction; rather if he said 'my house for you as 'umra for you to dwell in' it would be similar; stated in al-Nihaya. His statement (not a gift of dwelling or a dwelling gift) with nasb of 'gift' in both as hal, and it is possible they are as tamiz because of the ambiguity in his saying 'my house for you', meaning that it is a loan in both, because dwelling is definitive in granting usufruct, so it is a loan whether he puts the word 'gift' first or last. And if he mentioned 'loan' instead of 'dwelling' it is a loan a fortiori. And if he said 'it is for you a gift-rental every month for a dirham' or 'rental-gift' it is a non-binding rental; each may rescind it after possession, and if he dwells the rent is obligatory; thus in al-Muhit. His statement (and acceptance) means the gift is valid by offer and acceptance with respect to the donee because it is a contract, so it is concluded by them like other contracts. We restricted it to being with respect to the donee because it is valid by offer alone with respect to the donor, as they mentioned in oaths: if he swore to give his slave to so-and-so and he gave but he did not accept, he is true to his oath, unlike sale. Acceptance is sometimes by speech and sometimes by action. From the second is what we have mentioned: if he said 'I have given this my slave-girl to one of you, whoever wishes let him take her' and a man among them took her, she becomes his and his taking was acceptance. And what is in al-Muhit that it indicates that acceptance is not a condition in gift is problematic. In it: a man: making (ja'l) is an expression for giving ownership; if he said 'plant it in the name of my son' it is not a gift; if he said 'I have made it for my son' it is a gift because people intend thereby giving ownership and gift. And there is contradiction with what is in al-Khulasa as is obvious. Al-Ramli said in Hashiyat al-Minhaj: what is in al-Khaniyya is closer to the custom of people. And I saw in al-Walwaljiyya its text: a man has a young son and planted a vineyard for him; this has three aspects: if he said 'I plant this vineyard in the name of my son so-and-so' or said 'I have made it for my son so-and-so as a gift' because making (ja'l) is establishing thus it is giving ownership; if he said 'I have made it in the name of my son' then the matter is uncertain and it is closer to the first aspect. And reference another copy. His statement (and what is in al-Muhit that it indicates that acceptance is not a condition in gift is problematic) the pronoun in 'it' refers to the preceding issue, and it appears to me that he meant acceptance explicitly, and that acceptance by action suffices, and upon this the disagreement about stipulating acceptance or not is carried. And success is from Allah. He said in al-Tatarkhaniyya and in al-Dhakhira: Abu Bakr said: then if a man says to another 'I have gifted my slave to you' and the slave is present, and the donee takes possession of the slave and did not say 'I accept', the gift is valid; similarly if the slave was absent and he went and took possession and did not say 'I accept', the gift is valid. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: we take by the statement of Abu Bakr. And in al-Tahdhib: and if... He gave two garments to a man and said 'whichever you wish is for you and the other is for your son so-and-so'; if he specified which one before they separate it is valid; if he did not specify it is not valid because the ambiguity is not removed.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq concerning the rulings of gift (hiba). The matn phrase "وكسوتك هذا الثوب وداري لك هبة تسكنها لا هبة سكنى أو سكنى هبة وقبول" is unpacked. First, the term 'كسوة' (clothing) is explained: the Quranic verse (5:89) uses 'كسوة' to mean giving ownership, not lending. Therefore, if someone says 'clothe yourself with this garment' it is a gift, but if he gives dirhams and says 'spend them' it is a loan. The word 'تمتع' (enjoyment) also indicates a gift. Second, regarding the phrase 'دار لك هبة تسكنها', the commentator clarifies that 'تسكنها' is a recommendation (mashurah), not a condition, so it remains a gift; it is like 'عمرى' (life grant). Third, if one says 'هبة سكنى' or 'سكنى هبة', it is actually a loan ('ariya) because the focus is on usufruct, not ownership. Similarly, if rental is mentioned, it is a non-binding lease. Finally, the matn says 'وقبول' (acceptance). The commentary explains that gift is a contract requiring offer and acceptance from the donee, but from the donor's side, the offer alone suffices for his oath. Acceptance can be verbal or by taking possession (qabd). There is a disagreement about whether verbal acceptance is necessary; the author favors the view that taking possession suffices, citing Abu Bakr al-Jassas and others. The example of two garments illustrates that ambiguity in specification invalidates the gift.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A contract of gift transferring ownership without compensation.
عارية (ariyah): A loan of an object for use without transfer of ownership.
قبول (qabul): Acceptance of the offer, which can be verbal or by taking possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, if a person says to another 'I have given you this garment, take it' and the other takes it without saying 'I accept', what is the ruling?
A) The gift is invalid because verbal acceptance is required.
B) The gift is valid because taking possession constitutes acceptance.
C) The gift is valid only if the donor repeats the offer after taking.
D) The gift is a loan, not a gift, because the word 'take' implies loan.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift is valid by offer and acceptance; acceptance by taking possession suffices, and wording that implies advice does not convert a gift into a loan.

Constructive Delivery in Gift: Permission and Possession
Commentary on delivery rules in gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when delivery (qabd) in gift is valid under Hanafi law, apply Abu Hanifa’s principle of constructive possession, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
The ability to take possession is as good as possession in gift, but explicit prohibition overrides implied permission.

This lesson unpacks a key commentary on the rules of delivery in gift. The matn states that delivery without permission is valid only in the session, while after the session permission is required. The commentator clarifies that delivery is needed for ownership to become binding—not for the contract’s validity itself. He then presents Abu Hanifa’s innovative analogy: just as takhliya (vacating) suffices in sale, the ability to take possession (tamakkun) suffices in gift. This leads to a dispute with Abu Yusuf, who insists on actual physical possession. We will also see how explicit prohibition overrides the implied permission of the session, and the practical rule regarding locked versus open boxes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the matn’s distinction
Identify the core rule: taking possession in the session is valid without explicit permission (implied permission), but after the session, explicit permission is required.
The matn phrase is 'وقبض بلا إذن في المجلس وبعده به'.
2
Recognize the function of qabd
The commentator explains that qabd is necessary for the establishment of ownership (thubut al-milk), not for the validity (sihha) of the gift contract. Understand the difference.
This means the contract is valid without delivery, but ownership does not become binding until delivery occurs.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa equates the ability to take possession (tamakkun) with actual possession, analogizing to takhliya in sale. Abu Yusuf requires actual physical possession. Note the practical difference.
Under Abu Hanifa, saying 'I have taken possession' suffices if the item is available; Abu Yusuf demands actual holding.
4
Apply the prohibition rule
If the donor explicitly forbids taking possession, that prohibition overrides the implied permission of the session, preventing valid delivery even in the session.
The commentator: 'نهي صريح يفوق الإذن الدلالي'.
5
Test with the locked box scenario
Analyze the example: if gifted clothes are in a locked box, giving the box does not constitute delivery because the donee cannot take possession. If the box is open, giving the box does constitute delivery because the donee can take the clothes easily, illustrating tamakkun.
The ruling is from al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وقبض بلا إذن في المجلس وبعده به) يعني وبعد المجلس لا بد من الإذن صريحاً فأفاد أنه لا بد من القبض فيها الثبوت الملك لا للصحة والتمكن من القبض كالقبض ولهذا قال في الاختيار ولو وهب من رجل ثوباً فقال قبضته صار قابضاً عند أبي حنيفة وجعل تمكنه من القبض كالقبض كالتخلية في البيع. وقال أبو يوسف : لا بد من القبض في يده .اهـ. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو نهاه عن القبض لا يقبضه لا في المجلس ولا بعده لأن صحة قبضه في المجلس لأجل أنه أذن به دلالة لتسليطه عليه بها فإذا نهاه كان صريحاً وهو يفوقها، ولو وهب لرجل ثياباً في صندوق مقفل ودفع إليه الصندوق لم يكن قبضاً، وإن كان الصندوق مفتوحاً كان قبضاً لأن يمكنه القبض؛ كذا في المحيط .
His statement (and possession without permission in the session and after it with permission) means after the session explicit permission is necessary. So it indicates that possession is required for the establishment of ownership, not for validity; and the ability to take possession is like possession. For this reason, it is said in al-Ikhtiyar: if a man gave a garment to another and he said 'I have taken possession', he becomes a possessor according to Abu Hanifa, and he considers the ability to take possession as possession, like takhliya in sale. Abu Yusuf said: possession in his hand is necessary. And the author indicated that if he forbids him from taking possession, he should not take possession neither in the session nor after, because the validity of his taking possession in the session is due to implied permission by giving him authority over it; if he explicitly forbids, that overrides. And if he gifted clothes in a locked box to a man and gave him the box, it is not possession; if the box is open, it is possession because he can take possession; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase about delivery (qabd) in gift. The matn states that taking possession without explicit permission is valid in the session of the contract (majlis), but after the session, permission is required. The commentator explains that delivery is necessary for ownership to become established (not for the validity of the contract itself). Abu Hanifa holds that the ability to take possession (tamakkun) is equivalent to actual possession, akin to takhliya (vacating) in sale. Abu Yusuf disagrees, requiring actual physical possession. If the donor explicitly forbids taking possession, that prohibition overrides the implied permission of the session. An example: if clothes are in a locked box and the donor gives the box, possession is not achieved; if the box is open, it is possession because the donee can easily take the clothes.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Delivery or taking possession; in gift, it is a condition for ownership to become binding.
تخلية (takhliya): Vacating or enabling possession without physical handover; considered delivery in sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the ability to take possession considered effective in gift, similar to takhliya in sale?
A) Because gift and sale are both binding contracts.
B) Because ownership in gift requires actual holding.
C) Because enabling possession achieves the purpose of transfer.
D) Because of a direct Quranic command.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the ability to take possession suffices for delivery in gift, and permission is implied in the session but required explicitly thereafter.

Gift of Undivided Shares and Partitionability
Commentary on matn regarding gift of divided and undivided property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on gift of undivided property (musha') and distinguish it from other contracts regarding partitionability.

Core Idea
Gift of undivided property is invalid if partitionable; sale is valid regardless.

Gift contracts in Hanafi fiqh are strict: the gifted item must be delivered and separately possessed. This lesson unpacks the commentary on the matn regarding gift of undivided shares (musha'), explaining when such a gift is valid and how it differs from sale, lease, loan, pledge, and endowment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly drafting Islamic property transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Ruling
State that a gift of undivided property (musha') is valid only if the property is not partitionable (e.g., a shared road). If partitionable, the gift is invalid.
The matn restricts validity to 'mahu'z maqsum' (possessed divided) and 'musha' la yuqsam' (undivided non-partitionable).
2
Understand Key Terms
Define 'mahu'z' (possessed) and 'musha'' (undivided). Note that attached fruit (like on a tree) is not considered 'mahu'z'.
Possession is a condition for gift; attached items are not independently possessed.
3
Compare Gift with Sale
Unlike gift, sale of undivided property is valid whether partitionable or not. This is because sale tolerates uncertainty (jahala) in price and object more than gift.
The text explicitly states: 'sale of undivided is valid whether it is partitionable or not'.
4
Examine Other Contracts
Lease from a stranger is invalid (fasid) unless from a partner. Loan ('ariyah) from a partner is valid; otherwise only if the whole property is handed over. Pledge (rahn) is corrupt (fasid) whether from partner or stranger. Waqf is valid if the undivided property is non-partitionable, with intra-Hanafi disagreement on partitionable ones.
Each contract has its own rule: gift is the strictest regarding undivided shares.
5
Note Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf allows waqf of partitionable undivided property; Muhammad disallows. Also, regarding lease from a stranger, there is disagreement whether it is fasid (corrupt) or batil (void). The text prefers 'fasid' on the correct view.
The mufta bihi on waqf is the view of Abu Yusuf according to later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (في محوز مقسوم ومشاع لا يقسم أي تجوز الهبة فيما ذكر. قيد بالمحوز لأن المتصل كالثمرة على الشجر لا تجوز هبته، وقيد المشاع بما لم يقسم لأن هبة المشاع الذي يقسم لا تجوز وأطلقها فشمل الهبة من الشريك مشاعاً يقسم. قيد بالهبة لأن بيع الشائع جائز فيما يقسم وما لا يقسم وأما إجارته فإن كان من الشريك فهو جائز، وإن من أجنبي لا يجوز مطلقاً عند أ أبي حنيفة، وهي فاسدة على قوله فيجب أجر المثل على الأصح خلافاً لمن قال ببطلانها فلم يوجب شيئاً . وأما الشيوع الطارئ ففي ظاهر الرواية لا يفسد الإجارة، وأما إعارته فجائزة إن كانت من شريكه وإلا فإن سلم الكل فهي إعارة مستأنفة للكل وإلا لا يجبر . وأما رهنه فهو فاسد فيما ينقسم أولاً من شريكه أو من أجنبي بخلاف الرهن من اثنين فإنه جائز، وأما وقفه فهو جائز عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد فيما يحتملها، وإن كان مما لا يقسم .
His statement (in possessed divided [property] and undivided [property] that is not partitionable) means the gift is valid in what is mentioned. It is restricted to possessed because something attached like fruit on a tree, its gift is not valid. And it is restricted to undivided that is not partitionable because the gift of undivided that is partitionable is not valid; and he left it unrestricted so it includes the gift from a partner of an undivided share that is partitionable. It is restricted to gift because the sale of undivided is valid whether it is partitionable or not. As for its lease, if it is from the partner it is valid; if from a stranger, it is not valid absolutely according to Abu Hanifa, and it is corrupt according to his statement, so the equivalent rent is obligatory on the correct view, contrary to those who say it is void and therefore nothing is obligatory. As for supervening common ownership, in the apparent narration it does not invalidate the lease. As for its loan, it is valid if it is from his partner; otherwise, if he delivers the whole, it is a new loan for the whole, otherwise he is not compelled. As for its pledge, it is corrupt whether it is partitionable or not, whether from his partner or a stranger, unlike the pledge from two persons, it is valid. As for its endowment, it is valid according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad in property that is partitionable, and if it is not partitionable.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn phrase regarding when a gift of property is valid. The matn identifies three categories: possessed divided property (mahu'z maqsum), undivided property that cannot be partitioned (musha' la yuqsam), and by implication, undivided property that can be partitioned. The commentator explains that gifts are valid only in the first two categories; a gift of undivided property that is partitionable is invalid. Then the passage compares gift with other contracts: sale of undivided property is valid regardless of partitionability; lease from a stranger is invalid unless it is from a partner; loan ('ariyah) from a partner is valid; pledge (rahn) is corrupt; endowment (waqf) is valid according to Abu Yusuf in partitionable property, but Muhammad disagrees. The passage highlights the unique strictness of gift contracts regarding undivided shares.

Key Terms
مشاع (musha'): Undivided share in jointly owned property.
محوز (mahu'z): Property that is possessed or physically separated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift of an undivided share in a house that is partitionable is:
A) Valid because the donee can later partition.
B) Invalid because gift of partitionable undivided property is not allowed.
C) Valid only if the donor is also a partner.
D) Valid only if it is a gift of usufruct, not ownership.

Key Takeaway
A gift of undivided property is valid only if the property is not partitionable; sale is valid regardless, and other contracts have their own specific rules.

Possession (Qabd) in Hiba: Constructive Possession and the Ability to Possess
Explanation of the condition of possession and the principle of ability to possess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on constructive possession (tamakkun) in gift contracts to determine the validity of possession in various scenarios.

Core Idea
Ability to possess equals actual possession in Hanafi hiba, via qiyas to takhliya in sale.

Possession (qabd) is a key condition for the completion of a gift in Hanafi fiqh. This lesson explores the ruling that constructive possession suffices when the donee is able to take physical control, and examines the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this principle helps avoid disputes over whether a gift has been properly completed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on possession in hiba
Locate the statement that possession is required for the establishment of ownership (thubut al-milk), not for validity (sihha). Note the distinction between possession in the session (majlis) and after the session.
The matn states: 'Wa qabd bila idhn fi al-majlis wa ba‘dahu bih' – possession without permission in the session and after it with permission.
2
Understand the principle of tamakkun
The passage states that ability to take possession (tamakkun min al-qabd) is treated as actual possession. This is the key innovation: the donee's capability to physically take the item suffices for valid qabd.
Example: saying 'I have taken possession' while being able to do so is sufficient according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Trace the qiyas to takhliya in sale
The ruling is derived by analogy to sale: in a sale, the seller relinquishing control (takhliya) suffices for delivery. Similarly, in hiba, ability to take possession is equivalent to actual possession.
The illa (effective cause) is the relinquishment of control enabling the other party to take physical possession.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note that Abu Yusuf requires actual physical possession in the hand, while Abu Hanifa (and Muhammad al-Shaybani in some versions) consider tamakkun sufficient. The reliance is on Abu Hanifa's view as per al-Ikhtiyar.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
5
Apply the ruling to examples
The passage applies the principle to two cases: (a) if the donor explicitly forbids possession, qabd is invalid even in the session; (b) if a chest is locked, possession is not valid because the donee cannot take it; if open, it is valid.
These examples illustrate the importance of actual ability to possess.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة وقبض في المجلس بلا إذنه وبعده به في محوز مقسوم ومشاع لا يقسم لا فيما يقسم دفع ثوبين إلى رجل فقال أيما شئت لك والآخر لابنك فلان، فإن بين الذي له قبل أن يتفرقا جاز، وإن لم يبين لم يجز لأن الجهالة لم ترتفع قوله : (وقبض بلا إذن في المجلس وبعده به) يعني وبعد المجلس لا بد من الإذن صريحاً فأفاد أنه لا بد من القبض فيها ثبوت الملك لا للصحة والتمكن من القبض كالقبض ولهذا قال في الاختيار ولو وهب من رجل ثوباً فقال قبضته صار قابضاً عند أبي حنيفة وجعل تمكنه من القبض كالقبض كالتخلية في البيع. وقال أبو يوسف : لا بد من القبض في يده .اهـ. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو نهاه عن القبض لا قبضه لا في المجلس ولا بعده لأن صحة قبضه في المجلس لأجل أنه أذن به دلالة لتسليطه عليه بها فإذا نهاه كان صريحاً وهو يفوقها، ولو وهب لرجل ثياباً في صندوق مقفل ودفع إليه الصندوق لم يكن قبضاً، وإن كان الصندوق مفتوحاً كان قبضاً لأن يمكنه القبض؛ كذا في المحيط. قسمته لا يصح
Book of Gift. And possession in the session without his permission and after it with it. In a possessed [property] that is divided, and a share that is undivided in what is not divisible, not in what is divisible. He gave two garments to a man and said, 'Whichever you want is for you, and the other is for my son so-and-so.' If he specifies which one [belongs to whom] before they separate, it is valid. If he does not specify, it is not valid because the ignorance is not removed. His statement: 'And possession without permission in the session and after it with permission' means after the session explicit permission is necessary. So it indicates that possession is necessary for the establishment of ownership, not for validity, and the ability to possess is like possession. For this reason, he said in al-Ikhtiyar: 'If one gives a garment to a man and he says, "I have taken possession," he becomes a possessor according to Abu Hanifa, and he considered his ability to possess as possessory delivery, like takhliya in sale. Abu Yusuf said: Actual possession in his hand is necessary.' End. And the author indicated that if he forbids him from possession, his possession is not valid, neither in the session nor after it, because the validity of his possession in the session is due to his implied permission by giving him control; so when he forbids, it is explicit and overrides it. And if one gives garments to a man in a locked chest and hands over the chest, it is not possession. If the chest was open, it is possession because he can take possession. Thus in al-Muhit. Its division is not valid.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi rulings on possession (qabd) in gift (hiba). It begins with a matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq stating that possession in the same session (majlis) is valid without explicit permission, but after the session explicit permission is required. The author then clarifies the case of giving two garments to a man with a choice; the gift is valid only if the donor specifies which goes to whom before they separate; otherwise, ignorance (jahala) prevents validity. The commentary then details the principle that ability to take possession (tamakkun min al-qabd) is equivalent to actual possession in gift contracts, relying on qiyas to takhliya in sale. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that if the donee says 'I took possession' while able to do so, it suffices; Abu Yusuf requires actual physical possession. The text also rules that if the donor explicitly forbids possession, the donee cannot take possession even in the session, as explicit prohibition overrides implied permission. Finally, the example of a locked vs. open chest illustrates tamakkun: if the chest is open, the ability to take possession suffices; if locked, it does not. The line 'its division is not valid' likely refers to the necessity of proper possession for hiba to be complete.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking delivery of the gifted item; a condition for completion of hiba.
تمكن من القبض (tamakkun min al-qabd): Ability to take physical possession; treated as actual possession in hiba.
هبة (hiba): Gift; a contract that transfers ownership without compensation, requiring offer, acceptance, and possession.
تخلية (takhliya): Relinquishment of control; in sale, it suffices for delivery of possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a donor gives a garment to a donee and the donee says 'I have taken possession' while able to physically take it, is the possession valid?
A) Yes, because ability to possess suffices.
B) No, because actual physical possession is required.
C) Yes, but only if the donor explicitly permits.
D) No, because saying so does not constitute possession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi hiba, constructive possession (ability to take control) is sufficient for completion, following qiyas to sale and istihsan.
