Validity of Gifting an Undivided Share (Hiba al-Mushā')
Explaining validity conditions for gifting an undivided share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a valid gift of an undivided share (mushā') in Hanafi fiqh and apply the criterion for divisibility to determine whether division is required.

Core Idea
Gift of an undivided share is valid only if the property is indivisible or, if divisible, after division and delivery.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on gifting an undivided share (hiba al-mushā'). The classical text distinguishes between divisible and indivisible property, establishing a clear criterion based on judicial compellability. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the conditions of validity and avoiding invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The matn states: if the donor divides and delivers, the gift of mushā' is valid. The commentary clarifies that this applies to divisible property. For indivisible property, the gift is valid even without division, provided the share is known.
Start by recognizing that the ruling depends on the nature of the property.
2
Apply the divisibility criterion (al-ḥadd al-fāṣil)
The text gives the criterion: if a judge can compel the partner to division, the property is divisible (e.g., a large house); if not, it is indivisible (e.g., a slave, a small room). Determine the category of the property in question.
Use the judge's power as the objective measure, not size or movability.
3
Check the conditions for the property category
For divisible property: division and delivery must occur before possession. For indivisible property: the share must be a known quantity (no ignorance leading to dispute). If these conditions are met, the gift is valid (sahih); otherwise it is invalid (fasid).
The condition for indivisible property is knowledge of the share, not division.
4
Evaluate the effect of shuyū' (undividedness)
The author distinguishes between shuyū' concurrent with the contract (which prevents validity) and supervening shuyū' (e.g., donor takes back part undivided, which does not invalidate). In pledge, supervening shuyū' does invalidate, but not in gift.
Focus on the timing: concurrent vs. supervening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة
فإن قسمه وسلمه صح وإن وهب دقيقاً في بر لا وإن طحن وسلم وكذا الدهن في :
يحتملها فجائز اتفاقاً، وأفتى الكثير بقول محمد، واختار مشايخ بلخ قول أبي يوسف. وأما وديعته فجائزة وتكون مع الشريك، وأما قرضه فجائز كما إذا دفع إليه ألفاً وقال خمسمائة قرضاً وخمسمائة شركة ؛ كذا في النهاية هنا. وأما غصبه فمتصوّر قال البزازي : وعليه الفتوى وذكر له في الفصول صوراً. وأما صدقته فكهبته إلا إذا تصدق بالكل على اثنين فإنه يجوز على الأصح. وإذا عرف هذا فهبة المشاع فيما لا ينقسم تفيد الملك للموهوب له على وجه لا يستحق المطالبة بالقسمة لأنها لا تمكن وأما المهايأة فلا تجب في ظاهر الرواية لأنها إعارة فإن كل واحد منهما يصير معيراً نصيبه من صاحبه والجبر على الإعارة غير مشروع، وفي رواية تجب. ثم الحد الفاصل بين ما يحتمل القسمة وما لا يحتملها أن كل ما كان مشتركاً بين اثنين فطلب أحدهما القسمة وأبى الآخر فإن كان للقاضي أن يجبر الآبي على القسمة فهو مما يحتملها كالدار والبيت الكبير، وإن كان مما لا يجبره فهو مما لا يحتملها كالعبد والحمام والبيت الصغير والحائط. ويشترط في صحة هبة المشاع الذي لا يحتملها أن يكون قدراً معلوماً حتى لو وهب نصيبه من عبد ولم يعلمه به لم يجز لأنها جهالة توجب المنازعة، ومما لا يحتملها الدرهم الصحيح حتى لو وهب درهماً صحيحاً لرجلين صح. ولو كان معه در همان فقال لرجل وهبت لك درهماً منهما، فإن كانا مستويين لم تجز الهبة إلا أن يفرز أحدهما، وإن كانا مختلفين يجوز لعدم احتمالهما، فأما في المقطعة فلا تجوز إلا بالإفراز ولو كان عبد بين رجلين فوهب أحدهما لهذا العبد شيئاً، فإن كان يحتمل القسمة لا تصح أصلاً، وإن كان لا يحتملها صحت في نصيب صاحبه. ولو وهب أحد الشريكين حصته من المربح لآخر، فإن كان المال لم يصح لاحتماله القسم، وإن كان مستهلكاً صح لأن الدين لا يحتملها؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي الصحاح : يقال سهم شائع أي غير مقسوم وأراد المصنف بالشيوع المانع الشيوع المقارن للعقد لا الطارئ كأن يرجع الواهب في بعض الهبة شائعاً فإنه لا يفسدها، أما الاستحقاق فيفسد الكل لأنه مقارن لا طارئ. قيدنا بالهبة لأن الرهن يبطله الشيوع الطارئ كالمقارن؛ كذا في النهاية قوله : (فإن قسمه وسلمه صح) أي لو وهب مشاعاً يقسم ثم قسمه وسلمه صح وملكه لأن التمام بالقبض وعنده لا شيوع فأفاد أنه لو قبضه مشاعاً لا يملكه فلا ينفذ تصرفه فيه لأنها هبة فاسدة مآلاً وهي مضمونة بالقبض ولا تفيد الملك للموهوب له وهو المختار فلو باعه الموهوب له لا يصح ؛ ؛ كذا في المبتغى بالمعجمة. وأفاد أنه لو دفع درهمين إلى رجل وقال أحدهما هبة لك والآخر أمانة عندك فهلكا جميعاً يضمن درهم الهبة وهو في الآخر أمين ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار من رجل ولم يسلم ثم وهب النصف الباقي لذلك الرجل فسلم جميع الدار منه جملة يجوز، وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار لرجل فسلم ثم وهب النصف الباقي لذلك الرجل فسلم فكلا العقدين فاسد كما صرح به الأسبيجابي. وبما ذكره هنا علم أن قوله تصح في محوز مقسوم معناه أنها تملك بهذه الشروط لا أن الصحة متوقفة على القسمة لأنه لو وهب شائعاً يقسم تصح من غير ملك ولهذا لو قبضه مقسوماً ملكه ولو كان شرطاً للصحة لاحتيج إلى تجديد العقد كما لا يخفى.
Book of Gift. If he divides it and delivers it, it is valid. And if he gives flour in wheat, it is not valid unless he grinds it and delivers; similarly oil in sesame. [If it can bear division] it is valid by agreement, and many gave fatwa according to Muhammad, and the scholars of Balkh chose the view of Abu Yusuf. As for his deposit, it is valid and remains with the partner. As for his loan, it is valid, like when he gives him a thousand and says five hundred as loan and five hundred as partnership. As for his usurpation, it is conceivable; al-Bazzazi said: the fatwa is upon it, and he mentioned images in al-Fusul. As for his charity, it is like his gift, except if he gives all to two, then it is valid according to the soundest view. When this is known, the gift of an undivided share (musha') in what cannot be divided confers ownership to the donee in such a way that he does not deserve to demand division because it is not possible. As for division (muhaya'a), it is not obligatory in the apparent narration because it is a loan, as each of them becomes a lender of his share to the other, and compulsion in lending is not legislated; and in one narration it is obligatory. Then the dividing line between what can bear division and what cannot is that everything shared between two, if one requests division and the other refuses, if the judge can compel the refuser to division, it is of those that can bear division, like a large house or big room; and if he cannot compel him, it is of those that cannot bear division, like a slave, a pigeon, a small room, a wall. It is a condition for the validity of the gift of an undivided share that cannot bear division that it be a known quantity; such that if he gives his share of a slave without informing him, it is not valid because ignorance leads to dispute. Among what cannot bear division is a single dirham; so that if he gives a single dirham to two men, it is valid. And if he has two dirhams and says to a man 'I gave you one dirham of them', if they are equal, the gift is not valid unless he separates one; if they are different, it is valid because they cannot bear division. As for cut dirhams, it is not valid except by separation. And if there was a slave between two men, and one of them gives something to this slave, if it can bear division, it is not valid at all; if it cannot bear it, it is valid in the share of his partner. And if one of the two partners gives his share of profit (murabah) to another, if the capital exists, it is not valid because it can bear division; if it is consumed, it is valid because a debt cannot bear it. In al-Muhit. In al-Sihah: it is said sahm sha'i' i.e., undivided, and the author intended by preventing shuyu' the shuyu' concurrent with the contract, not the supervening one; such as if the donor takes back part of the gift undivided, it does not invalidate it. As for the case of entitlement (istihqaq), it invalidates the whole because it is concurrent, not supervening. We restricted it to gift because in pledge, supervening shuyu' invalidates like concurrent. In al-Nihayah. His statement: (If he divides it and delivers it, it is valid) i.e., if he gives an undivided share that can be divided, then divides it and delivers it, it is valid and he owns it because completion is with possession, and at that time there is no shuyu'. So it indicates that if he takes possession of it undivided, he does not own it, thus his disposal in it is not effective because it is a gift that is invalid in outcome, and it is guaranteed with possession, and it does not confer ownership to the donee, and this is the chosen view; so if the donee sells it, it is not valid. This is in al-Mubtagha. And it indicates that if he gives two dirhams to a man and says 'one is a gift to you and the other is a trust with you', and both are lost, he guarantees the gift dirham and is a trustee for the other. In Fatawa Qadikhan. And it indicates that if he gives half the house to a man without delivery, then gives the remaining half to that same man and delivers the entire house together, it is valid. And it indicates that if he gives half the house to a man and delivers, then gives the remaining half to that man and delivers, both contracts are invalid, as al-Asbijabi stated. From what he mentioned here, it is known that his statement 'it is valid in possessed divided' means that ownership occurs with these conditions, not that validity depends on division, because if he gives an undivided share that can be divided, it is valid without ownership; therefore, if he takes possession of it divided, he owns it; if division were a condition for validity, renewal of the contract would be necessary, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 487

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq opens with the ruling on gifting an undivided share (musha'). The matn states that if the donor divides the property and delivers it, the gift is valid. The commentary clarifies that this applies to property that is divisible. For indivisible property, the gift of an undivided share is valid even without division, provided the donee knows the share. The author then establishes the criterion for divisibility: if a judge can compel the partner to divide, it is divisible; otherwise not. Examples: a large house vs. a slave or small room. The condition for validity of gifting an undivided share in indivisible property is that the share must be known. The commentary also distinguishes between shuyu' concurrent with the contract (which prevents validity) and supervening shuyu' (which does not). The passage includes views of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the issue of gifting flour in wheat, but the main focus is on hiba of musha'. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the gift of an undivided share in divisible property must be divided and delivered to be valid, otherwise it is fasid.

Key Terms
هبة المشاع (hiba al-mushā'): Gift of an undivided share in a joint property.
الشيوع (al-shuyū'): Undivided share; the state of common ownership without physical division.
الحد الفاصل (al-ḥadd al-fāṣil): The criterion distinguishing divisible from indivisible property based on whether a judge can compel division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a necessary condition for the validity of a gift of an undivided share in one of the two partners' share of a large house?
A) The donee must know the exact area of the house
B) The donor must physically divide the house and deliver the specific portion
C) The judge must approve the gift
D) The gift must be registered with the authorities

Key Takeaway
The validity of a gift of an undivided share in Hanafi fiqh hinges on the property's divisibility: divisible property requires division and delivery, while indivisible property only requires the share to be known.

Ruling on Hiba of Non-Existent Objects (Ma'dum)
Ruling and exceptions for gifting non-existent objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on gifting non-existent objects and identify which items are treated as exceptions akin to musha'.

Core Idea
A gift of something non-existent is void and not cured by later delivery; exceptions are items considered like undivided shares.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq about gifting non-existent objects. It establishes the general invalidity of such gifts, then lists several exceptions where the item is treated as a musha' (undivided share) and the gift becomes valid upon separation and delivery. Understanding this distinction is key to correctly applying the rules of hiba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn states that gifting flour in wheat is not valid unless ground and delivered. The commentary explains that the gift of a non-existent thing (ma'dum) is void (batil) and does not become valid by later delivery.
Examples: oil in sesame, fat in milk, pregnancy of a slave-girl.
2
Recognize the exceptions
Certain items like milk in the udder, wool on sheep, crops in the ground, dates on palms, furniture in a house, flour in a sack, saddle/bridle on a riding animal, jewelry on a slave-girl, load on an animal, water in a ewer are treated as musha' and their gift is valid if separated and delivered.
The key is that these items exist separately and are only attached; they are not truly non-existent.
3
Understand the condition of possession (qabd)
For the gift to be valid, possession must be taken after separation. Permission for possession given before separation is not valid. The gifted object must be divided and separated at the time of possession, not at the time of the contract.
Shuyu' (undivided share) at the time of possession prevents validity, but at contract it does not.
4
Apply the principle of occupancy
If the gifted object is occupied by the donor's property (e.g., a saddle on a horse given without the saddle), the gift is invalid because possession is obstructed. Conversely, if the donor's property is occupied by the gifted object (e.g., giving a horse without its saddle), it is valid.
This is based on Fatawa al-Qadi Zahir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهبة
قوله : (وإن وهب دقيقاً في بر لا وإن طحن وسلم) أي لا يصح الهبة وأشار به إلى أن هبة المعدوم تقع باطلة فلا تعود صحيحة بالتسليم فدخل فيه ما لو وهب دهناً في سمسم أو سمنا في لبن أو حمل جارية، وخرج عنه اللبن في الضرع، والصوف على ظهر الغنم، والزرع والنخل في الأرض، والتمر في النخل، والدار التي فيها متاع الواهب، والجولق الذي فيه الدقيق، أو السرج أو اللجام دون الدابة، أو حلي الجارية دونها، أو دابة وله عليها حمل، أو قمقمة فيها ماء دونه، فإنه كالمشاع يصح، ويملك إذا فصله وسلمه. ويعتبر الإذن بالقبض بعد الفراغ ولا يعتد بالإذن قبله كما يعتد بالتسليم قبله بخلاف ما لو وهب المتاع الذي في الدار وسلمها معه، أو الدقيق في الجوالق ،وسلمها أو دابة مسرجة ملجمة دونهما، أو جارية عليها حلي دونه أو حملاً على دابة دونها وسلمهما، أو ماء في قمقمة دونها، أو دارها ولها فيها أمتعة وهو ساكن فيها حيث يجوز . وإن وهب داراً فيها متاع وسلمها كذلك ثم وهب المتاع منه أيضاً جازت في المتاع خاصة، وإن بدأ فوهب له المتاع وقبض الدار والمتاع ثم وهب الدار جازت الهبة فيهما لأنه حيث هبة الدار لم يكن للواهب فيها شيء وحين هبة
قوله : (وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار الخ) قال في جامع الفصولين برمز فتاوى القاضي ظهير ويشترط كون الموهوب مقسوماً مفرزاً وقت القبض لا وقت الهبة حتى لو وهب نصف دار شائعاً ولم يسلم حتى وهب النصف الآخر وسلم الكل جاز اهـ. ثم رمز الخواهر زاده : الشيوع حالة القبض يمنع الهبة وحالة العقد لا يمنع والتخلية في الهبة الصحيح قبض لا في الفاسدة اهـ قوله : (بخلاف ما لو وهب المتاع الخ (فقط) أي فتاوى القاضي ظهير جاز هبة الشاغل لا المشغول، والأصل أن اشتغال الموهوب بملك الواهب يمنع تمام الهبة إذ القبض شرط أما اشتغال ملك الواهب بالموهوب فلا يمنعه. (ت) أي الزيادات وهبه دابة مسرجة بدون سرجها ولجامها وسامها كذلك لم يجز لاشتغالها بهما، وجاز عكسه لعدم اشتغالهما بها وعلى هذا الرهن قال صاحب جامع الفصولين :
The Gift. His statement: (And if he gives flour in wheat, it is not valid unless he grinds it and delivers) i.e., the gift is not valid. He indicated by it that the gift of a non-existent thing occurs void and does not become valid by delivery. Included therein is if he gives oil in sesame, or fat in milk, or the pregnancy of a slave-girl. Excluded from it is milk in the udder, wool on the back of sheep, crops and palm-trees in the ground, dates on palm-trees, the house in which is the donor's furniture, the sack in which is flour, the saddle or bridle without the riding animal, the jewelry of a slave-girl without her, a riding animal with a load upon it, a ewer with water without it; for that is like an undivided share, valid, and he owns it when he separates and delivers it. Permission for possession is considered after completion, and permission before it is not considered, just as delivery before it is considered. Contrary to if he gives the furniture in the house and delivers the house with it, the flour in the sack and delivers it, a saddled and bridled riding animal without them, a slave-girl with her jewelry without it, a load on a riding animal without her, and delivers them, water in a ewer without it, a house and she has furniture in it and he is living in it, where it is valid. And if he gives a house with furniture and delivers it likewise, then gives the furniture from it also, it is valid specifically in the furniture. And if he began by giving him the furniture and he took possession of the house and furniture, then gave the house, the gift in both is valid because at the time of the gift of the house, the donor had nothing in it. His statement: (And it indicates that if he gives half the house...) He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn with the symbol of Fatawa al-Qadi Zahir: It is necessary that the gifted object be divided and separated at the time of possession, not at the time of the gift; so that if he gives half a house undivided and does not deliver until he gives the other half and delivers all, it is valid. Then the symbol of Khawahirzadeh: Shuyu' at the time of possession prevents the gift, but at the time of contract does not prevent. Khalwah (vacant possession) in a valid gift is possession, not in an invalid one. His statement: (Contrary to if he gives the furniture...) i.e., Fatawa al-Qadi Zahir says: The gift of the container is valid, not the content. The principle is that the occupancy of the gifted object with the donor's property prevents completion of the gift because possession is a condition, while the occupancy of the donor's property with the gifted object does not prevent it. (T) i.e., al-Ziyadat: If he gives a saddled riding animal without her saddle and bridle and delivers it likewise, it is not valid due to its occupancy with them; the opposite is valid due to the lack of occupancy. And upon this is the pledge. The author of Jami' al-Fusulayn said:
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the ruling on gifting something that does not exist at the time of the contract (hiba al-ma'dum). The matn states that if one gives flour in wheat, it is not valid unless he grinds it and delivers. The commentary explains that the gift of a non-existent thing is absolutely void and cannot become valid by later delivery. However, things like milk in the udder, wool on sheep, etc., are considered akin to musha' (undivided share) and thus the gift is valid if separated and delivered. The distinction is based on whether the gifted object is considered a separate entity or part of a larger whole. The commentary also discusses rules about possession (qabd) and permission (idhn). The key principle is that the gifted object must be separable and possessed after separation. The passage includes views from various authorities (al-Qadi Zahir, Khawahirzadeh) emphasizing that shuyu' at the time of possession prevents validity. The relied-upon fatwa is that gifts of undivided shares in divisible property are valid only if possession is taken after division.

Key Terms
هبة المعدوم (hiba al-ma'dūm): Gift of something that does not exist at the time of the contract, e.g., flour in wheat, oil in sesame.
المشاع (al-mushā'): Here: an undivided share or something attached that is treated like a share, e.g., milk in udder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered like a musha' and thus its gift is valid if separated and delivered?
A) Flour in wheat
B) Oil in sesame
C) Milk in the udder
D) A load on a riding animal

Key Takeaway
The gift of a non-existent object is void; items treated as musha' are exceptions if separated and delivered.

The Defective Gift (Hiba Fasida): Liability Without Ownership
States the ruling on defective gift and its effects on possession and ownership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a defective gift (hiba fasida) and explain its effects on ownership and liability.

Core Idea
A defective gift creates liability but not ownership until the donee takes possession after division.

A gift that is valid in form but defective in a condition — such as being of an undivided share before possession — is called hiba fasida. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the validity of the contract and the transfer of ownership. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the consequences of such a gift and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act: gifting an undivided share. The ruling is that the gift is fasid (defective), not batil (void).
Fasid means valid in essence but deficient in a condition.
2
Distinguish validity from ownership
Note that a defective gift is valid (sahih) in contract but does not transfer ownership (milk). The donee becomes liable (daman) upon possession.
The phrase 'madhmunah bil-qabd' means the item is guaranteed by possession.
3
Analyze the three furu' (applications)
Examine the examples: (1) two dirhams – one gift, one trust; (2) giving half without surrender, then whole at once; (3) giving half and surrendering, then the other half separately. Each illustrates the effect of possession and division.
The third example: both contracts are fasid because after surrendering the first half, donor no longer has full possession.
4
Understand the condition of qabd and division
The passage clarifies that 'valid in a possessed divided thing' means ownership is achieved by these conditions; validity does not require division at the time of contract.
If the gift is of a divisible share, it is valid without ownership until possession after division.
5
Clarify the matn interpretation
The phrase 'sahih fi mahuzaq maqsum' is interpreted: ownership (not validity) depends on the item being possessed and divided.
No need to renew the contract upon division; the original contract still stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينفذ تصرفه فيه لأنها هبة فاسدة مآلاً وهي مضمونة بالقبض ولا تفيد الملك للموهوب له وهو المختار فلو باعه الموهوب له لا يصح ؛ كذا في المبتغى بالمعجمة. وأفاد أنه لو دفع درهمين إلى رجل وقال أحدهما هبة لك والآخر أمانة عندك فهلكا جميعاً يضمن درهم الهبة وهو في الآخر أمين ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار من رجل ولم يسلم ثم وهب النصف الباقي لذلك الرجل فسلم جميع الدار منه جملة يجوز، وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار لرجل فسلم ثم وهب النصف الباقي لذلك الرجل فسلم فكلا العقدين فاسد كما صرح به الأسبيجابي. وبما ذكره هنا علم أن قوله تصح في محوز مقسوم معناه أنها تملك بهذه الشروط لا أن الصحة متوقفة على القسمة لأنه لو وهب شائعاً يقسم تصح من غير ملك ولهذا لو قبضه مقسوماً ملكه ولو كان شرطاً للصحة لاحتيج إلى تجديد العقد كما لا يخفى.
His disposal in it is effective because it is a defective gift in outcome, and it is guaranteed by possession and does not confer ownership to the donee, and that is the chosen view. So if the donee sells it, it is not valid. Likewise in al-Mubtagha with the dotted letter. And he stated that if he gave two dirhams to a man and said, 'One of them is a gift to you and the other is a trust with you,' and both were destroyed, he guarantees the dirham of the gift, and he is a trustee regarding the other. Likewise in Fatawa Qadikhan. And he stated that if he gave half the house to a man and did not surrender, then gave the remaining half to that man and surrendered the whole house from him at once, it is permissible. And he stated that if he gave half the house to a man and surrendered, then gave the remaining half to that man and surrendered, both contracts are defective, as al-Asbijabi stated. And from what he mentioned here, it is understood that his statement, 'It is valid in a possessed divided thing,' means that it is owned by these conditions, not that validity depends on division, because if he gave an undivided share that is divisible, it is valid without ownership, and therefore if he receives it divided, he owns it. If it were a condition for validity, renewal of the contract would be needed, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the defective gift (hiba fasida). It first states that the donor's disposal in the gifted item is effective because the gift is defective in outcome (fasida ma'alan) meaning it is valid but deficient in some condition, such as being given before division. Such a gift is guaranteed (madhmun) by possession, meaning the donee is liable for it if destroyed, but it does not transfer ownership to the donee. This is the chosen view (mukhtar). Consequently, if the donee sells the item, the sale is invalid because he does not own it. The passage then gives three applications from Fatawa Qadikhan and Al-Mubtagha: (1) If someone gives two dirhams to a man, saying one is a gift and the other a trust, and both are destroyed, the donee guarantees the gift dirham (is liable for it) but is a trustee (amin) for the other, so no liability. (2) If one gives half a house without surrendering (taslim), then later gives the remaining half and surrenders the whole house at once, the gift is valid. (3) However, if he gives half and surrenders it, then gives the other half and surrenders it separately, both gifts are defective (fasid) according to al-Asbijabi. The reason is that after surrendering the first half, the donor no longer has possession of the whole, so the second gift is of a part without the donor having full possession. Finally, the passage clarifies the statement from the matn that a gift is valid in a possessed divided thing (mahuzaq maqsum). This means that valid ownership is achieved with these conditions; it does not mean validity is contingent upon the item being divided at the time of contract. If a person gives an undivided share that is divisible, the gift is valid but does not transfer ownership until the donee takes possession after division. This is because the condition for ownership is possession after division, not at the contract. Thus, no renewal of the contract is needed.

Key Terms
هبة فاسدة (hiba fasida): A gift that is valid in form but defective in some condition, such as being of an undivided share before possession.
مضمونة بالقبض (madhmunah bil-qabd): The gifted item is guaranteed by the donee upon possession, meaning the donee is liable for its value if destroyed.
محوز مقسوم (mahuzaq maqsum): An item that is both possessed by the donor and divided (separated) from other property, making it eligible for gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a defective gift (hiba fasida), what is the effect on ownership when the donee takes possession?
A) The donee becomes full owner immediately.
B) The donee becomes liable for the item but does not own it.
C) The gift becomes void and the item returns to the donor.
D) The donee owns it but cannot sell it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, possession of a defective gift creates liability but not ownership; the gift is valid in form but ownership is suspended until the item is divided and possession is taken after division.

Gift of Non-Existent Items and Customary Exceptions
Explains the rule and lists exceptions for gifts of non-existent items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between void gifts of non-existent items and valid gifts of items considered existent by custom in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A gift is void if the item does not exist; but custom treats some items as existing.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that a gift of a non-existent item is void (batil) and cannot be cured by later delivery. However, Hanafi jurisprudence recognizes that custom ('urf) renders certain items – like milk in the udder or wool on a sheep – as legally existent, allowing their gift. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the rules of gift contracts correctly in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the opening statement: gift of non-existent items (like oil in seeds) is void and does not become valid by delivery after the fact.
The rule is strict: existence at contract is a condition for validity.
2
Recognize the exceptions
List the items explicitly excluded from the rule: milk in udder, wool on sheep, crops in ground, dates on tree, etc. Note that these are considered existent by custom.
These items are like undivided shares (shuyu') and become owned upon separation and delivery.
3
Understand possession conditions
Learn that permission to take possession is valid only after the donor completes separation. Delivery of the container (e.g., house with furniture) can count as delivery of the contents if intention is clear.
Vacating (takhiya) counts as possession in a valid gift but not in a defective one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن وهب دقيقاً في بر لا وإن طحن وسلم) أي لا يصح الهبة وأشار به إلى أن هبة المعدوم تقع باطلة فلا تعود صحيحة بالتسليم فدخل فيه ما لو وهب دهناً في سمسم أو سمنا في لبن أو حمل جارية، وخرج عنه اللبن في الضرع، والصوف على ظهر الغنم، والزرع والنخل في الأرض، والتمر في النخل، والدار التي فيها متاع الواهب، والجولق الذي فيه الدقيق، أو السرج أو اللجام دون الدابة، أو حلي الجارية دونها، أو دابة وله عليها حمل، أو قمقمة فيها ماء دونه، فإنه كالمشاع يصح، ويملك إذا فصله وسلمه. ويعتبر الإذن بالقبض بعد الفراغ ولا يعتد بالإذن قبله كما يعتد بالتسليم قبله بخلاف ما لو وهب المتاع الذي في الدار وسلمها معه، أو الدقيق في الجوالق ،وسلمها أو دابة مسرجة ملجمة دونهما، أو جارية عليها حلي دونه أو حملاً على دابة دونها وسلمهما، أو ماء في قمقمة دونها، أو دارها ولها فيها أمتعة وهو ساكن فيها حيث يجوز . وإن وهب داراً فيها متاع وسلمها كذلك ثم وهب المتاع منه أيضاً جازت في المتاع خاصة، وإن بدأ فوهب له المتاع وقبض الدار والمتاع ثم وهب الدار جازت الهبة فيهما لأنه حيث هبة الدار لم يكن للواهب فيها شيء وحين هبة قوله : (وأفاد أنه لو وهب نصف الدار الخ) قال في جامع الفصولين برمز فتاوى القاضي ظهير ويشترط كون الموهوب مقسوماً مفرزاً وقت القبض لا وقت الهبة حتى لو وهب نصف دار شائعاً ولم يسلم حتى وهب النصف الآخر وسلم الكل جاز اهـ. ثم رمز الخواهر زاده : الشيوع حالة القبض يمنع الهبة وحالة العقد لا يمنع والتخلية في الهبة الصحيح قبض لا في الفاسدة اهـ قوله : (بخلاف ما لو وهب المتاع الخ (فقط) أي فتاوى القاضي ظهير جاز هبة الشاغل لا المشغول، والأصل أن اشتغال الموهوب بملك الواهب يمنع تمام الهبة إذ القبض شرط أما اشتغال ملك الواهب بالموهوب فلا يمنعه. (ت) أي الزيادات وهبه دابة مسرجة بدون سرجها ولجامها وسامها كذلك لم يجز لاشتغالهما بهما، وجاز عكسه لعدم اشتغالهما بها وعلى هذا الرهن قال صاحب جامع الفصولين : السمسم والسمن في اللبن وملك بلا قبض جديد لو في يد الموهوب له وهبة الأب
His statement: (And if he gifted flour in wheat, no, even if he ground and surrendered) means the gift is not valid. He indicated by this that the gift of a non-existent thing is void and does not become valid by surrender. Included in this is if he gifted oil in sesame, or ghee in milk, or the pregnancy of a slave-girl. Excluded from it is milk in the udder, wool on the sheep's back, crops and palm-trees in the ground, dates on the palm-tree, a house that contains the donor's furniture, a sack that contains flour, or a saddle or bridle without the animal, or jewelry of a slave-girl without her, or an animal that has a load on it, or a waterskin that has water without it, for it is like an undivided share and is valid, and he owns it when he separates and surrenders it. And permission for possession after completion is considered, and permission before it is not considered, just as surrender before it is considered. Unlike if he gifted the furniture in the house and surrendered it together with it, or flour in a sack and surrendered it, or a saddled and bridled animal without them, or a slave-girl with her jewelry without it, or a load on an animal without her and surrendered both, or water in a waterskin without it, or her house and she has furniture in it and he is resident in it, where it is permissible. And if he gifted a house containing furniture and surrendered it likewise, then gifted the furniture from it as well, it is valid specifically for the furniture. And if he started by gifting the furniture and took possession of the house and the furniture, then gifted the house, the gift is valid for both because when he gifted the house, the donor had nothing in it, and at the time of the gift... His statement: (And he stated that if he gifted half the house...). He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn with the symbol of Fatawa al-Qadi Zahir: It is conditioned that the gifted item be divided and separated at the time of possession, not at the time of the gift. So if he gifted half a house as an undivided share and did not surrender until he gifted the other half and surrendered the whole, it is permissible. Then the symbol of al-Khawaharzadah: Undividedness at the time of possession prevents the gift, but at the time of contract does not prevent it. And vacating in a valid gift is possession, not in a defective one. His statement: (Unlike if he gifted the furniture... only) i.e., Fatawa al-Qadi Zahir: Gift of the occupant is valid, not the occupied. The principle is that the occupation of the gifted item with the donor's property prevents the completion of the gift because possession is a condition. But the occupation of the donor's property with the gifted item does not prevent it. (T) i.e., al-Ziyadat: He gifted a saddled animal without its saddle and bridle, and similarly said it is not valid because of their occupation with them, and the opposite is valid because they are not occupied by it. And similarly for pledge, said the author of Jami' al-Fusulayn: Sesame and ghee in milk, and ownership without new possession if in the hand of the donee, and the father's gift to his child.
Source pages p. 488

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the condition that the gifted item must exist at the time of contract. The commentary explains that gift of a non-existent item (هبة المعدوم) is batil (void) and cannot become valid by subsequent surrender. Examples include gifting oil that is still in sesame seeds, ghee that is still in milk, or the future child of a slave-girl. However, several items are excluded from this rule and considered to be in existence by legal custom: milk in the udder, wool on the sheep, crops and trees in the ground, dates on the tree, a house containing the donor's furniture, a sack containing flour, a saddle or bridle on an animal (if the animal is not gifted), jewelry on a slave-girl (if she is not gifted), an animal with a load on it, a waterskin with water. These are treated like undivided shares (shuyu') and the gift is valid, with ownership transferred when the donee separates and takes possession. The critical moment for permission to take possession is after the donor has completed separation (faragh); permission before that is not effective. However, if the donor surrenders the whole container (like a house with furniture, or a sack with flour) in a way that includes the gifted item, it is valid. The passage then returns to the earlier case of gifting half a house, quoting Jami' al-Fusulayn that the condition is that the gifted item be divided and separated at the time of possession, not at the time of contract. Vacating (takhiya) in a valid gift constitutes possession, but not in a defective gift. Finally, it discusses the principle that occupancy of the gifted item by the donor's property prevents completion of the gift, but occupancy of the donor's property by the gifted item does not prevent it. For example, gifting a saddled animal without the saddle is invalid because the saddle occupies the animal, but gifting the saddle without the animal is valid. The same applies to pledge.

Key Terms
هبة المعدوم (hibat al-ma'dum): A gift of something that does not exist at the time of the contract, such as oil that is still in the seed.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking physical control of the gifted item, a condition for completion of the gift.
اللبن في الضرع (al-laban fi al-dar'): Milk in the udder, considered as existent by custom for the purpose of gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Gift of oil in sesame seeds.
B) Gift of flour in wheat before grinding.
C) Gift of milk in the udder.
D) Gift of a future crop not yet planted.

Key Takeaway
The gift of a non-existent item is void in the Hanafi school, but items considered existent by custom are valid gifts.

Gift Validity When Donee Already Holds the Item
Explains Hanafi rule on ownership when donee already holds the item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that existing possession suffices for a gift and distinguish when trust possession vs. liability possession can substitute.

Core Idea
Existing possession of the donee eliminates the need for a new delivery in a gift.

This passage clarifies a key Hanafi principle in gift (hiba) law: if the person receiving the gift already physically possesses the item—whether as a trust (e.g., deposit) or under liability (e.g., usurpation)—the gift is complete without a new handover. The ruling rests on the idea that possession types can substitute for one another under specific conditions. Understanding this saves scholars and practitioners from unnecessary formalism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Confirm that the gifted item is already in the physical control of the donee before the gift contract.
Does not matter how the donee got the item—deposit, loan, usurpation, etc.
2
Classify the existing possession type
Determine whether the donee holds the item as trust (amanah) or liability (daman). Trust: no liability for loss (e.g., deposit). Liability: liable for loss (e.g., usurper, buyer under a corrupt sale).
3
Apply the substitution rule
If the existing possession is of the same legal type as the possession required for a gift (which is liability possession), it substitutes directly. If it is trust possession, the gift's required liability possession does NOT substitute for trust; but liability possession can substitute for trust because it is higher.
The principle: two possessions of same type → one substitutes for the other. Different types → higher (liability) substitutes for lower (trust), not vice versa.
4
Derive the ruling
Since the donee already has physical control, and the gift contract requires possession, the condition is met. Therefore, ownership transfers immediately upon the contract without any new physical delivery.
The gift is valid (sahih) and complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وملك بلا قبض جديد لو في يد الموهوب له يعني يملك الموهوب له العين من غير اشتراط تجديد القبض إذا كانت في يده لحصول الشرط، أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كانت في يده أمانة أو مضمونة ولو وديعة لأنه بعد الهبة لم يكن عاملاً للمالك فاعتبرت يده الحقيقية، والأصل أنه متى تجانس القبضان ناب أحدهما عن الآخر، وإذا تغاير أناب الأعلى عن الأدنى لا عكسه فناب قبض المغصوب والمبيع فاسداً عن قبض البيع الصحيح ولا ينوب قبض الأمانة عنه. وفي الكافي من باب المتفرقات : تقابضا فتقايلا فاشترى أحدهما ما أقال صار قابضاً بنفس العقد لأن العرضين قائمان فكان كل واحد مضموناً بقيمة نفسه كالمغصوب ولو هلك أحدهما العقد فتقايلا ثم جدد في القائم لا. قابضاً بنفس العقد لأنه مضموناً بقيمة العرض يصير الآخر فشابه الموهون .اهـ. وذكر فروعاً تتعلق بالقبضين فراجعها
And he owns without new possession if in the hand of the donee means the donee owns the object without requiring renewal of possession if it is in his hand because the condition is fulfilled. He made it absolute so it includes whether it is in his hand as a trust or as a liability even if a deposit, because after the gift he was not an agent for the owner, so his actual hand is considered. The principle is that whenever two possessions are of the same type, one substitutes for the other; and if they differ, the higher substitutes for the lower, not vice versa. So possession of a usurped or a corruptly sold item substitutes for a valid sale possession, but trust possession does not substitute for it. In al-Kafi from the chapter of miscellaneous: they exchanged and then dissolved, then one bought what he had dissolved – he becomes a possessor by the very contract because the two objects are present, so each is guaranteed by its own value like a usurped item. If one of them dies, the contract is dissolved, then renewed on the remaining, no – he is a possessor by the very contract because it is guaranteed by the value of the object, so the other becomes like a pawn. End. And he mentioned branches related to the two possessions, refer to them.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi rule that if the donee already physically possesses the gifted item (whether by trust, deposit, or as a usurper), a new delivery is not required for the gift to be complete; the existing possession suffices. The key principle is that when two possessions are of the same legal nature (e.g., both are liability-based), one substitutes for the other. When they differ, the higher type (liability possession) substitutes for the lower (trust possession), but not vice versa. Examples: possession of a usurped item or a corrupt sale (both are liability) can substitute for possession in a valid sale; but trust possession (e.g., deposit) cannot substitute for liability possession. The text then cites a case from al-Kafi about a barter dissolution and re-purchase to illustrate how certain transactions create possession by the contract itself, similar to a pawn.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession or delivery that transfers liability; physical control with legal consequences.
يد أمانة (yad amanah): Trust possession (e.g., deposit, loan) where the holder is not liable for loss.
يد ضمان (yad daman): Liability possession (e.g., usurpation, corrupt sale) where the holder is liable for the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A deposit (wadi'ah) is trust possession. If the depositor gifts the deposited item to the depositee, according to the Hanafi rule in this passage, is a new physical delivery required?
A) Yes, because trust possession cannot substitute for gift possession.
B) No, because the item is already in the donee's hand, and that suffices regardless of the type.
C) Yes, because a gift requires a new qabd.
D) No, but only if the donee converts the trust to liability first.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a gift to someone already holding the item is complete without a new handover, based on the principle that existing physical control fulfills the possession condition.

Gift of Occupied Property: Hand of Donee Decides Validity
Resolves intra-Hanafi dispute on gift validity with non-donor occupancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction regarding occupancy of gifted property to determine gift validity, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute between Qadikhan and al-Fusulayn.

Core Idea
The gift is valid if the occupying goods are in the donee's hand — hand control, not mere ownership, determines.

The general Hanafi rule states that a gift of property occupied by the donor's belongings is invalid. But what if the occupying items belong to a third party or the donee himself? This passage resolves an intra-Hanafi conflict: some scholars hold that any occupation by a non-donee prevents the gift, while the relied-upon view distinguishes based on whose hand the goods are in. You will learn to apply this nuanced rule using concrete examples from lease, usurpation, and claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its exception
The general rule: a gifted property occupied by the donor's belongings is invalid. The exception: if the occupying goods belong to a non-donor, the gift may be valid. The key is to determine whose 'hand' the goods are in — the donor's, the donee's, or a third party's.
The term 'hand' (yad) here refers to physical possession or control, not mere ownership.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
Qadikhan in his Ziyadat holds that occupation by any property of a non-donee (whether donor or third party) prevents the gift. Al-Fusulayn argues that prevention depends on whose hand the goods are in: if in the hand of the donor or a non-donee, the gift is invalid; if in the hand of the donee, it is valid.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school; both views rely on the same general principle but differ in application.
3
Examine the supporting examples
The text cites three examples: (1) A lender loans a house to a borrower, later places his own goods in it, then gifts the house to the borrower — valid. (2) Gifting a house with its contents, then the contents are claimed by a third party — the house gift remains valid. (3) Similar for a sack with goods. These show that when the donee already has control over the goods (e.g., by loan or usurpation), the gift is not prevented.
The logic: the donor's 'hand' was only over the house, not over the goods, because the goods were already in the donee's hand.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the gift is valid when the occupying goods are in the hand of the donee. This is affirmed by Jami' al-Fusulayn and Nur al-'Ayn. The opposing view of Qadikhan is considered marjuh (weak) for fatwa.
This ruling also applies to pawn (rahn) and charity (sadaqah), as possession is a condition for their completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وقيدنا بكون الدار الموهوبة مشغولة الخ) (ت) رمز الزيادات جاز هبة المشغول بملك غير الواهب فلو أعار بيتاً فوضع فيه المعير أن المستعير متاعاً غصبه ثم وهب البيت من المستعير جاز، وكذا لو وهب بيتاً بما فيه أو جوالقاً بما فيه من المتاع وسلم ثم استحق المتاع جاز في الدار والجوالق إذ يد الواهب كانت ثابتة على البيت والمتاع جميعاً حقيقة فصح التسليم، ثم بالاستحقاق ظهر أن المتاع لغيره ولم يظهر أن البيت مشغول بملك الواهب وهو المانع ، وكذا الرهن والصدقة إذ القبض شرط تمامها كالهبة أقول في الفصولين : استدل بهذه المسائل على جواز هبة المشغول بملك غير الواهب، وقد صرح في زيادات قاضيخان أن الاشتغال بملك غير الموهوب له يمنع صحة الهبة، سواء كان ملك الواهب أو غيره، لكن الهبة إنما تمتنع إذا كان الاشتغال بمتاع في يد الواهب أو في يد غير الموهوب له، أما إذا كان المتاع في يد الموهوب له بغصب أو عارية أو غير ذلك فلا تمتنع، واستدل عليه بما مر من مسائل الإجارة والغصب والاستحقاق، فظهر أن الأصل أن الهبة إذا كانت مشغولة يملك الواهب أو بملك غير الموهوب له تمنع الهبة إذا لم يكن في يد الموهوب له، وقد قررته في شرح لطائف الإشارات ؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين وأقره في نور العين.
His saying: (And we restricted it to the gifted house being occupied with the donor's belongings) ... (t) symbol of al-Ziyadat: A gift of an occupied property is permissible with the ownership of a non-donor. So if he loaned a house and the lender placed in it the borrower's goods that he had usurped, then gifted the house to the borrower – it is valid. Likewise, if he gifted a house with its contents or a sack with its contents of goods and delivered, then the goods were claimed – the gift of the house and sack is valid because the donor's hand was established over both the house and the goods in reality, so delivery was valid. Then by the claim it became apparent that the goods belonged to someone else, and it did not appear that the house was occupied by the donor's ownership, which is the preventer. Similarly, a pawn and charity, since possession is a condition for their completion like a gift. I say in al-Fusulayn: It is deduced from these issues that a gift of an occupied property is permissible with the ownership of a non-donor, and it is explicitly stated in Ziyadat Qadikhan that occupation by the ownership of a non-donee prevents the validity of the gift, whether it is the ownership of the donor or someone else. But the gift is only prevented if the occupation is with goods in the hand of the donor or in the hand of a non-donee. If the goods are in the hand of the donee by usurpation or loan or otherwise, then it is not prevented. This is deduced from the previously mentioned issues of lease, usurpation, and claim. So it appears that the principle is that if the gift is occupied by the donor's ownership or by the ownership of a non-donee, the gift is prevented only if it is not in the hand of the donee. I have established this in Sharh Lata'if al-Isharat; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn and affirmed in Nur al-'Ayn.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuance within the Hanafi school regarding gifts of property that contain items belonging to the donor or a third party. The initial restriction in the text is that if the gifted house is occupied with the donor's belongings, the gift is invalid. However, the commentator expands: if the occupying goods belong to a non-donor (e.g., a third party), the gift is valid because the donor's hand was only over the house, not the goods. The key distinction is whose hand the goods are in: if the goods are in the hand of the donor or a non-donee, the gift is prevented; but if the goods are already in the hand of the donee (by loan, usurpation, etc.), the gift is valid. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Qadikhan (in Ziyadat) states that occupation by non-donee's property prevents the gift, while al-Fusulayn uses examples to show it does not if the goods are in the donee's hand. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the gift is valid when the goods are in the donee's hand, as the occupation does not create a preventer in that case. This ruling applies similarly to pawn and charity, as possession is a condition for their completion.

Key Terms
مشغول (mashghul): Property occupied by items belonging to someone else, affecting the validity of gift delivery.
يد الموهوب له (yad al-mawhub lahu): The hand of the donee; if items are in this hand, occupation does not prevent gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A gifts a house to B, and B is already living in the house with furniture that belongs to A. Is the gift valid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) No, because the house is occupied by A's furniture.
B) Yes, because the furniture is in B's hand (donee's hand), so occupation does not prevent.
C) No, because A's ownership of the furniture prevents delivery of the house.
D) Yes, because the gift contract overrides the occupation.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a gift of occupied property hinges on the hand control of the occupying goods: if in the donee's hand, the gift is valid; otherwise, it is invalid.

Father's Gift to Minor Child: Legal Validity and Substitute Possession
Defines the legal effect of father's gift to minor child and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of substitute possession to determine the validity of a father's gift to a minor child in various scenarios.

Core Idea
Father's possession substitutes for minor's, making the gift valid immediately by contract.

The Hanafi school holds that when a father gives a gift to his minor child, the gift becomes valid upon the contract itself, without the child's acceptance or separate possession. This is because the father's legal possession (qabd) stands in for the child's, a rule that extends to guardians and covers a wide range of circumstances. Understanding this principle helps clarify why certain gifts are valid while others are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that the father's gift to his minor child becomes valid by the contract (yassir bil-'aqd). This establishes the ruling: the gift is valid immediately upon the offer, without requiring the child's acceptance.
The key is that the father's 'qabd' substitutes for the child's.
2
Locate the key principle
The author gives the reason: 'because the father's possession substitutes for him' (li-anna qabd al-ab yanubu anhu). This is the core Hanafi principle that sets parental gifts apart from other transfers.
This principle is unique to guardians (father and those in his place).
3
Explore the scope: valid and invalid scenarios
The passage explains that the gift is valid when the item is held by the father's depositee, but invalid if held by a usurper, pledgee, or lessee, because their possession is for themselves, not for the father. This shows that 'qabd' requires the father to have actual or constructive control.
Witnesses are not required for validity, only a caution against denial.
4
Understand the requirement of possession for adult children
The ruling is restricted to minor children. For an adult child, even if living with the father, the gift is not completed until the child himself takes possession. This highlights the boundary of the substitute possession rule.
The term 'tifl' (minor) is pivotal.
5
Extend the ruling to other guardians
The term 'father' is interpreted broadly to include anyone with guardianship (wilayah), such as the mother, brother, or paternal uncle when the father is absent. Their possession similarly substitutes for the minor's if they are the caretakers.
This extension is based on the common need for discipline and upbringing.

And the gift of the father to his minor child becomes valid by the contract because the father's possession substitutes for him. His statement includes when it is in the hand of the father's depositee because his hand is like his hand, unlike when it is in the hand of a usurper, pledgee, or lessee, where the gift is not valid due to lack of his possession because their possession is for themselves. Commentary
This passage defines the scope of a father's gift to his minor child. The father's possession substitutes for the child's, so the gift is valid immediately upon contract without the child's acceptance or possession. This applies broadly: if the item is with the father's depositee, or even if the property is occupied by the father's belongings. The rule extends to anyone with guardianship (wilayah), including mother, brother, uncle in certain circumstances. The passage also includes important branches (furu') on gift-giving among children: it is disliked to prefer one child over another without religious reason; giving all wealth to one child is legally valid but sinful. Additional rulings address cases like a sinful child, or gifts through agents, and the requirement to specify the nature of a gift (e.g., loan) to retain flexibility. The commentary also notes a difference within the Hanafi school about the validity of a father's gift of a house occupied by a tenant for rent, where Muhammad holds it invalid if the tenant pays rent, but valid if rent-free.

Key Terms
الأب (al-ab): The father or anyone with guardianship (wilayah) over the child, including mother, brother, uncle in absence of father.
الطفل (al-tifl): Minor child, distinguishing from adult child who must take possession himself.
قبض الأب ينوب عنه (qabd al-ab yanubu anhu): Father's possession substitutes for the minor's possession, so no new qabd needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because father's possession substitutes for daughter's.
B) No, because the pledgee's possession is for themselves.
C) Yes, if the father informs the pawnbroker.
D) No, because witnesses are required.

Key Takeaway
Father's gift to minor child is valid immediately upon contract, as the father's possession substitutes for the child's, but this does not apply when a third party's possession is independent.

Rulings on Preferring Some Children Over Others in Gifts
Presents ethical and legal rulings on preferential gifts among children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on unequal gifts to children and identify exceptions where preference is allowed or prohibited.

Core Idea
Preferring some children in gifts is discouraged but legally valid unless done with sinful intent.

While parental gifts to minor children are straightforward, the question of fairness among children raises important ethical and legal considerations. This passage outlines the Hanafi position: it is disliked (makruh) to favor some children over others without a valid religious reason, but such gifts are legally valid if executed. Additional rulings cover special cases like a sinful child, gifts through agents, and the distinction between gift and loan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling on preference
The passage states: 'it is disliked to prefer some children over others in gift during health unless for an extra virtue in religion.' This is the default: tafdeel is makruh, but permitted if the favored child has greater religious merit.
The ruling is 'makruh tanzihan' (disliked, but not sinful).
2
Understand the legal vs. moral dimension
If a father gives all his wealth to one child, the gift is valid legally (ja'iz qada'an) but he is sinful (itham). This shows that contravening the ethical recommendation does not invalidate the gift.
Legal validity does not imply moral approval.
3
Explore special cases: sinful child and inheritance
If a child is sinful, the father may withhold extra gifts and may even spend his wealth on good causes to prevent aiding sin. However, outright deprivation of inheritance (not through gift) is addressed separately.
The passage says spending on good causes is better than leaving wealth to a sinful child.
4
Distinguish between gift and agency/loan
If a father orders his partner to give money to his child, the child may sue if it was an agency (wakalah) but not if it was a gift, because the gift is incomplete without possession. Also, if a father makes clothes for his child, he cannot later give them to another unless he specified it was a loan at the time of making.
Intention at the time of action determines the legal nature (gift vs. loan).
5
Note the gender equality view
The chosen view (mukhtar) is equality between male and female children in gifts, implying that preference based on gender is not allowed.
This is from al-Khulasah.

Key Terms
تفضيل (tafdeel): Preferring some children over others in gift-giving.
عارية (ariyah): Loan for use; ownership is not transferred.
فاسق (fasiq): A sinful child who openly commits sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a father made clothes for his son and later wants to give those clothes to another child, what must he have done at the time of making the clothes?
A) He must have said it is a gift to the son.
B) He must have specified it was a loan (ariyah).
C) He must have the son's permission.
D) He must have witnesses.

Key Takeaway
While preferring some children in gifts is legally valid, it is morally discouraged except for religious merit; special cases (sinful child) allow departure from equality to prevent sin.

Father's Acceptance of Gift on Behalf of Minor Child
A Hanafi legal passage expanding the scope of a father's guardianship in accepting gifts for minors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the father's acceptance suffices for a gift to a minor child and identify the conditions and exceptions.

Core Idea
The father's possession substitutes for the minor's in accepting gifts, but only if he has general guardianship.

In Hanafi law, making a gift to a minor child raises the question of who can accept on their behalf. The father, as primary guardian, has the authority to accept the gift, and his possession of the item substitutes for the child's own possession. This lesson explores the scope of this rule, including who qualifies as 'father' and in which situations the gift is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage opens by stating that the father's possession (qabd) substitutes for the child's. This means when a gift is made to a minor, the father's acceptance and taking possession are sufficient to complete the gift.
This is the key principle: al-ab yanubu 'an al-saghir fi al-qabd.
2
Determine who qualifies as a guardian
The term 'father' is expanded to include any person with general guardianship (wilayah) over the child, such as the mother (if no other guardian), brother, or paternal uncle, provided they are the child's maintainer.
Guardianship here includes the authority to discipline and manage the child's affairs.
3
Analyze the conditions for validity
The gift is valid only if the property is in the father's hand or his depositary's hand, not in the hand of a usurper, pledgee, or lessee (since they possess for themselves). Witnessing is not required. The father does not need to verbally accept; it suffices that he is satisfied.
The gift is also valid even if the property is occupied by the father's belongings or if the father is living in it.
4
Recognize exceptions and limitations
If the child is an adult (even if dependent), the gift requires the adult's own possession. If the mother gifts her unpaid dower to her child, the child must take possession after her authorization.
The rule is restricted to minor children (tifl).
5
Apply the rule to similar acts
The ruling for gifts applies a fortiori to charity (sadaqah), as giving charity to a minor is even more meritorious. The passage also notes that the rule applies to corporeal properties, not to debts or other intangibles.
This is an example of qiyas a fortiori (al-awlawi).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعقد) لأن قبض الأب ينوب عنه. وشمل كلامه ما إذا كانت في يد مودع الأب لأن يده كيده بخلاف ما إذا كانت في يد الغاصب أو المرتهن أو المستأجر حيث لا تجوز الهبة لعدم قبضه لأن قبضهم لأنفسهم. وشمل ما إذا لم يشهد فإن الإشهاد ليس بشرط لصحتها، وما في الكافي للحاكم من إشهاد الأب عليها فللاحتياط للتحرز عن جحوده أو جحود ورثته. وشمل ما إذا لم يقبل الأب لأن الأب يتولاه فاكتفى فيه بالإيجاب كبيع ماله من ابنه الصغير . وشمل ما إذا كان عبداً آبقاً أو أرسله في حاجته فوهبه له قبل عوده فإنها صحيحة، وشمل ما إذا كانت داراً مشغولة بمتاع الأب فإنه لا يمنع كما إذا كان ساكناً فيها. وأراد بالأب من له ولاية عليه في الجملة فشمل الأم إذا وهبت ولا ولي له ولا وصي وكل من يعوله لوجود الولاية في التأديب والتسليم في الصناعة فدخل الأخ والعم عند غيبة الأب غيبة منقطة إذا كان في عيالهم، وإذا علم الحكم في الهبة علم في الصدقة بالأولى. وقيد بالطفل لأن الهبة للولد الكبير لا تتم إلا بقبضه ولو كان في عياله ؛ كذا في المحيط وأطلق الهبة فانصرفت إلى الأعيان فاستفيد منه أن الأم لو وهبت مهرها لولدها قبل أن تقبضه لا يتم إلا بقبض الولد بعد أن تسلطه عليه ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان .
By the contract) because the father's possession substitutes for him. And his statement includes when it is in the hand of the father's depositary because his hand is like his hand, contrary to when it is in the hand of a usurper, pledgee, or lessee, where the gift is not valid due to lack of possession because their possession is for themselves. And it includes when he did not call witnesses, because calling witnesses is not a condition for its validity, and what is in al-Kafi of al-Hakim about the father's witnessing is for caution to guard against his denial or the denial of his heirs. And it includes when the father did not accept, because the father undertakes it, so the offer suffices, like the sale of his property from his minor son. And it includes when it is a runaway slave or he sent him on his errand and gifted him before his return, for it is valid. And it includes when it is a house occupied by the father's belongings, for it does not prevent, just as if he was living in it. And by 'father' he means one who has guardianship over him in general, so it includes the mother when she gifts and there is no guardian or executor, and everyone who supports him, due to the existence of guardianship in discipline and delivery in craft, so the brother and paternal uncle enter when the father is absent in a way that severs contact if they are his dependents. And if the ruling on gift is known, it is known for charity a fortiori. And it is restricted to a child because the gift to an adult child is not complete except by his possession even if he is his dependent; as in al-Muhit. And he used the word gift absolutely, so it applies to corporeal properties, so it is understood that if the mother gifted her dowry to her child before she took possession, it is not complete except by the child's possession after he authorizes him over it; as in Fatawa Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage explains the scope of the father's acceptance of a gift on behalf of his minor child according to the Hanafi school. The opening statement establishes that the father's possession stands in for the child's. The author then clarifies that this applies when the gifted property is in the hands of the father's depositary (since the depositary's hand is like the father's), but not when it is with a usurper, pledgee, or lessee, as their possession is for themselves. Witnessing is not a condition for validity, though a judge may recommend it for caution. The father does not need to verbally accept; it suffices that he is satisfied. The gift is valid even if the property is occupied by the father's belongings, and the father includes anyone with general guardianship such as the mother or even the brother or uncle if they are maintenance providers. Also, the rule applies only to minor children; adult children must take possession themselves, even if dependents. Additionally, if a mother gifts her unpaid dower, the child must take possession after her authorization.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking possession of the gifted property, which is a condition for the completion of the gift in many cases. Here, the father's qabd substitutes for the child's.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or legal authority to act on behalf of another. Here, it refers to the father's authority to accept gifts for his minor child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Any relative's acceptance

Key Takeaway
The father's acceptance of a gift for his minor child is valid and substitutes for the child's possession, but the rule has specific conditions and does not extend to adult children even if dependent.

Preferential Gifts to Children in Hanafi Fiqh
Rulings on the dislike of preference in gifts to children

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving preference to some children in gifts and explain the distinction between legal validity and sin.

Core Idea
Preferring one child in gifts is disliked, not prohibited—the gift is valid but the giver is sinful.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on gifts to children. While parents may wish to favour one child, the school holds that equality is recommended, and preference is discouraged unless for religious reasons. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq outlines the core ruling, its exceptions, and several practical sub-rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify the act (preferring some children in gifts during health) and its hukm level: makruh tanzih (disliked), unless there is an increase in religious merit.
The dislike applies only in health; illness may trigger different rules.
2
Understand the consequence of violation
If a parent gives all property to one child despite the dislike, the gift is legally valid but the parent commits a sin.
The distinction is between legal enforceability and moral accountability.
3
Analyze the case of agency
A father orders his partner to give money to his child. If the partner refuses, the son can sue only if the instruction was not a gift; a gift requires delivery (qabd) to be complete.
Agency ends upon refusal, but a gift is incomplete without possession.
4
Note the mufta bihi
Equality between male and female children in gifts is the chosen (mukhtar) position in the school.
This is stated in al-Khulasa.
5
Apply rulings to special cases
For a sinful child: it is better to spend wealth on good causes than to leave it to him. For clothes made for a specific child or student: they cannot be given to another without specifying at the time that it was a loan.
These cases highlight the importance of intention and specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروع : يكره تفضيل بعض الأولاد على البعض في الهبة حالة الصحة إلا لزيادة فضل له في الدين، وإن وهب ماله كله لواحد جاز قضاء وهو آثم ؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : رجل أمر شريكه بأن يدفع إلى ولده مالاً فامتنع الشريك عن الأداء كان للابن أن يخاصمه إن لم يكن على وجه الهبة، وإن كان على وجهها لا لأنه في الأول وكيل عن الأب، وفي الثاني لا وهي غير تامة لعدم الملك لعدم القبض وفي الخلاصة: المختار التسوية بين الذكر والأنثى في الهبة، ولو كان ولده فاسقاً فأراد أن يصرف ماله إلى وجوه الخير ويحرمه عن الميراث هذا خير من تركه لأن فيه إعانه على المعصية، ولو كان ولده فاسقاً لا يعطى له أكثر من قوته ولو اتخذ لولده ثياباً ثم أراد أن يدفع إلى آخر ليس له ذلك إلا أن يبين وقت الاتخاذ أنه عارية وكذا لو اتخذ لتلميذه ثياباً فأراد أن يدفع إلى غيره، وإن أراد الاحتياط يبين أنها عارية حتى يمكنه أن يدفع إلى غيره .اهـ. وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة من آخره: من صنع لطفلة تتم بالعقد وإن له أجنبي يتم بقبض وليه وأمه وأجنبي لو في حجرهما وهب لولده ثياباً قبل أن يولد ليوضع عليها نحو الملحفة والوسادة ثم ولدته امرأته ووضع عليها ثم مات الولد لا تكون الثياب ميراثاً ما لم يقر أن الثياب ملك الولد بخلاف ثياب البدن فإنه يملكها إذا لبسها كمن قال إن فلاناً كان لابساً فهو إقرار له بخلاف ما إذا قال كان قاعداً على هذا البساط أو نائماً عليه لا يكون مقراً له بذلك اهـ
Branches: It is disliked to prefer some children over others in gifts in a state of health, except for an increase in religious merit. And if he gives all his property to one, it is valid legally but he is sinful; as in al-Muhit. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: A man ordered his partner to give money to his child, and the partner refused to pay. The son may sue him if it was not in the form of a gift, but if it was in the form of a gift, then not, because in the first he is a deputy of the father, and in the second it is incomplete due to lack of possession. And in al-Khulasa: The chosen view is equality between male and female in gifts. And if his child is sinful and he wants to spend his wealth on good causes and deprive him of inheritance, this is better than leaving it to him because it is assisting in sin. And if his child is sinful, he should not give him more than his sustenance. And if he made clothes for his child and then wanted to give them to another, he cannot unless he specified at the time of making that it is a loan. Similarly, if he made clothes for his student and wanted to give to another. If he wants to be cautious, he should state that it is a loan so that he can give to another. End. And in al-Mubtagha: ... [the quote about the girl and the clothes] ...
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This section presents several subsidiary rulings (فروع) related to gifts to children. The first ruling is that it is makruh (disliked) to give preference to some children over others in gifts when the parent is healthy, unless there is a religious reason for preference. If a parent gives all his wealth to one child, the gift is valid in court but the parent commits a sin. The next ruling concerns a case where a father orders his partner to give money to his child; if the partner refuses, the son can sue if the transaction was not a gift. Equality between male and female in gifts is the preferred position. For a sinful child, it is better to give charity with the money rather than leave it to him to enable sin. At most, only basic sustenance should be given. If a parent makes clothes for a specific child, they cannot later give them to another unless specified as a loan. The quote from al-Mubtagha provides a case about clothes made for a future child and the issue of inheritance.

Key Terms
كراهة (karahah): Dislike or that which is discouraged. Here referring to the religious discouragement of giving preference to some children.
هبة (hibah): Gift, a voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
آثم (athim): Sinner, one who commits a sin.
عارية (ariyyah): Loan, a temporary transfer of use without ownership.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or delivery, a condition for completion of a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The gift is invalid and the property remains with the father
B) The gift is valid but the father commits a sin
C) The gift is valid and recommended
D) The gift is invalid due to inequality

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, favoring one child in gifts during health is disliked (makruh tanzih); the gift is valid but the giver is sinful unless there is a religious justification.
