Acceptance of Gifts for a Minor: Guardians and Custody Conditions
Explaining who can accept a gift for a minor from a non-relative and the conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four guardians who can accept a gift for a minor without custody, and state the additional custody condition required for the mother and a stranger to accept such a gift.

Core Idea
Only four specific guardians can accept a gift for a minor; the mother and a stranger need custody.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the Hanafi ruling on who may accept a gift given to a minor by a non-relative. It distinguishes between the four primary guardians—father, his executor, grandfather, his executor—who have general authority, and others like the mother or a stranger, who require the minor to be in their actual care. The text also clarifies special scenarios such as gifts given at feasts and the liability of a father when handing items to his child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is accepting a gift from a stranger (non-relative) for a minor. The ruling is that the gift is validly accepted only when possession is taken by a proper guardian. Determine the hukm level: it is sahih (valid) when conditions are met.
The gift itself is a contract; possession is necessary for completion.
2
List the four primary guardians
The four guardians in order are: the father, the father's executor (wasi), the grandfather, and the grandfather's executor. They can accept regardless of whether the minor is in their custody. No other person can accept if any of these four is present.
Presence means being at the location or reachable; if absent with severed contact, the next in line may accept.
3
Identify the condition for mother and stranger
The mother and any stranger (including a finder of a foundling) can accept a gift for the minor only if the minor is in their custody (hijr). Custody means actual care and control. If the minor is not in their care, their acceptance is invalid.
The mother has guardianship over the child's welfare, and the stranger's hand is considered valid because no other stranger can take possession from him.
4
Apply the ruling to special cases
The text addresses gifts given at feasts (circumcision, wedding). If the gift is suitable for the child (e.g., children's clothes), it belongs to the child. Otherwise, it is attributed to the parent whose relatives or acquaintances gave it, unless the giver specifies otherwise. This shows that the guardian's authority is over property, but the child's ownership is determined by the nature of the gift and the giver's intent.
If the giver says 'I give this to the father', his statement is taken.
5
Note the liability nuance
A fatwa from al-Ramli illustrates that if a father gives a piece of bread to his minor child and then takes it back and gives it to another, he is liable if he intended ownership; if he merely permitted use, he is not liable. This shows that mere handing over does not transfer ownership; the father's intention matters.
This nuance is important for understanding possession and liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن وهب له أجنبي يتم بقبض وليه) لأن للولي ولاية التصرف في ماله وقبضها منه أراد بالولي هنا واحداً من أربعة وهو الأب ووصيه والجد ووصيه على هذا الترتيب وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في حجره أو لا . ولا يجوز قبض غير هؤلاء الأربعة مع وجود واحد منهم سواء كان الصغير في عيال القابض أو لم يكن، وسواء كان ذا رحم محرم أو أجنبياً. والمراد بالوجود الحضور فلو غاب غيبة منقطعة جاز قبض الذي يتلوه إلى الولاية؛ كذا في الخلاصة. ويباح للوالدين أن يأكلا المأكول الموهوب للصغير؛ كذا في الخلاصة أيضاً فأفاد أن غير المأكول لا يباح لهما إلا من عند الاحتياج كما لا يخفى. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن ما علم أنه وهب للصغير يكون ملكاً له، أما لو اتخذ الأب وليمة للختان فأهدى الناس هدايا ووضعوا بين يدي الولد، فإن كانت الهبة تصلح للصبي مثل ثياب الصبيان أو شيء يستعمله الصبيان فالهدية للصبي، وإن كانت غير تلك كالدراهم والدنانير والحيوان ومتاع البيت ينظر إلى المهدي : إن كان أقرباء الأب أو من معارفه فهو للأب، وإن كان من أقرباء الأم أو معارفها فهو للأم، وسواء كان المهدي يقول عند الهدية هذا للصبي أو لم يقل. وكذا لو اتخذ الوليمة لزفاف بنته إلى بيت زوجها فأهدى أقرباء الزوج أو المرأة. وهذا إذا لم يقل المهدي أهديت للأب أو للأم وتعذر الرجوع إلى قوله، أما إذا قال شيئاً فالقول قوله ؛ كذا في الخلاصة .اهـ. قوله : ( وأمه وأجنبي لو في حجرهما) أي وتتم الهبة بقبض الأم أو الأجنبي بشرط أن يكون في حجر القابض لأن للأم الولاية فيما يرجع إلى حفظه وحفظ ماله وللأجنبي يد معتبرة ألا ترى أنه لا يتمكن أجنبي آخر أن ينزعه من يده فيملك ما تمحض نفعاً في حقه. وليس مراد المصنف رحمه الله قصر الحكم على الأم والأجنبي بل كل غريب غير الأب والجد ووصيهما كالأم يتم بقبضه إن كان الصغير في عياله وإلا فلا. ودخل الملتقط في الأجنبي فإن له أن يقبض هبة اللقيط إن كان في عياله وليس له أحد سواه؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وأشار المصنف إلى أن للأجنبي أن يسلم الولد الذي في حجره في صناعة كقبضه ما وهب له وإن لم يكن وصياً ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بقبض الهبة لأنه إذا قبضها الأجنبي أو غيره غير الأربعة المتقدمة ليس له الإنفاق منها؛ كذا في في قال الرملي : وفي الحاوي الزاهدي برمز (بم): دفع لولده الصغير قرصاً فأكل نصفه ثم أخذه منه ودفعه لآخر يضمنه إذا كان دفعه لولده على وجه التمليك، وإذا دفعه على وجه الإباحة لا يضمن. قال : عرف به أن مجرد الدفع من الأب إلى الصغير لا يكون تمليكاً وأنه حسن اهـ.
His statement: (And if a stranger gives him a gift, it is completed by the possession of his guardian) because the guardian has authority to dispose of his property and to take possession from him. He intended by 'guardian' here one of four: the father, his executor, the grandfather, and his executor, in this order. And he used it absolutely, so it includes whether he is in his custody or not. And it is not permissible for anyone other than these four to take possession if any one of them is present, whether the minor is in the care of the taker or not, and whether he is a close relative or a stranger. And by 'presence' is meant being present; so if he is away in a way that severs contact, it is permissible for the next in line of guardianship to take possession; as in al-Khulasa. And it is permissible for the parents to eat the edible food gifted to the minor; as in al-Khulasa also. So it indicates that non-edible items are not permissible for them except when needed, as is not hidden. And the author indicated that what is known to be gifted to the minor becomes his property. But if the father holds a circumcision feast and people bring gifts and place them before the child, then if the gift is suitable for the child like children's clothes or something used by children, the gift is for the child. If it is other than that, like dirhams, dinars, animals, or household goods, then consideration is given to the giver: if they are relatives of the father or his acquaintances, it is for the father; if they are relatives of the mother or her acquaintances, it is for the mother. This is regardless of whether the giver says 'this is for the child' or not. Similarly, if he holds a wedding feast for his daughter's wedding, and the relatives of the husband or wife bring gifts. This applies if the giver does not specify that he gifted it to the father or mother and it is impossible to refer to his statement. If he did say something, his statement is taken; as in al-Khulasa. End. His statement: (And his mother and a stranger if they are in his custody) meaning the gift is completed by the possession of the mother or the stranger on condition that the child is in the custody of the taker, because the mother has guardianship regarding his custody and property, and the stranger has a valid hand. Do you not see that no other stranger can take it from his hand, so he possesses what is purely beneficial for him? And it is not the intention of the author to limit the ruling to the mother and the stranger; rather, every stranger other than the father, grandfather and their executors, like the mother, his possession completes it if the child is in his care, otherwise not. And the finder of a foundling is included in 'stranger'; for he may accept a gift for the foundling if he is in his care and there is no one else; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. And the author indicated that a stranger may apprentice the child in his care, like accepting a gift for him, even if he is not an executor; as in al-Khulasa. And it is restricted to the possession of the gift, because if the stranger or someone other than the four mentioned takes possession, he is not permitted to spend from it; as in ... Al-Ramli said: In al-Hawi al-Zahidi, with the symbol (b.m.): A man gave his minor child a piece of bread, and he ate half, then he took it from him and gave it to another. He is liable if he gave it to his child as ownership, but if he gave it as mere permission (ibahah), he is not liable. He said: From this it is understood that mere giving from the father to the minor is not ownership, and that is good. End.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the acceptance of gifts for a minor from a stranger (non-relative). The ruling is that the gift is completed by the possession of the guardian, who is one of four in order: the father, the father's executor, the grandfather, and the grandfather's executor. Only these four can accept the gift if present; others cannot, even if they are close relatives. If the primary guardian is absent in a way that severs contact, the next in line may accept. The text also discusses the permissibility of parents eating edible gifts to the minor, and the rules for distributing gifts given at feasts like circumcision or weddings. When gifts are given directly to the child, if they are suitable for the child they belong to the child; otherwise, they go to the parents based on who the giver is related to. The second part covers the mother and a stranger: they can accept a gift for a minor only if the minor is in their custody. The mother has guardianship over his welfare, and a stranger's hand is considered valid since no other can take from him. The finder of a foundling is included. Lastly, a fatwa from al-Ramli clarifies that mere handing over by the father to the child does not constitute ownership unless intended as such.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): Guardian; specifically one of four: father, his executor, grandfather, his executor, in order, who can accept gifts for a minor.
حجر (hijr): Custody or care; the mother or stranger must have the minor in their actual care to accept gifts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who among the following can accept a gift for a minor child from a stranger even if the child is not in their custody?
A) The mother
B) The father
C) The maternal uncle
D) A family friend

Key Takeaway
Only the father, his executor, the grandfather, and his executor can accept gifts for a minor without custody; the mother and any stranger need the minor to be in their actual care.

Gift to a Minor and Possession by Guardian
Explaining completion of gift to minor by guardian's possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four guardians who can take possession of a gift on behalf of a minor and explain the conditions under which possession completes the gift.

Core Idea
A gift from a stranger to a minor is completed only when the guardian takes possession.

Gifts to minors involve special rules because minors cannot legally possess property. The Hanafi school specifies that while a father's gift to his child is completed by the contract alone, a gift from a stranger requires possession by a qualified guardian. This lesson unpacks who those guardians are and the conditions for valid possession, including exceptions for mothers and caretakers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: a gift (hiba) given to a minor. The ruling distinguishes between gifts from the father (completed by contract) and from strangers (completed by guardian's possession).
The father's gift is an exception because of his direct authority.
2
List the four guardians
The guardian (wali) who can take possession is one of four: the father, his executor (wasi), the grandfather, and his executor, in that order. No one else can act if one of these is present.
'Presence' means physically present; if absent without contact, the next in line acts.
3
Understand the condition of 'care' (fi hijrihi)
The mother or a stranger can take possession only if the minor is in their care (i.e., living with them). This is because they have a recognized hand (yad) over the minor.
The finder of a foundling (multaqit) is included as a stranger if the foundling is in his care.
4
Distinguish types of gifts
Gifts given at feasts (circumcision, wedding) depend on the nature of the item and the relationship of the giver. Suitable items go to the child; otherwise, they go to the parent associated with the giver.
If the giver explicitly states the recipient, that statement prevails.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
For example, an uncle gives a toy to his nephew. The uncle is a stranger; thus, the gift is completed only when the father (or other guardian) takes physical possession on behalf of the child.
The guardian's possession is a condition (shart) for the gift to become binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لطفلة تتم بالعقد وإن له أجنبي يتم بقبض وليه وأمه وأجنبي لو في حجرهما وهب لولده ثياباً قبل أن يولد ليوضع عليها نحو الملحفة والوسادة ثم ولدته امرأته ووضع عليها ثم مات الولد لا تكون الثياب ميراثاً ما لم يقر أن الثياب ملك الولد بخلاف ثياب البدن فإنه يملكها إذا لبسها كمن قال إن فلاناً كان لابساً فهو إقرار له بخلاف ما إذا قال كان قاعداً على هذا البساط أو نائماً عليه لا يكون مقراً له بذلك اهـ قوله : ( وإن وهب له أجنبي يتم بقبض وليه) لأن للولي ولاية التصرف في ماله وقبضها منه أراد بالولي هنا واحداً من أربعة وهو الأب ووصيه والجد ووصيه على هذا الترتيب وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان في حجره ره أو لا . ولا يجوز قبض غير هؤلاء الأربعة مع وجود واحد منهم سواء كان الصغير في عيال القابض أو لم يكن، وسواء كان ذا رحم محرم أو أجنبياً. والمراد بالوجود الحضور فلو غاب غيبة منقطعة جاز قبض الذي يتلوه إلى الولاية؛ كذا في الخلاصة. ويباح للوالدين أن يأكلا المأكول الموهوب للصغير؛ كذا في الخلاصة أيضاً فأفاد أن غير المأكول لا يباح لهما إلا من عند الاحتياج كما لا يخفى. وأشار المؤلف إلى أن ما علم أنه وهب للصغير يكون ملكاً له، أما لو اتخذ الأب وليمة للختان فأهدى الناس هدايا ووضعوا بين يدي الولد، فإن كانت الهبة تصلح للصبي مثل ثياب الصبيان أو شيء يستعمله الصبيان فالهدية للصبي، وإن كانت غير تلك كالدراهم والدنانير والحيوان ومتاع البيت ينظر إلى المهدي : إن كان أقرباء الأب أو من معارفه فهو للأب، وإن كان من أقرباء الأم أو معارفها فهو للأم، وسواء كان المهدي يقول عند الهدية هذا للصبي أو لم يقل. وكذا لو اتخذ الوليمة لزفاف بنته إلى بيت زوجها فأهدى أقرباء الزوج أو المرأة. وهذا إذا لم يقل المهدي أهديت للأب أو للأم وتعذر الرجوع إلى قوله، أما إذا قال شيئاً فالقول قوله ؛ كذا في الخلاصة .اهـ. قوله : ( وأمه وأجنبي لو في حجرهما) أي وتتم الهبة بقبض الأم أو الأجنبي بشرط أن يكون في حجر القابض لأن للأم الولاية فيما يرجع إلى حفظه وحفظ ماله وللأجنبي يد معتبرة ألا ترى أنه لا يتمكن أجنبي آخر أن ينزعه من يده فيملك ما تمحض نفعاً في حقه. وليس مراد المصنف رحمه الله قصر الحكم على الأم والأجنبي بل كل غريب غير الأب والجد ووصيهما كالأم يتم بقبضه إن كان الصغير في عياله وإلا فلا. ودخل الملتقط في الأجنبي فإن له أن يقبض هبة اللقيط إن كان في عياله وليس له أحد سواه؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان وأشار المصنف إلى أن للأجنبي أن يسلم الولد الذي في حجره في صناعة كقبضه ما وهب له وإن لم يكن وصياً ؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وقيد بقبض الهبة لأنه إذا قبضها الأجنبي أو غيره غير الأربعة المتقدمة ليس له الإنفاق منها؛ كذا في قال الرملي : وفي الحاوي الزاهدي برمز (بم): دفع لولده الصغير قرصاً فأكل نصفه ثم أخذه منه ودفعه لآخر يضمنه إذا كان دفعه لولده على وجه التمليك، وإذا دفعه على وجه الإباحة لا يضمن. قال : عرف به أن مجرد الدفع من الأب إلى الصغير لا يكون تمليكاً وأنه حسن اهـ.
A gift to a young girl is completed by the contract, and if a stranger gives to him, it is completed by the possession of his guardian, and his mother and a stranger if they are in their care. If a father gave clothes to his son before he was born to be placed on him like a blanket and pillow, then his wife gave birth and placed them on him, then the son died, the clothes are not inheritance unless he (the father) acknowledges that the clothes were the property of the son, unlike body clothes, for he owns them when he wears them, like one who says 'So-and-so was wearing' it is an acknowledgment for him, unlike if he said 'he was sitting on this carpet' or 'sleeping on it', he is not an acknowledger for him. He said: (And if a stranger gives to him, it is completed by possession of his guardian) because the guardian has the authority to dispose of his property and to receive possession thereof. By guardian here he means one of four: the father, his executor, the grandfather, and his executor in this order, and he made it absolute to include whether he is in his care or not. Possession by other than these four is not permissible in the presence of any one of them, whether the minor is in the care of the receiver or not, and whether he is a close relative or a stranger. 'Presence' means being present; if he is absent in a way that cuts off contact, the next in line of guardianship may receive possession; as in al-Khulasa. It is permissible for the parents to eat the edible items given as gift to the minor; as in al-Khulasa, indicating that non-edible items are not permissible except when needed. The author indicated that what is known to be given to the minor becomes his property. But if the father makes a feast for circumcision and people give gifts and place them before the child, then if the gift is suitable for the child like clothes or something the child uses, the gift is for the child; if not, like dirhams, dinars, animals, or household goods, then it depends on the giver: if he is from the father's relatives or acquaintances, it is for the father; if from the mother's relatives or acquaintances, it is for the mother, regardless of whether the giver says 'this is for the child' or not. Similarly, if he makes a feast for his daughter's wedding to her husband's house, and the relatives of the husband or wife give gifts. This applies when the giver does not say 'I gave to the father' or 'to the mother' and reference to his statement is impossible; if he says something, his word is accepted; as in al-Khulasa. He said: (And his mother and a stranger if they are in their care) i.e., the gift is completed by possession of the mother or the stranger provided that the minor is in the care of the receiver, because the mother has authority concerning his protection and the protection of his property, and the stranger has a considered hand; do you not see that another stranger cannot take it from his hand? Thus he owns what is purely beneficial for him. The author did not intend to limit the ruling to the mother and stranger; rather, every outsider other than the father, grandfather, and their executors is like the mother: possession completes it if the minor is in his household, otherwise not. The finder [of a foundling] is included in 'stranger'; he may receive possession of a gift to the foundling if he is in his household and he has no one else; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. The author indicated that a stranger may apprentice the child in his care to a craft, just as he may receive possession of what is given to him, even if he is not an executor; as in al-Khulasa. He restricted it to possession of the gift because if the stranger or someone other than the four preceding takes possession, he is not allowed to spend from it; as in al-Khulasa. Al-Ramli said: In al-Hawi al-Zahidi, with a symbol (b m): A father gave a loaf to his young son; he ate half, then the father took it from him and gave it to another; he is liable if he gave it to his son as ownership, but if he gave it as permissibility (ibaha), he is not liable. He said: From this it is known that mere handing over by the father to the child is not ownership, and that it is good.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage begins with a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, followed by commentary from Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The matn states that a gift to a minor is completed by the contract itself, but the commentary clarifies that this is only for a gift given by the father to his own minor child; for a gift from a stranger, possession by the guardian is necessary. The guardian is specifically one of four: the father, his executor, the grandfather, or his executor, in that order. If one of them is present, no other person can take possession on behalf of the minor. The 'presence' means being physically present; if absent without contact, the next in line may act. The parents are allowed to eat edible gifts given to the child, but non-edible items are only permissible if needed. The commentary then discusses gifts given at feasts, such as circumcision or weddings, explaining how to determine ownership based on the nature of the gift and the relation of the giver. The mother and a stranger can also take possession if the minor is in their care, because the mother has authority over the child's protection and property, and a stranger has a recognized hand of possession. This includes the finder of a foundling. However, the stranger or other non-guardian cannot spend from the gifted property after taking possession. The passage ends with a note from al-Ramli illustrating that merely giving a loaf to a child does not constitute ownership unless done with the intention of ownership; otherwise, it is mere permissibility.

Key Terms
ولي (wali): The legal guardian who has authority over the minor's property; specifically the father, his executor, the grandfather, and his executor in that order.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or legal possession of a gifted item, which is necessary for the completion of a gift from a stranger to a minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four primary guardians who can take possession of a gift for a minor according to the Hanafi school?
A) Father
B) Mother
C) Grandfather
D) Executor of the father

Key Takeaway
A gift to a minor from a stranger requires possession by a specific guardian; the father's gift is an exception completed by contract alone.

Gift to a Minor Who Is Rational
Expansion on the minor's capacity to accept gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a minor may accept a gift himself, and distinguish when a guardian is required.

Core Idea
A discerning minor can accept and reject a gift himself, but the donor retains the right to take it back unless the judge intervenes.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the matn's statement that a gift to a rational minor is completed by the minor's possession. The commentary clarifies the meaning of rationality (tamyiz), confirms that the minor's rejection is also valid, and details additional rulings such as the donor's right to retract and the judge's authority to sell the gifted property to prevent retraction. It also distinguishes gift acceptance from debt payment to a minor.

Guided Path
1
Define rationality for a minor
Identify the meaning of 'aql in this context: it is tamyiz (discernment), i.e., the ability to understand the transaction and express oneself. The age varies but is typically around 7 years in Hanafi fiqh.
The minor must understand what a gift is and that he is receiving it.
2
Determine who can accept the gift
If the minor is rational, he can take possession himself, completing the gift. If not rational (non-discerning), the guardian must take possession. This applies whether the father is alive or dead.
The passage states that the author's wording is absolute, covering both cases.
3
Recognize the minor's right to reject
A rational minor may also reject a gift, just as he can accept it, because gift is a contract requiring acceptance.
Rejection is equivalent to not accepting; the gift is void.
4
Understand the donor's right to retract
The donor has the right to take back the gift even after the minor has possessed it. The father cannot compensate from the minor's property. However, the judge may sell the gifted property to prevent the donor from retracting.
This ruling is specific to gifts to minors; other transactions like debt payment are different.
5
Distinguish gift from debt/rent payments
If a debtor pays a debt to a minor, or a lessee pays rent, it is not valid because those are not purely beneficial; they involve liability. The guardian must receive such payments.
Gift is purely beneficial, so the minor may accept directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وبقبضه إن عقل ) أي تتم هبة الأجنبي للصغير بقبض الصغير إن كان عاقلاً لأنه نافع في حقه وهو من أهله والمراد من العقل هنا أن يكون مميزاً يعقل التحصيل. أطلق المصنف رحمه الله تعالى فشمل ما إذا كان الأب حياً أو ميتاً كما الخلاصة. وأشار إلى أنه كما يتم بقبضه يصح رده ولهذا قال في المبتغي بالمعجمة؛ من وهب لصغير يعبر عن نفسه شيئاً فرده يصح كما يصح قبوله، وفي المبسوط : من وهب للصغير شيئاً له أن يرجع فيه وليس للأب التعويض من مال الصغير .اهـ. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : ويبيع القاضي ما وهب للصغير حتى لا يرجع الواهب في هبته .اهـ. وقيد بالهبة لأن المديون لو دفع ما عليه للصبي ومستأجره لو دفع الأجرة إليه لا وأفاد أنه يصح، تصح الهبة للصغير الذي لا يعقل ويقبضه وليه. وأشار بإطلاقه إلى أن الموهوب لو كان مديوناً للصغير تصح الهبة ويسقط الدين كما صرح به قاضيخان في فتاواه
He said: (And by his possession if he is rational) i.e., the gift of a stranger to a minor is completed by the minor's possession if he is rational, because it is beneficial for him and he is capable of it. By 'rational' here is meant that he is discerning, able to understand acquisition. The author made it absolute, including whether the father is alive or dead, as in al-Khulasa. He indicated that just as it is completed by his possession, his rejection is also valid; hence in al-Mubtaghi: If someone gives something to a minor who can express himself and he rejects it, it is valid just as his acceptance is valid. In al-Mabsut: Whoever gives something to a minor has the right to take it back, and the father cannot compensate from the minor's property. In Fatawa Qadikhan: The judge sells what was given to the minor so that the donor does not take back his gift. He restricted it to gift because if a debtor pays what he owes to the minor, or a lessee pays the rent to him, it is not valid. And it indicates that it is valid: the gift to a minor who does not understand is valid, and his guardian takes possession. By his absolute expression he indicated that if the gifted item is a debt owed by the donor to the minor, the gift is valid and the debt is discharged, as Qadikhan stated in his Fatawa.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage explains the condition of the minor's rationality for taking possession of a gift from a stranger. The matn says 'by his possession if he is rational,' meaning if the minor is discerning (mumayyiz) and understands receiving the item, he can complete the gift by taking possession himself, regardless of whether his father is alive or dead. The commentary clarifies that a rational minor's rejection of a gift is also valid. However, the donor has the right to take back his gift, even if the minor has it, and the father cannot compensate from the minor's property. To prevent the donor from retracting, the judge may sell the gifted property. The ruling is specific to gifts; if a debtor pays a debt to the minor or a lessee pays rent, it is not valid because those require the guardian's receipt. For a minor who is not rational, the gift is valid and the guardian takes possession. The passage also notes that a gift to discharge a debt owed by the donor to the minor is valid, and the debt is cleared.

Key Terms
عقل (aql): Rationality sufficient for discernment (tamyiz), meaning the minor can understand the transaction and express himself.
رد (radd): Rejection of a gift by the minor; it is valid if he is rational and expresses it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, who may take possession of a gift given to a non-rational minor?
A) The minor himself
B) The mother
C) The guardian
D) The donor

Key Takeaway
A discerning minor (mumayyiz) can accept and reject a gift himself; for a non-discerning minor, the guardian acts; the donor retains the right to retract unless the judge intervenes.

Husband’s Possession of Gift to His Minor Wife
Explaining the permissibility and conditions of a husband taking possession of gifts to his minor wife after the wedding night.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a husband may take possession of a gift given to his minor wife and identify when this is permissible according to Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The husband may take possession of gifts to his minor wife only after the wedding night, as he then maintains her and has a rightful hand over her.

In Hanafi fiqh, a minor wife's property is managed by her father or guardian, but after the wedding night, the husband gains certain rights over her maintenance. This lesson examines the specific ruling on whether the husband can take possession of gifts given to his minor wife, and under what conditions. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the permissibility and its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband taking possession of a gift given to his minor wife. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) after the wedding night.
The text uses 'يجوز' (it is permissible).
2
Note the restrictions
The text restricts it to a minor wife (not adult) and after the wedding night (not before). Understand why: before the wedding night, the husband does not maintain her and has no rightful hand.
The conditions are cumulative.
3
Understand the composite cause
According to the author of al-Nihaya, the ruling depends on two conditions: maintenance (y'uluha) and a rightful hand (yad mustahaqqa). Without both, the ruling is negated.
Some scholars consider only maintenance as the cause.
4
Consider the exception
If the wife is capable of intercourse and the impediment is from the husband's side, some allow possession before the wedding night because he maintains her, but the correct view rejects this due to lack of rightful hand.
The exception is not accepted according to the composite cause.
5
Recognize other related rulings
The father may also take possession, and the minor wife herself may take possession if rational. The ruling applies only to gifts, not debts, and not to slaves or mukatibs.
These are additional points from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ويجوز قبض زوج الصغيرة ما وهب بعد الزفاف لتفويض الأب أمورها إليه دلالة قيد بالصغيره لأنه لا يملك قبض ما وهب لزوجته البالغة كما لا يملكه الأب وقيد بكونه بعد الزفاف لأنه لا يملكه قبله، وعلل الشارح له بأنه لا يعولها قبله فاستفيد منه أنها لو كانت ممن تقدر على الجماع وكان المانع من من قبله جاز قبضه قبله لأنه حينئذ ،يعولها، لكن ذكر صاحب النهاية علة مركبة من بعد الزفاف يعولها وله عليها يد مستحقة ففي المسألة المفروضة وإن كان يعلوها ليس له عليها يد مستحقة فانتفى الحكم مطلقاً كما لا يخفى وأطلق المصنف فأفاد أنه يملك القبض بعد الزفاف حال حياة الأب أيضاً بخلاف الأم ومن بمعناها كما تقدم. وأشار إلى أنه لا فرق بين كونها ممن تجامع أو لا وهو الصحيح، وأشار بقوله يجوز» إلى أن الأب لو قبضها جاز، وإلى أنه لو قبضتها جاز أيضاً إن كانت عاقلة . وقيد بقوله ما وهب لأنه لا يملك قبض ديونها مطلقاً، وقيد بالصغير والصغيرة لأن ما وهب للعبد المحجور لا يملك المولى قبضه وإنما يملكه العبد وإذا قبضه ملكه المولى لأنه كسب عبده، وكذا المكاتب لكن لا يملكه المولى لأنه أحق بإكسابه؛ كذا في المحيط .
He said: And it is permissible for the husband of a small wife to take possession of what is given to her after the wedding night, because the father has delegated her affairs to him by implication. He restricted it to a small wife because he cannot take possession of what is given to his adult wife, just as the father cannot. And he restricted it to being after the wedding night because he cannot take possession before it. The commentator explained it by the fact that he does not maintain her before it. So it is understood that if she is capable of intercourse and the impediment is from his side, he may take possession before it because then he maintains her. But the author of al-Nihaya mentioned a composite cause: after the wedding night, he maintains her and has a right of hand over her. So in the assumed case, even if he maintains her, he does not have a right of hand over her, so the ruling is negated absolutely, as is clear. The author made it absolute, indicating that he has the right to take possession after the wedding night even during the father's life, unlike the mother and those similar, as previously mentioned. And he indicated that there is no difference between whether she is one who can be had intercourse with or not, and that is the correct view. And by his saying 'it is permissible' he indicates that if the father takes possession, it is also permissible, and if she takes possession herself, it is also permissible if she is rational. And he restricted it by 'what is given' because he cannot take possession of her debts absolutely. And he restricted it to a small male and female because if something is given to a restricted slave, the master cannot take possession; rather the slave takes possession, and when he takes it, the master owns it as the slave's earnings. Similarly for a mukatib, but the master does not own it because he is more entitled to his earnings; as in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of a husband taking possession of gifts given to his minor wife. The key condition is 'after the wedding night' (بعد الزفاف), because before that, he does not have the right of support or a legitimate hand over her. The father delegates authority to the husband by implication. The husband cannot do this for an adult wife nor before the wedding night. The commentator explains the rationale: after the wedding night, the husband maintains her (يعولها) and has a right of hand (يد مستحقة) over her. However, if she is capable of intercourse but the impediment is from his side, there is a difference of opinion: some say he may take possession before the wedding night because he maintains her, but the author of al-Nihaya argues that without the right of hand, the ruling does not apply. The correct view is that it is permissible after the wedding night regardless of whether intercourse is actually possible. The text also notes that the father may still take possession if the husband does not, and the minor wife may take possession herself if she is rational. The ruling is specific to gifts, not debts, and does not apply to slaves or mukatibs because of different ownership rules.

Key Terms
زفاف (zifaf): The wedding night or the actual consummation; here it is the point after which the husband has a right to possess gifts for his wife.
يد مستحقة (yad mustahaqqa): A hand that has a right over the wife's property due to maintenance obligations; a legal basis for possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a husband allowed to take possession of a gift given to his minor wife?
A) Immediately after marriage
B) Only after the wedding night
C) Only if she is an adult
D) At any time before consummation

Key Takeaway
The husband of a minor wife may take possession of gifts given to her only after the wedding night, based on the twin conditions of maintenance and a rightful hand.

Gift from One to Two: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Explaining the validity of a gift from two persons to one, and the invalidity of the reverse according to Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a gift from one person to two donees without specification and explain the qiyas argument that distinguishes it from sale.

Core Idea
Giving one item to two persons is two contracts; invalid without share specification.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a gift from one person to two donees without specifying shares. The matn states the general rule, and the commentary explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning using qiyas, contrasting with sale. The Sahibayn hold a different view, considering it a single valid contract. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze contractual unity in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from Abu Hanifa: a gift from one person to two donees without specifying each one's share is invalid. This is the position stated in the matn.
The matn says 'la' (not) in 'la caksah' meaning the reverse is invalid.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold it is valid because they consider it a single contract with no division.
Their view is introduced with 'wa qala' (they said).
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues by qiyas: if one donee accepts in an indivisible item, the gift is valid for his share and invalid for the other, showing it is two contracts. This is contrasted with sale, where acceptance by one invalidates the whole because sale is one contract.
The proof uses the case of acceptance by one donee as evidence that two contracts are formed.
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn consider it one contract, so no division (shuyu') occurs if both accept together. They restrict the analogy to gifts only, noting that pledge and lease from two persons are unanimously valid.
They focus on the unity of the transaction from the donor's perspective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the gift is invalid unless shares are specified; under the Sahibayn, it is valid without specification. A mufti must know which view is relied upon.
The mufta bihi is not explicitly stated here; later Hanafi works often prefer Abu Hanifa's view for strictness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو وهب اثنان دار الواحد صح لأنهما سلماها جملة وهو قد قبضها جملة فلا شیوع قوله : (لا عكسه وهو أن يهب واحد من اثنين كبيرين ولم يبين نصيب كل واحد عند أبي حنيفة لأنه هبة النصف من كل واحد منهما بدليل أنه لو قبل أحدهما فيما لا يقسم صحت في حصته دون الآخر فعلم أنها عقدان بخلاف البيع فإنه لو قبل أحدهما فإنه لا يصح لأنه عقد واحد. وقالا يجوز نظراً إلى أنه عقد واحد فلا شيوع قيد بالهبة لأن الرهن من رجلين والإجارة من اثنين جائز اتفاقاً. وقيد بكون الواهب واحداً لأن الواهب لو كان اثنين
He said: (If two persons give a house to one, it is valid) because they both handed it over together and he received it together, so there is no division. He said: (Not the reverse), i.e., one person giving to two adults without specifying each one's share: according to Abu Hanifa it is invalid, because it is a gift of half to each of them, as evidenced by the fact that if one of them accepts in something indivisible, the gift is valid for his share and not the other, showing it is two contracts. Unlike sale, for if one of them accepts, it is invalid because it is one contract. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say it is permissible, considering it as one contract with no division. It is restricted to gift because a pledge from two men and a lease from two are unanimously valid. And it is restricted to the donor being one because if the donor were two, it would be valid.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a gift from one person to two donees. The matn states the general rule: two persons giving a house to one donee is valid, but the reverse (one person giving to two donees without specifying shares) is invalid according to Abu Hanifa. The commentary explains Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the gift is essentially a gift of half to each donee, making it two separate contracts. Evidence for this is that if one donee accepts in a case where the item is indivisible, the gift is valid for his share and invalid for the other, which would not happen in a single contract. Sale is contrasted because acceptance by one in an indivisible sale invalidates the entire contract. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the gift is permissible as a single contract with no division (i.e., the donees own it in common). The dispute is specific to gifts; in cases of pledge (rahn) or lease (ijara) from two to one, it is valid by consensus. The ruling also depends on the donor being one; if two donate to one, it is valid.

Key Terms
شيوع (shuyu'): Division or distribution of shares; here it refers to the lack of need to divide because the property is given jointly.
هبة النصف (hibat al-nisf): A gift of half of the property to each donee, which is the implicit construct when one gives to two without specification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for the invalidity of a gift from one person to two donees under Abu Hanifa?
A) The donees must accept separately.
B) It is considered two separate gifts (half to each) forming two contracts.
C) The gift must be in writing.
D) The donor must be present at the time of gift.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a gift from one person to two donees without specifying shares is invalid according to Abu Hanifa because it constitutes two contracts; the Sahibayn validate it as one contract.

Gift of an Undivided Share to Two Persons (Hibah al-Shai'i li-Ithnayn)
Detailed ruling on hibah shai'i with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and hila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for the validity of gifting an undivided share to two persons and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the role of possession.

Core Idea
Gifting an undivided share to two is invalid unless both are adults and shares are specified; possession after specification may cure it according to Muhammad.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq examines the ruling on gifting an undivided share of a divisible property to two persons. The Hanafi school holds that such a gift is generally void due to commonality (shuyu'), but there are exceptions and disagreements. Understanding this ruling requires careful attention to the conditions and the diverse opinions of the early Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
State that gifting an undivided share of a divisible property to two persons without specification is invalid (fasid) by default according to the matn.
This is the base ruling in Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
List the conditions for validity
The text mentions four conditions: donees must be adults, shares must be specified (e.g., half for each), the property must be divisible, and the donees must be exactly two.
If any condition is missing, the gift is invalid.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the gift invalid even if possession occurs after specification. Muhammad holds it valid if possession occurs after specification. For two adults, the two companions permit it, but Abu Hanifa still invalidates.
The dispute hinges on whether possession resolves commonality.
4
Examine the consensus case and the hila
If one donee is a minor, the gift is invalid by consensus because the father's immediate possession for the minor creates commonality. The trick (hila) is to first hand the property to the adult donee and then gift it to both.
This avoids the timing issue of commonality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الهبة
والموهوب له كذلك على أن يكون نصيب أحدهما لأحدهما بعينه ونصيب الآخر للآخر لا يجوز اتفاقاً؛ كذا في النهاية. وقيدنا بكون الموهوب لهما كبيرين لأنه لو وهب داراً من اثنين أحدهما صغير والآخر كبير والصغير في عياله لم تجز الهبة اتفاقاً لأنه حين وهب صار قابضاً حصة الصغير فبقي النصف الآخر شائعاً؛ كذا في المحيط. وقيدنا بعدم البيان لأنه لو بين بأن قال لهذا ثلثها ولهذا ثلثاها أو لهذا نصفها ولهذا نصفها لا يجوز عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف وإن قبضه . وقال محمد يجوز إن قبصه وقيدنا بالدار ومراده منها ما يحتمل القسمة لأن ما لا يحتملها كالبيت يجوز اتفاقاً. وقيد بكون الموهوب له اثنين لأنه لو كان واحداً فوكل اثنين بقبضها فقبضاها جاز ؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان قوله : وصح) تصدق عشرة وهبتها لفقيرين لا (لغنيين أي لا يجوز التصدق بها على غنيين ولا هبتها لهما والفرق أن الصدقة يراد بها
قوله : (وقيدنا بكون الموهوب لهما كبيرين الخ قال الرملي التقييد لا يفيد إلا الإشارة إلى خلافهما فكان الأولى أن لا يذكره ويقول أطلق الاثنين فأفاد أنه لا فرق بين أن يكونا كبيرين أو صغيرين أو أحدهما كبيراً والآخر صغيراً وفي الأوليين خلافهما تأمل قوله : (لأنه لو وهب داراً من اثنين الخ) قال الرملي : ظاهر هذا أنهما لو كانا صغيرين في عياله جاز، وفي البزازية ما يدل عليه ولكن هذا كله على قولهما لا على قوله لما صرح به في الخانية فراجعه إن شئت. وأصل الوهم أن صاحب المنتقى ذكر الحكم في مسألة الاثنين الصغير والكبير غير مضاف إلى أحد فتوهم أنه قول الكل ولو كان كذلك لبطل إطلاق المتون في قوله لا عكسه تأمل .اهـ. أقول نص عبارة الخانية هكذا: ولو وهب داراً لابنين له أحدهما صغير في عياله كانت الهبة فاسدة عند الكل بخلاف ما لو وهب من كبيرين وسلم إليهما جملة فإنه الهبة جائزة عند أبي يوسف ومحمد لأن في الكبيرين لم يوجد الشيوع لا وقت العقد ولا وقت البعض، وأما إذا كان أحدهما صغيراً فكما وهب يصير الأب قابضاً حصة الصغير فيتمكن الشيوع وقت القبض .اهـ. وأنت خبير بأن إظهار الفرق بين المسألتين مبني على قول الصاحبين القائلين بجوازها للكبيرين مع موافقتهما الإمام بعدم جوازها لكبير وصغير بدليل قوله كانت الهبة فاسدة عند الكل فليست مسألة الكبير والصغير مبنية على قولهما فقط، فما فهمه المؤلف من عبارة صاحب المنتقى أنها قول الكل صحيح لا وهم فيه، وعبارة المتون لا تنافيه كما لا يخفى على نبيه. نعم إذا قلنا إذا كان الولدان صغيرين تجوز الهبة يكون مخالفاً لإطلاق المتون عدم جواز هبة واحد من اثنين، ولكن إذا تأمل الفقيه في علة عدم الجواز على قول الإمام وهي : تحقق الشيوع يجزم بتقييد كلام المتون بغير ما إذا كانا صغيرين لأن الأب إذا وهب منهما تحقق القبض منه لهما بمجرد العقد بخلاف ما إذا كان أحدهما كبيراً فإن قبض الكبير يتأخر عن العقد فيتحقق الشيوع عند قبضه الخانية. وعبارة البزازية أوضح في إفادة المراد حيث قال : لأن هبة الصغير منعقدة حال مباشرة الهبة لقيام قبض الأب مقام قبضه وهبة الكبير محتاجة إلى قبول فسبقت هبة الصغير فتمكن الشيوع، والحيلة أن يسلم الدار إلى الكبير ويهبها منهما .اهـ. أي فإذا سلمها إلى الكبير أولاً ثم وهبها

Book of Gift. And the donee likewise, on the condition that the share of one is specifically for one and the share of the other for the other, is not permissible by consensus; as in al-Nihayah. And we restricted it to the two donees being adults because if he gifted a house to two, one minor and the other adult, and the minor is in his care, the gift is not permissible by consensus because when he gifted, he became possessor of the minor's share, so the other half remained undivided; as in al-Muhit. And we restricted it to non-specification because if he specified by saying: for this one a third and for this one two-thirds, or for this one half and for this one half, it is not permissible according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf even if he took possession. And Muhammad said it is permissible if he took possession. And we restricted it to the house, and it is meant to include what admits division, because what does not admit it, like a room, is permissible by consensus. And it was restricted to the donee being two because if he was one and appointed two to take possession, and they took possession, it is permissible; as in Fatawa Qadikhan. His saying: 'And it is valid: charity of ten and its gift to two poor, not to two rich' means that it is not permissible to give charity to two rich nor to gift it to them, and the difference is that charity is intended for... His saying: '(And we restricted it to the two donees being adults, etc.)' Al-Ramli said: The restriction only indicates their disagreement, so it would have been better not to mention it and say 'he mentioned two' to indicate that there is no difference between whether they are both adults, both minors, or one adult and one minor, but in the first two there is disagreement, reflect. His saying: '(Because if he gifted a house to two, etc.)' Al-Ramli said: The apparent meaning is that if both were minors in his care it is permissible, and in al-Bazzaziyyah there is evidence for it, but all this is according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not according to him (Abu Hanifah) as clarified in al-Khaniyyah, so refer to it if you wish. The origin of the misunderstanding is that the author of al-Muntaqa mentioned the ruling in the case of two, minor and adult, without attributing it to anyone, so it was assumed that it is the consensus, but if that were the case, the absolute statement of the texts in his saying 'no, not the opposite' would be invalid. Reflect. End. I say: The text of al-Khaniyyah is as follows: If he gifted a house to his two sons, one of whom is a minor in his care, the gift is invalid by consensus, unlike if he gifted to two adults and handed it over to them together, for the gift is valid according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad because in the two adults no commonality existed at the time of the contract nor at the time of partial possession. But if one is a minor, as soon as he gifts, the father becomes the possessor of the minor's share, so commonality occurs at the time of possession. End. And you know that the distinction between the two cases is based on the statement of the two companions who say it is valid for two adults, while agreeing with the Imam that it is not valid for an adult and a minor, as evidenced by his statement 'the gift is invalid by consensus'. So the case of adult and minor is not based on their opinion alone. Therefore, the author's understanding of al-Muntaqa's statement that it is the consensus is correct, no misunderstanding. And the words of the texts do not contradict it, as is not hidden from the astute. Yes, if we say that when the two children are minors the gift is permissible, that contradicts the absolute ruling of the texts that the gift of one to two is not permissible. But if the jurist reflects on the cause of impermissibility according to the Imam, namely the occurrence of commonality, he will certainly restrict the words of the texts to cases other than when both are minors, because when the father gifts from them, possession from him for them occurs immediately with the contract, unlike when one is an adult, for the adult's possession is delayed from the contract, so commonality occurs at his possession, as in al-Khaniyyah. And the statement of al-Bazzaziyyah is clearer in conveying the meaning where he said: Because the gift of the minor is concluded at the time of the gift directly, due to the father's possession standing in place of his possession, while the gift of the adult requires acceptance, so the gift of the minor preceded, thus commonality occurred. And the trick is to hand over the house to the adult first and then gift it from both. End. Meaning, if he handed it over to the adult first, then gifted it from both, as mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of gifting an undivided share of a divisible property to two persons. The base matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) states that such a gift is invalid unless the shares are specifically assigned. The commentator, al-Ramli, expands by explaining the conditions: the donees must be adults, the shares must be specified, the property must be divisible, and the donees must be two. He details the disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it invalid even if possession occurs, while Muhammad holds it valid if possession occurs. When the donees are both adults, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) permit it, but Abu Hanifa still invalidates due to commonality. The commentary also addresses the case where one donee is a minor in the father's care—then the gift is invalid by consensus because the father's immediate possession for the minor creates commonality. The text provides a trick (hila): to first hand the property to the adult donee and then gift it to both, avoiding commonality. The discussion underscores the Hanafi principle that commonality (shuyu') prevents the validity of a gift of an undivided share unless resolved.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Gift; a contract of voluntary transfer of ownership without compensation.
شائع (shai'i): Undivided share; a common share in a property not yet partitioned.
شيوع (shuyu'): Commonality; the state of being undivided/co-owned.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession; physical or constructive acquisition of the gifted item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is valid.
B) No, it is invalid.
C) It is valid if the donees are adults.
D) It is valid if possession occurs.

Key Takeaway
The gift of an undivided share to two is invalid due to commonality unless shares are specified and donees are adults; possession after specification may cure it according to some Hanafis, and a hila exists for mixed adult-minor cases.

Ruling on Revocation of Gift
Opening of chapter on revocation of gift: ruling and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revocation of a gift and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position by applying the distinction between legal validity (sihhah) and moral permissibility (jawaz).

Core Idea
Revocation of a gift is valid but disliked — legal effectiveness and ethical standing are distinct.

This passage opens the chapter on revocation of gifts. The author immediately establishes that revocation is valid (sahih), but by using the term 'sahih' rather than 'ja'iz', he signals a moral disapproval. This raises a key legal question: can an act be legally effective yet morally wrong? The Hanafi school answers yes, and the proof is a well-known hadith. We will examine how the Hanafis reconcile the apparent prohibition in the hadith with the validity of the act, and how the Shafi'i school reaches a different conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the donor's revocation of a gift after the donee has taken possession. The author states: 'صح الرجوع فيها' – 'valid is revocation in it'. Note he uses 'sahih' not 'ja'iz', which is deliberate.
The choice of 'sahih' indicates validity; the absence of 'ja'iz' hints at dislike.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator brings the hadith: 'لا يحل لرجل أن يعطي عطية أو يهب هبة فيرجع فيها إلا الوالد فيما يعطي ولده' – 'It is not lawful for a man to give a gift and then take it back, except a parent regarding what he gives his child.' This is the central evidence.
The hadith is narrated by the four Sunan and others, and is considered sahih by al-Hafiz.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'لا يحل' (not lawful) indicates prohibition. The Hanafis interpret this as a moral prohibition that does not affect the legal validity of the revocation. The act remains effective in transferring ownership back, but it is ethically blameworthy.
The exception for the parent shows that revocation is generally prohibited morally, but valid legally; otherwise the exception would not make sense.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the same hadith makes revocation haram and therefore invalid (batil), except for the parent-child case. He does not separate moral and legal validity.
The Hanafi response: the hadith's prohibition is moral; validity is a matter of legal effect, which is not negated by prohibition.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position that revocation is valid but disliked is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi). The commentary cites al-Mabsut and al-Nihayah describing it as 'ghayr mustahabb' (not recommended), indicating karahah tanzih. This is the sounder position because it maintains the legal effect while acknowledging the moral censure.
The degree of dislike is debated: some later texts suggest karahah tahrim (prohibitive dislike), but the textual evidence leans toward tanzih.

Source Text

باب الرجوع في الهبة
وجه
باب الرجوع في الهبة
صح الرجوع فيها ومنع الرجوع دمع خزقه فالدال الزيادة المتصلة كالغرس والبناء
الله وهو واحد فلا شيوع والهبة يراد بها وجه الغني وهما اثنان، والصدقة على الغني مجاز عن الهبة كالهبة من الفقير مجاز عن الصدقة لأن بينهما اتصالاً معنوياً وهو إن كل واحد منهما تمليك بغير بدل فيجوز استعارة أحدهما للآخر، فالهبة للفقير لا توجب الرجوع، والصدقة على الغني تجوز الرجوع وصحح في الهداية ما ذكره المصنف من الفرق وهو رواية الجامع الصغير وقد علم بما قدمناه أن المراد من نفي الصحة هنا نفي الملك فلو قسمها وسلمها لهما صحت وملكاها كما لا يخفى والله أعلم.

قوله : فلا شيوع) أشار بنفي الشيوع في هذه الصورة إلى أن الشيوع إذا تحقق في الصدقة يفسدها لأنها كالهبة في ذلك كما سيأتي آخر الباب فإذا تصدق ببعض ما يحتمل القسمة على فقير واحد لم يصح لتحقق الشيوع بخلاف التصدق بكله على فقيرين لما علمته من عدم الشيوع .
باب الرجوع في الهبة
قوله : (فإنه قال إنه غير مستحب قد يقال إن ما كان غير محبوب شرعاً كان مكروهاً فمعنى غير مستحب كونه مكروهاً ومطلق الكراهة للتحريم، ويدل له تعبير الزيلعي بأنه قبيح كما يأتي ولا سيما وقد وجد دليل خاص من السنة على التحريم وهو الحديث الآتي.
(۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الهبة باب .۳۰ مسلم في كتاب الهبات حديث ٧، ٨. أبو داود في كتاب البيوع باب ۸۱. النسائي في كتاب الهبة باب ٢ ٤ أحمد في مسنده (١/ ٢١٧، ٢٥٠، ٢٩١).
Chapter of Revocation in Gift. Face. Chapter of Revocation in Gift. Valid is the revocation in it. And prevention of revocation is except for charity?[?] Thus the attached increase like planting and building. Allah is One so no commonality. And gift is intended for the face of the rich, and they are two. And charity upon the rich is a metaphor for gift, like gift from the poor is a metaphor for charity, because between them is a spiritual connection, which is that each one is a transfer of ownership without exchange, so it is permissible to use one as a metaphor for the other. Thus gift to the poor does not require revocation, and charity upon the rich allows revocation. And in al-Hidayah, what the author mentioned of the difference is corrected, and it is the narration of al-Jami' al-Saghir. And it is known from what we have presented that the meaning of negation of validity here is negation of ownership. So if he divided it and handed it over to them, it is valid and they own it, as is not hidden, and Allah knows best. Chapter of Revocation in Gift. There is no hiddenness in the goodness of its delay. His saying: (Valid is revocation in it) means valid is revocation in the gift after possession if no preventer from the coming preventers exists. And the meaning of gift is the gifted object, because revocation is only regarding properties, not regarding statements. And by mentioning validity without permissibility, he pointed out that revocation is disliked in it. And the apparent meaning of al-Mabsut and followed by al-Nihayah is that it is karahah tanzih (disliked but not prohibited), as he said: It is not recommended. And the implication of the evidence of al-Shafi'i, who says there is no revocation except in what a parent gifts to his child, is that it is karahah tahrim (disliked to the point of prohibition), which is what the authors of the four Sunan narrated as marfu': 'It is not lawful for a man to give a gift or a hibah and then take it back, except a parent regarding what he gives his child. And the likeness of the one who gives a gift and then takes it back is like the dog that returns to its vomit: it eats until it is full, then vomits, then returns to its vomit.' And its authentication by al-Hafiz is transmitted. His saying: 'No commonality' – by negating commonality in this form, he pointed out that if commonality occurs in charity, it invalidates it, because it is like gift in that regard, as will come at the end of the chapter. So if one gives charity of part of something that admits division to a single poor person, it is not valid due to commonality, unlike giving charity of all of it to two poor persons, because of the absence of commonality as you know. Chapter of Revocation in Gift. His saying: (For he said it is not recommended) – It may be said that what is not loved by the Law is disliked, so the meaning of 'not recommended' is that it is disliked, and absolute dislike is for prohibition. And it is supported by al-Zayla'i's expression that it is ugly, as will come, especially since there is specific evidence from the Sunnah for prohibition, which is the hadith. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in the Book of Gift, Chapter 30; Muslim in the Book of Gifts, Hadith 7, 8; Abu Dawud in the Book of Sales, Chapter 81; al-Nasa'i in the Book of Gift, Chapter 24; Ahmad in his Musnad (1/217, 250, 291).
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on revocation of gifts (ruju' fi al-hibah). The author states the ruling: the revocation is valid (sahih), but he uses 'sahih' rather than 'ja'iz' to indicate that while the legal act is effective, it is disliked (makruh). The commentator explains the reasoning: the act of revocation is valid in terms of ownership transfer back to the donor, but it is ethically blameworthy. He cites al-Mabsut and al-Nihayah, which describe it as 'not recommended' (ghayr mustahabb), implying karahah tanzih (slight dislike). However, the hadith reported in the four Sunan strongly condemns the retraction of a gift, comparing it to a dog returning to its vomit, and makes an exception only for a parent retracting a gift to his child. This hadith leads Imam al-Shafi'i to rule that revocation is haram (unlawful) and thus invalid, except for the parent-child case. The Hanafi stance is that the hadith indicates prohibition, but this prohibition does not affect the legal validity of the revocation; the gift is correctly returned, but the act is sinful. The passage also discusses the difference between gift (hibah) and charity (sadaqah): charity given to a rich person is metaphorically a gift, and revocation is allowed; but charity to a poor person does not allow revocation. The commentary also touches on the rule regarding commonality (shuyu') in charity, stating that giving part of a divisible property as charity to one person is invalid due to commonality, but giving all of it to two persons is valid.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation; the act of a donor taking back a gift after possession.
صحة (sihhah): Legal validity; the act meets all requirements to produce legal effects.
كراهة (karahah): Dislike; an act that is not recommended or discouraged, with degrees of tanzih (slight) and tahrim (prohibitive).
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity; a gift given with intent of reward from Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a donor revoking a gift after the donee has taken possession?
A) It is invalid and prohibited.
B) It is valid but disliked.
C) It is valid and recommended.
D) It is only allowed if the donee is a stranger.

Key Takeaway
Revocation of a gift is legally valid (sahih) in Hanafi fiqh but morally disliked (makruh), based on the distinction between validity and permissibility.

The Hanafi Proof and Ruling on Revocation of Gifts
Stating the proof and ruling for gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on revoking a gift (makruh tahrimi) and identify the hadith proof and its interpretation.

Core Idea
Revocation of gifts is allowed but prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) in Hanafi law.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents the Hanafi proof for the permissibility of revoking a gift, while also establishing its disliked nature. The author cites a hadith narrated by al-Hakim al-Zayla'i and explains why the ruling is makruh tahrimi based on the commentator's use of the term 'qabih'. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between legal permissibility and spiritual recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? What is the hukm level (fard, wajib, etc.)?
The act is revoking a gift (al-ruju' fi al-hibah). The ruling is that it is permissible but makruh tahrimi.
2
Locate the dalil
What evidence does the author cite? Quote the hadith and note its source.
Hadith: 'من وهب هبة فهو أحق بها ما لم يثب منها' from al-Hakim al-Zayla'i.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? What is the legal link?
The hadith implies the donor retains a right to the gift until compensation, thus allowing revocation.
4
Determine the hukm level
Why is it makruh tahrimi and not mubah? What linguistic indicator is used?
The commentator describes revocation as 'qabih' (ugly); in Hanafi usul, this indicates a prohibition-like dislike.
5
Interpret the hadith
How is the hadith's negation of permission understood? Does it apply to all cases?
The hadith is interpreted to apply only when revocation is done without judicial decree or the donee's consent, as per al-Muhit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما استدل أئمتنا لصحته به وهو ما رواه الحاكم الزيلعي فإن بهذا يحصل الجمع بين [؟] وبين وصححه مرفوعاً من وهب هبة فهو أحق بها ما لم يثب منها أي لم يعوض. ويدل على أنها كراهة تحريم قول الشارح إن الرجوع قبيح ولا يقال للمكروه تنزيهاً قبيح لأنه من قبيل المباح أو قريب منه . وقد يقال : إن الحديث المفيد لعدم الحل محمول على ما إذا كان بغير قضاء ولا رضا كما أشار إليه في المحيط.
What our imams used as proof for its validity is what al-Hakim al-Zayla'i narrated, for with this is achieved the reconciliation between [?] and he authenticated it as marfu': 'Whoever gives a gift, he is most entitled to it as long as he is not compensated, i.e., not recompensed.' And it indicates that it is prohibitively disliked by the statement of the commentator that revocation is ugly, and it is not said that a tanzihan disliked thing is ugly because it is from the category of permissible or close to it. And it may be said: The hadith that conveys the lack of permissibility is interpreted as when it is without judgment and without consent, as alluded to in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof on the revocation of gifts. The author presents the Hanafi proof: a hadith narrated by al-Hakim al-Zayla'i stating that the donor has the greatest right to the gift as long as he has not been compensated. The hadith establishes the permissibility of revocation. However, the act is not recommended; the commentator (al-Sharih) classifies it as karahah tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) because the Arabic term 'qabih' (ugly) is used for it, which applies only to prohibitions, not to tanzihi disliked actions. The passage also notes that the hadith's negation of permission (lack of permissibility) is understood to refer to cases where the revocation is done arbitrarily without judicial decree or the donee's consent. Thus, the Hanafi position is that revocation is legally allowed but spiritually discouraged, and the hadith is interpreted to support this nuanced view.

Key Terms
الرجوع في الهبة (al-ruju' fi al-hibah): The legal act of the donor taking back the gifted property after it has been given to the donee, subject to certain conditions and preventers.
كراهة تحريم (karahah tahrimi): Prohibitively disliked; an act that is sinful but not as severe as haram. It is a distinct category in Hanafi law between haram and makruh tanzihi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Haram
B) Makruh Tahrimi
C) Mubah
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that revoking a gift is legally permissible but prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi), based on the hadith that the donor is most entitled to the gift until compensated.

Waiving the Right of Revocation is Ineffective
Ruling that the donor's statement waiving the right to revoke does not nullify that right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a donor's verbal waiver of the right to revoke a gift and explain why it is ineffective.

Core Idea
Merely saying 'I waive my right' does not erase it—without consideration, the right to revoke remains.

A donor may think that a simple verbal declaration can nullify his right to revoke a gift. However, Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between rights that can be waived unilaterally and those that persist despite a statement. This lesson examines a clear ruling from Fatawa Qadikhan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Statement
The donor declares: 'I have dropped my right of revocation.' This is a verbal attempt to waive the right.
2
State the Legal Effect
According to Hanafi school, such a statement does not drop the right. The donor retains the right to revoke.
Ruling from Fatawa Qadikhan.
3
Understand the Rationale
The right of revocation is not a purely personal right; it is attached to the gift contract. It cannot be unilaterally waived without consideration or a preventing condition (e.g., marriage). A mere verbal statement is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل كلامه ما إذا قال الواهب أسقطت حقي من الرجوع فإنه لا يسقط حقه وله الرجوع؛ كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان.
And his statement includes the case where the donor says, 'I have dropped my right of revocation,' for he does not drop his right and he has the right to revoke; similarly in Fatawa Qadikhan.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement from Fatawa Qadikhan. It addresses a common scenario: the donor explicitly declares that he renounces his right to revoke the gift. The ruling is emphatic: such a statement has no legal effect. The donor retains his right to revoke despite the declaration. This is because the right of revocation is not a purely personal right that can be waived unilaterally; it is a right attached to the contract that persists until certain preventers arise. The Hanafi school holds that a mere verbal waiver without actual consideration or reciprocal action does not nullify the right.

Key Terms
إسقاط الحق (isqat al-haqq): The act of dropping or waiving a legal right. Here, the donor attempts to drop his right to revoke.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, the right is lost
C) It depends on the intention
D) Only if the donee agrees

Key Takeaway
The right to revoke a gift in Hanafi law cannot be dropped by a mere verbal statement; it requires a substantive act or condition.

Commissioned Gift: The Commissioner's Right of Revocation
Ruling on revocation in commissioned gifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the commissioner's right to revoke a commissioned gift and distinguish between the rights of the commissioner and the commissioned agent.

Core Idea
The commissioner retains the right to revoke a commissioned gift; the agent has no such right.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a commissioned gift (hibah bi al-amr). When a person asks another to give a gift on his behalf, the gift is attributed to the commissioner, who remains the true donor and retains the right to revoke. The commissioned agent is merely an executor, not a donor. We will explore the implications, including the rule that the agent's liability is covered by the commissioner if the agent stipulates a guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
Determine who is the commissioner (amir), who is the commissioned agent (mamur), and who is the recipient.
The commissioner is the one who orders the gift; the agent executes it.
2
Determine attribution of the gift
According to the passage, the gift is attributed to the commissioner, not the agent. The agent is a mere conveyor.
This is the foundational principle.
3
Determine the right of revocation
Only the commissioner has the right to revoke the gift before it is complete. The agent cannot revoke.
The passage explicitly states that the commissioner has the right to revoke.
4
Analyze the guarantee scenario
If the agent adds a condition that he is a guarantor, the gift remains valid and the commissioner becomes a guarantor to the agent for the value.
This nuance is from Fatawa Qadikhan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل ما إذا قال لآخر هب لفلان عني ألف درهم فوهب المأمور كما أمر كانت الهبة من الآمر ولا المأمور على الآمر ولا على القابض، وللآمر أن يرجع في الهبة والدافع يكون متطوعاً. درهم على أني ضامن ففعل جازت الهبة ويضمن الآمر للمأمور ولو قال هب لفلان ألف والأمر أن يرجع في الهبة ولا يرجع الدافع، كذا في فتاوى قاضيخان من باب الكفالة بالمال.
And it includes the case where he says to another, 'Give a gift to so-and-so on my behalf of one thousand dirhams,' and the commissioned gives as ordered: the gift is from the commissioner, not from the commissioned, and the commissioned is neither liable to the commissioner nor to the recipient; and the commissioner has the right to revoke the gift, and the giver is a volunteer. 'Dirham on condition that I am guarantor,' and he does so, the gift is valid and the commissioner guarantees to the commissioned. And if he said, 'Give a gift to so-and-so of one thousand,' and the order is that he (the commissioner) may revoke the gift, and the giver cannot revoke; similarly in Fatawa Qadikhan from the chapter of surety for property.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on commissioned gifts. When A says to B, 'Give a gift to C on my behalf,' and B executes the gift as instructed, the gift is attributed to A (the commissioner), not to B. B acts as a mere agent. The commissioner (A) retains the right to revoke the gift, while B has no right to revoke. If B adds a condition that he is a guarantor, the gift is still valid, and A becomes a guarantor for B's liability. The passage also notes that if A explicitly stipulates that he has the right to revoke, that is upheld, and B cannot revoke. These rules are cited from Fatawa Qadikhan under the topic of surety for property.

Key Terms
الآمر (al-āmir): The person who commissions or orders another to give a gift on his behalf; the true donor.
المأمور (al-ma'mūr): The commissioned agent who physically executes the gift on behalf of the commissioner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only A (the commissioner)
B) Only B (the commissioned)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
In a commissioned gift, the commissioner is the true donor and retains the right of revocation; the agent acts only as an executor without revocation power.

Joint Gift Revocation: Individual Co-Donor's Right
Expansion on joint gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a co-donor can revoke his share of a jointly gifted property independently, and identify the qiyas proof based on partial revocation by a single donor.

Core Idea
A co-donor may revoke his share alone; shuyu' does not block partial annulment.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a ruling about joint gifts. When two people jointly own and gift an item, each retains the right to revoke his own portion, even if the other co-donor is absent or disagrees. The reasoning is that joint ownership (shuyu') does not prevent annulment, as evidenced by the fact that a single donor may revoke part of a gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The passage considers two persons jointly giving a slave as a gift. Recognize that the gifted item is owned in common (shuyu') before the gift.
The scenario is a joint gift, not separate gifts.
2
State the Ruling
Each co-donor has the right to revoke his own share independently, even if the other co-donor is absent or does not consent.
Revocation is a unilateral right; it does not require the co-donor's presence.
3
Understand the Legal Basis
Joint ownership (shuyu') does not prevent the annulment of the gift for one's share. The gift is considered divisible for revocation purposes.
Shuyu' is not a bar to legal acts that affect only one's portion.
4
Recognize the Proof (Qiyas)
The ruling is derived by analogy: just as a single donor may revoke part of a gift (e.g., half), so too may a co-donor revoke his fractional share. The illa (effective cause) is that revocation of a part is permissible when the gift is divisible.
This qiyas is mentioned in al-Muhit as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل كلامه ما إذا وهبا عبداً فلأحدهم الرجوع في نصيبه مع غيبة صاحبه لأن الشيوع لا يمنع فسخها بدليل أن للواهب أن يرجع في بعضها؛ كذا في المحيط.
And his statement includes the case where two persons give a slave as a gift; then one of them has the right to revoke his share even in the absence of his co-donor, because joint ownership (shuyu') does not prevent its annulment, as evidence that a donor may revoke part of the gift; similarly in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage addresses joint gifts. If two co-owners jointly gift a slave to a donee, each co-donor retains the right to revoke his own share independently. This is allowed even if the other co-donor is absent or disagrees. The reasoning is that joint ownership (shuyu') of the gifted item does not hinder the annulment of one's share. The proof is that a single donor may revoke only part of a gift (e.g., half), so similarly, a co-donor may revoke his portion. This ruling is taken from al-Muhit.

Key Terms
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Joint ownership or common ownership among multiple parties in a single asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A co-donor's right to revoke his share is independent and not contingent on the co-donor's consent or presence.
